Taharah — Purification
advanced
8 min
Ruling on Blood from Vomiting: Stomach vs Head
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on blood from vomiting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on blood from the stomach versus the head in vomiting and apply the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) regarding nullification of wudu.

Core Idea
Blood from the head breaks wudu in any amount; blood from the stomach only if it fills the mouth.

This lesson examines a specific fiqh issue: when does vomiting blood nullify wudu? The Hanafi school distinguishes between blood originating from the head (which breaks wudu regardless of quantity) and blood from the stomach (which, according to the relied-upon view, requires a mouthful). This distinction is rooted in the difference in the nature of the blood and its connection to the body. The passage also resolves an apparent contradiction in the statements of Hanafi jurists, particularly al-Zayla'i, and establishes the preferred ruling through istihsan and careful analysis of the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed-upon rule
The text begins by stating an agreed-upon principle: blood rising from the stomach when saliva overcomes it does not break wudu. Note that this is a concession due to the difficulty of avoiding such cases.
The Arabic key phrase: 'إذا غلبه البزاق لا ينقض اتفاقاً'.
2
Distinguish between head and stomach blood
Blood that descends from the head (e.g., nosebleed) breaks wudu whether little or much, once it reaches a place that requires purification. In contrast, blood from the stomach only breaks wudu if it fills the mouth (mal' al-fam), because the stomach is not the originating place for blood; the mouth is connected to the stomach for food, so small amounts are excused.
This is based on the hadith: 'If one of you vomits, let him not break his wudu unless it fills the mouth.'
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one that blood from the stomach breaks wudu in any amount (like head blood), and another (narrated by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad) that it requires filling the mouth. Imam Muhammad agreed with the latter. The chosen view (al-mukhtar) differentiates: if the blood is coagulated (black bile), it requires filling the mouth; if it is liquid (fluid blood), it breaks wudu even in small amounts.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it requires filling the mouth, as liquid blood from the stomach is rare.
4
Understand the ruling for saliva mixed with blood
If saliva contains traces of blood (e.g., veins of blood), it is excused and does not break wudu. However, if blood is predominant or equal in quantity when swallowed, it affects the validity of fasting (analogous to wudu). For wudu, the criterion is whether the blood is from the head or stomach, not just the mixture.
This is mentioned in the context of 'عروق الدم' being excused.
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
Example: If a person vomits a small amount of dark, thick substance (sawda'), wudu is not broken because it is not real blood and did not fill the mouth. If the vomit is fluid blood, even a little breaks wudu. This distinction is based on the nature of the substance.
The istihsan (juristic preference) is that the mouth is like a continuation of the stomach for small amounts, except for substances not originally from the stomach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة أن الصاعد الدم الصاعد من الجوف إذا غلبه البزاق لا ينقض اتفاقاً. وظاهر كلام الزيلعي أن الدم من الجوف المختلط بالبزاق ينقض قليله وكثيره على المختار ولا يخفى عدم صحته لمخالفته المنقول مع عدم تعقل فرق بين الخارج من الفم والخارج من الجوف المختلطين بالبزاق. وقد استفيد مما ذكروا هنا أن ما خرج من المعدة لا ينقض ما لم يملأ الفم وما لم يخرج منها كالدم ينقض قليله وكثيره إذا وصل إلى موضع يلحقه حكم التطهير، وإنما كان كذلك لأن الفم له تعلق بالمعدة من حيث إن وصول الطعام إليها منه فكان منها لاتصاله بها فيجوز أن يلحق بها في حق ما يخرج منها إذا كان قليلاً بخلاف الدم لأن المعدة ليست بموضعه، ولا ضرورة في حكم الدم فيكون له حكم الظاهر من كل وجه، وكذا في معراج الدراية. وفي شرح النقاية : ولو كان في البزاق عروق الدم فهو عفو . وفي السراج الوهاج : وإن استعط فخرج السعوط إلى الفم إن ملأ الفم نقض وإن خرج من الأذنين لا ينقض وفيه تأمل. وحمله بعضهم على أنه وصل إلى الجوف في المسألة الأولى ثم خرج وإلا فهو لم يصل إلى موضع النجاسة لكن في البدائع خلاف في النقض في المسألة الأولى ووجه القول بالنقض بما ذكرنا . وقال السراج الهندي: علامة كونه وصل إلى الجوف أن يتغير والتغير أن يستحيل (اتفاقاً، وكذلك الصاعد من الجوف لا ينقض اتفاقاً إلا أن يملأ الفم كما في شرح المنية. قوله : (وظاهر كلام الزيلعي أن الدم الصاعد من الجوف الخ) اعترض عليه العلامة المقدسي كما نقل عنه بما معناه لم نجد ذلك في كلام الزيلعي بل ذكر الدم مطلقاً عن قيد الاختلاط اهـ. وأقول قال الزيلعي : ولو قاء دماً إن نزل من الرأس نقض قل أو كثر بإجماع أصحابنا، وإن صعد من الجوف فروي عن أبي حنيفة مثله، وروى الحسن عنه أنه يعتبر ملء الفم وهو قول محمد، والمختار إن كان أ يعتبر ملء الفم لأنه ليس بدم وإنما هو سوداء احترقت وإن كان مائعاً نقض وإن قل. ثم قال فيما إذا غلب عليه البصاق : وإن خرج من الجوف فقد ذكرنا تفاصيله واختلاف الروايات فيه اهـ . فذكر حكم ما غلب عليه البزاق ثم قال : هذا إذا خرج من نفس الفم، فإن خرج من الجوف الخ فمراده بقوله فإن خرج يعني الدم لا بقيد كونه غلب عليه البزاق بدليل قوله «فقد ذكرنا تفاصيله الخ» فإن الذي ذكر تفاصيله الدم لا بهذا القيد لأن تفاصيله ما إذا كان جامداً أو مائعاً ملأ الفم أو لا، والذي غلب عليه البزاق لا يتصوّر فيه أن يكون ملء الفم فعلم أن مراده ما ذكرنا وأن مراده بالقليل ما لا يملأ الفم، وبالكثير ما يملؤه على أن الخارج من الجوف لا يخالطه البزاق إلا بعد وصوله إلى الفم لأن البزاق محله الفم لا الجوف، وبهذا يظهر الفرق بين الخارج من الفم والخارج من الجوف فإن الخارج من الفم إنما كان سيلانه بسبب البزاق، وجعل غلبته على البزاق دلیل سیلانه بنفسه بخلاف الخارج من الجوف فإنه لا يصل إلى الفم إلا إذا كان سائلاً بنفسه، فالفرق بينهما واضح، وبه يرجح كلام الزيلعي على كلام بن ملك، ويظهر أن إطلاق كلام الشارحين في محل التقييد فلم يكن كلام مخالفاً للمنقول والله أعلم. قوله : (وما لم يخرج منها كالدم الخ) هذا في غير الخارج من الزيلعي) إلى نتن وفساد فحينئذ يكون نجساً . والبزاق بالزاي والسين والصاد لغات كما في شرح المنية . واعلم أن حكم الصوم كحكم الوضوء هنا حتى إذا ابتلع البصاق وفيه دم إن كان الدم غالباً أو كان سواء أفطر وإلا فلا.
The book of purity: That which rises, blood rising from the stomach if saliva overcomes it does not break wudu, by agreement. And the apparent statement of al-Zayla'i is that blood from the stomach mixed with saliva breaks wudu, its little and much, according to the chosen view, and its incorrectness is not hidden due to contradiction with transmitted reports and lack of comprehensible difference between what exits from the mouth and what exits from the stomach mixed with saliva. And it is benefited from what they mentioned here that what comes out of the stomach does not break wudu unless it fills the mouth, and what does not come out of it, like blood, breaks wudu, its little and much, when it reaches a place subject to the ruling of purification. And it is only thus because the mouth has a connection to the stomach in that the arrival of food to it is from it, so it is considered from it due to its connection, thus it may be attached to it regarding what exits from it if it is little, contrary to blood because the stomach is not its place, and there is no necessity in the ruling of blood, so it has the ruling of the apparent from every aspect. Likewise in Mi'raj al-Dirayah. And in Sharh al-Niqayah: If there are veins of blood in the saliva, it is excused. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If one uses snuff and the snuff exits to the mouth, if it fills the mouth it breaks wudu, and if exits from the ears it does not break wudu, and there is reflection on it. And some carried it that it reached the stomach in the first issue then exited, otherwise it did not reach the place of impurity. But in al-Bada'i' there is a difference regarding breaking in the first issue, and the argument for breaking is what we mentioned. And al-Siraj al-Hindi said: The sign of it having reached the stomach is that it changes, and change is that it becomes putrid and spoiled, then it is impure. And 'buzāq' (saliva) is with zay, sin, and sad as languages, as in Sharh al-Munyah. And know that the ruling of fasting is like the ruling of wudu here, so if one swallows saliva and there is blood in it, if the blood is predominant or equal, he breaks fast; otherwise no.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of vomiting blood on wudu. It begins by stating the agreed rule: blood rising from the stomach when saliva overcomes it does not break wudu. The author then critiques al-Zayla'i's apparent position that blood from the stomach mixed with saliva breaks wudu in any quantity, noting it contradicts transmitted reports and lacks logic. The correct Hanafi position is that what exits from the stomach (like food or water) does not break wudu unless it fills the mouth, while blood from the head (nazal) breaks wudu regardless of quantity once it reaches a place subject to purification. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the mouth is connected to the stomach, so small amounts from the stomach are considered from the stomach and excused, but blood is not originally from the stomach, so it retains the ruling of external impurities. The passage also discusses related issues: veins of blood in saliva are excused; snuff reaching the mouth breaks wudu if it fills the mouth; the sign of reaching the stomach is putrefaction; and the ruling for fasting mirrors wudu—swallowing saliva with blood if blood is predominant or equal breaks the fast. The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is detailed: Abu Hanifa has two narrations—one that blood from stomach breaks wudu in any amount, and another (from Hasan ibn Ziyad) that it requires filling the mouth. The preferred view (mukhtar) is that it requires filling the mouth because it is not real blood but burnt black bile. The author defends al-Zayla'i's statement, explaining that the phrase 'when saliva overcomes' distinguishes between blood that flows naturally (from mouth) and blood that only reaches mouth after being liquid (from stomach). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that blood from stomach breaks wudu only if it fills the mouth, based on istihsan and the difference in cause.

Key Terms
قيء (qay'): Vomiting; the act of expelling contents from the stomach through the mouth.
بزاق (bizāq): Saliva; the fluid in the mouth. It is considered pure and not a nullifier of wudu unless mixed with something that breaks it.
ملء الفم (mal' al-fam): Filling the mouth; the quantity that would fill the mouth if the person were to hold it. Used as a measure for when vomiting breaks wudu.
سوداء (sawdā'): Black bile; a thick, dark substance that can be vomited. In Hanafi fiqh, it is considered not real blood and thus subject to the mal' al-fam rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, when does blood exiting from the stomach (via vomiting) break wudu?
A) In any quantity, regardless of consistency.
B) Only if it fills the mouth (mal' al-fam).
C) Only if it is mixed with saliva.
D) Never, because stomach contents are pure.

Key Takeaway
Blood from the head breaks wudu in any amount; blood from the stomach breaks wudu only if it fills the mouth (for solid) or if it is liquid (any amount).

Unifying Cause for Multiple Vomits in Wudu Nullification
Explains principle and intra-Hanafi dispute on combining scattered vomits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on combining multiple vomits for wudu nullification and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the criterion of unity of cause versus unity of sitting.

Core Idea
Multiple vomits break wudu only if total is a mouthful and cause (nausea) is continuous — sitting alone insufficient.

This passage addresses a subtle fiqh issue: when a person vomits several times, each less than a mouthful, but the combined amount would fill the mouth. Does that nullify wudu? The Hanafi school teaches that the ruling depends on whether the vomits arise from a single cause (same episode of nausea) or from multiple causes. Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Yusuf disagree on the criterion, leading to different practical outcomes. The relied-upon view is Muhammad's, which gives primacy to the cause over the sitting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
Determine when scattered vomits combine to break wudu. The condition is that the total must fill the mouth, but the key dispute is whether they must arise from a single continuous cause (nausea) or simply occur in one sitting.
Both agree that if each vomit is a mouthful individually, wudu is broken immediately; the dispute only applies when each is less.
2
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that the cause (sabab) must be one: the nausea (ghathyan) must be continuous. If he vomits a second time before the first nausea settles, they are from the same cause and combine. If the nausea settles first, they are separate and do not combine even if in one sitting.
This is the standard Hanafi principle: rulings follow causes.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the unity of sitting (majlis) is the criterion. If all vomits occur in one sitting without leaving, they are combined regardless of whether the nausea settled. He analogizes to contracts and prostration recitations, where multiple statements in one sitting are unified.
Abu Yusuf considers the sitting as a unifying factor due to custom and necessity.
4
Analyse the tarjih and practical difference
The author of al-Kafi declares Muhammad's view more correct (asahh) because the general principle is to attribute rulings to causes. The sitting is only considered in exceptional cases out of necessity. The practical difference: if a person vomits, sits, the nausea settles, then vomits again in the same sitting, under Muhammad they are not combined; under Abu Yusuf they are.
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is Muhammad's view.

Source Text

اتحاده أن يقيء ثانياً قبل سكون النفس من الغثيان، وإن قاء ثانياً بعد سكون النفس كان مختلفاً وهذا عند محمد. وقال أبو يوسف : يجمع إن اتحد المجلس اتحاد ما يحتوي عليه المجلس كما ذكره الحدادي لأن للمجلس أثراً في جمع المتفرقات، ولهذا تتحد الأقوال المتفرقة في النكاح والبيع وسائر العقود باتحاد المجلس، وكذلك التلاوات المتعددة لآية السجدة تتحد باتحاد المجلس. ولمحمد رحمه الله أن الحكم يثبت على حسب ثبوت وإنما ترك في بعض الصور للضرورة كما في سجدة التلاوة إذا لو اعتبر السبب لانتفى التداخل لأن كل تلاوة سبب. وفي الأقارير اعتبر المجلس للعرف وفي الايجاب والقبول لدفع الضرر ا هـ . ثم هذه المسألة على أربعة أوجه : إما أن يتحد السبب والمجلس أو يتعدد أو يتحد الأول دون الثاني أو على العكس. ففي الأول يجمع اتفاقاً، وفي الثاني لا يجمع وفي الثالث يجمع عند محمد، وفي الرابع يجمع عند أبي يوسف. وقد نقلوا في كتاب الغصب مسألة اعتبر فيها محمد المجلس، وأبو يوسف اعتبر السبب وهي رجل نزع خاتماً من أصبع نائم ثم أعادها، إن أعادها في ذلك النوم يبرأ من الضمان إجماعاً، وإن استيقظ قبل أن يعيدها ثم نام في موضعه ولم يقم منه فأعادها في النومة الثانية لا يبرأ من الضمان عند أبي يوسف لأنه لما انتبه وجب ردها إليه فما لم يردها إليه حتى نام لم يبرأ بالرد إليه وهو نائم بخلاف الأولى، لأن هناك وجب الرد إلى نائم، وهنا لما استيقظ وجب الرد إلى مستيقظ فلا يبرأ بالرد إلى النائم . وعند محمد يبرأ لأنه ما دام في مجلسه ذلك لا ضمان عليه وإن تكرر نومه ويقظته، فإن قام عن مجلسه ذلك ولم يردها إليه ثم نام في موضع آخر فردها إليه لم يبرأ من His statement: (And the cause gathers its scattered parts) i.e., scattered vomit. Its form: if he vomits repeatedly, each time less than filling the mouth, but if combined it fills the mouth, they are combined and break wudu if the cause is one, which is nausea (ghathyan), which is a source of itself when it surges. If the cause differs, they are not combined. The interpretation of its unity is that he vomits a second time before the nausea calms down from the first vomit; if he vomits a second time after the nausea calms down, it is different. This is according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: They are combined if the sitting (majlis) is one, united as the sitting contains, as al-Hadadi mentioned, because the sitting has an effect in gathering scattered matters. For this reason, scattered statements in marriage, sale, and other contracts are united by unity of sitting, and likewise multiple recitations of a verse of prostration are united by unity of sitting. Muhammad's argument is that the ruling is established according to the establishment of the cause in terms of validity and invalidity, so it is united by its unity. Do you not see that if there are wounds and one dies from a wound before healing, the obligation is united; if healing intervenes, it differs. The author in al-Kafi said: The more correct is Muhammad's statement because the principle is to attribute rulings to causes; it is only left in some cases out of necessity, as in the prostration verse, because if cause were considered, intercession would be negated since each recitation is a cause. And in acknowledgments, the sitting is considered due to custom, and in offer and acceptance to prevent harm. Then this issue has four aspects: either the cause and sitting are united, or both are multiple, or the first is united and the second multiple, or the opposite. In the first, they are combined by agreement; in the second, not combined; in the third, combined according to Muhammad; in the fourth, combined according to Abu Yusuf. And they have transmitted in the book of usurpation a case in which Muhammad considered the sitting, and Abu Yusuf considered the cause: a man removed a ring from the finger of a sleeping person and then returned it. If he returned it in that sleep, he is discharged from liability by agreement. If he woke up before returning it, then slept in his place without leaving it, and returned it in the second sleep, he is not discharged from liability according to Abu Yusuf, because when he awoke, returning it to him became obligatory, and he did not return it until he slept, so he is not discharged by returning it to a sleeping person, unlike the first case, because there the return was to a sleeping person; here when he awoke, the return became to an awake person, so he is not discharged by returning to the sleeping person. According to Muhammad, he is discharged because as long as he is in that sitting, there is no liability on him, even if his sleep and wakefulness are repeated. If he leaves that sitting without returning it, then sleeps in another place and returns it, he is not discharged from liability by agreement, due to difference of sitting and cause. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, attributed to al-Waqi'at, and no statement of Abu Hanifa is mentioned therein. Qadi Khan said in his Fatwas of usurpation: He did not mention in these issues the statement of Abu Hanifa; indeed the correct from his madhhab is that he is not liable except with transfer. And what appears is that the dispute in the usurpation issue is not based on unity of cause or sitting, because sleep is not a cause for his discharge; rather the cause in it is only his returning it to its owner. But Abu Yusuf looked at the fact that he took it while he was asleep, then returning it became obligatory while he was awake, so when he did not return it until he slept a second time, he was not discharged. Muhammad looked at that as long as he is in
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the principle that the same cause (sabab) unites scattered vomits for the purpose of nullifying wudu. The key rule: if a person vomits multiple times, each less than a mouthful, but the total would fill the mouth, they are combined and break wudu only if the cause is one (i.e., the same episode of nausea). If the cause differs (e.g., he vomits again after the nausea has settled), they are not combined. The interpretation of unity of cause is that he vomits a second time before the nausea from the first vomit subsides. This is the view of Muhammad. Abu Yusuf, however, considers unity of sitting (majlis) rather than cause: if all vomits occur in one sitting (without leaving), they are combined even if the nausea settled. The author then explains the reasoning: Muhammad's basis is that rulings are attached to causes, so if the cause is one, the rulings are united. Abu Yusuf relies on the principle that the sitting has an effect in gathering scattered matters, analogizing to contracts (where multiple statements in one sitting form one contract) and multiple recitations of a prostration verse (where one sitting requires one prostration). The author of al-Kafi (Ibn al-Sa'ati) supports Muhammad's view as the more correct (asahh), because the principle is to attribute rulings to causes, and only in certain cases (like prostration recitation, acknowledgments, and offer/acceptance) is the sitting considered for necessity or custom. The passage then outlines four scenarios: (1) cause and sitting both united → combine by agreement; (2) both multiple → do not combine; (3) cause united but sitting multiple → combine according to Muhammad; (4) cause multiple but sitting united → combine according to Abu Yusuf. The text then discusses a related case from the book of usurpation (ghasb): a man removes a ring from a sleeping person's finger and then returns it. The dispute is about discharge from liability, but the author notes it is not actually about cause vs. sitting, because sleep is not a cause for discharge; the cause is the return. The passage cuts off mid-explanation of Muhammad's view.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): Cause; the effective reason that triggers a ruling. Here, it is the nausea (ghathyan) that causes vomiting.
مجلس (majlis): Sitting; the place or session where one is present. In fiqh, unity of majlis can unify multiple statements or acts.
غثيان (ghathyan): Nausea; the sensation that leads to vomiting. It is the cause (sabab) that triggers the vomiting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, under what condition are multiple vomits combined to break wudu?
A) If they occur in the same sitting (majlis).
B) If they occur from the same cause (sabab), i.e., continuous nausea.
C) If they occur within one hour.
D) If the person does not leave the place.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is Muhammad's: combine multiple vomits only if the total fills the mouth and the nausea is continuous; the sitting alone is not sufficient.

Ruling on Swallowing Spittle with Blood
States the ruling on spittle containing blood in fasting and wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swallowing spittle mixed with blood during fasting and wudu, and apply the quantity-based distinction.

Core Idea
Swallowing spittle with blood breaks wudu and fast only if the blood is predominant or equal.

This lesson clarifies a common concern: does a tiny amount of blood in saliva affect one's wudu or fast? The Hanafi school provides a clear rule based on the proportion of blood to spittle. Understanding this distinction helps avoid unnecessary doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and contexts
The act is swallowing spittle that contains blood. The contexts are wudu (impurity) and fasting (nullification).
2
State the analogy between wudu and fasting
The author states that the ruling for fasting is like that for wudu in this matter. So the same quantity test applies.
3
Apply the quantity test
Compare the amount of blood to spittle. If blood is predominant (more) or equal, then swallowing it nullifies wudu and breaks the fast. If blood is less, no nullification occurs.
Predominance is judged by color and taste.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq clarifies the ruling on spittle mixed with blood. The author first notes that 'buzāq' can be pronounced in three ways. He then states that the ruling in fasting is analogous to that in wuḍū': if one swallows spittle containing blood, if the blood is predominant or equal in quantity, then both fasting (if one is fasting) and wuḍū' are invalidated. If the blood is less than the spittle, neither is affected. This is a straightforward ruling without detailed proof here.

Key Terms
البصاق (al-buzāq): Spittle or saliva.
الدم (al-dam): Blood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on swallowing spittle that contains blood during fasting?
A) It always breaks the fast.
B) It breaks the fast only if the blood is predominant or equal.
C) It breaks the fast only if the blood is less.
D) It never breaks the fast.

Key Takeaway
Swallowing spittle with blood nullifies wudu and fast only when blood is predominant or equal in quantity.

Combining Scattered Vomit: Cause vs. Sitting (Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf)
Explains the rule for combining scattered vomit and the intra-Hanafi dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for combining scattered vomit episodes to nullify wudu and distinguish between the positions of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf based on cause vs. sitting.

Core Idea
Vomit episodes combine only if the cause (nausea) is continuous; Abu Yusuf adds the sitting as an alternative unifier.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq tackles a practical problem: how to treat multiple small vomits that individually don't nullify wudu. The Hanafi school agrees that if they collectively fill the mouth, wudu is broken, but they differ on the basis of combination. Imam Muhammad links it to the single urge (ghathayān), while Abu Yusuf links it to the sitting (majlis). Understanding this nuance is key to correctly applying the ruling in similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that scattered vomit episodes (each less than a mouthful) must be combined to determine if they nullify wudu. The dispute is over the criterion for combination.
2
Understand Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that combination depends on the continuity of the cause (ghathayān). If the second vomit occurs before the first urge subsides, they combine; otherwise, they do not.
The ruling follows the cause.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that combination depends on the unity of the sitting (majlis). As long as the person remains in the same sitting, all vomit episodes combine.
Analogizes to contracts and prostrations where sitting unites scattered actions.
4
Examine the four permutations
Consider the cases: (1) both cause and sitting same → combine by agreement; (2) both different → no combination; (3) cause same, sitting different → combine only acc. to Muhammad; (4) cause different, sitting same → combine only acc. to Abu Yusuf.
Use the ghaṣb analogy to reinforce.
5
Apply to a real scenario
Given a specific case (e.g., vomiting, pause, second vomit same place), determine which imam's criterion applies and the outcome. Then note the mufta bihi (Muhammad's view).
The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view per al-Kāfi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (والسبب يجمع متفرقة) أي متفرق القيء وصورته لو قاء مراراً كل مرة دون ملء الفم ولو جمع ملأ الفم يجمع وينقض الوضوء إن اتحد السبب وهو الغثيان وهو مصدر نفسه إذا جاشت، وإن اختلف السبب لا يجمع. وتفسير اتحاده أن يقيء ثانياً قبل سكون النفس من الغثيان، وإن قاء ثانياً بعد سكون النفس كان مختلفاً وهذا عند محمد. وقال أبو يوسف : يجمع إن اتحد المجلس اتحاد ما يحتوي عليه المجلس كما ذكره الحدادي لأن للمجلس أثراً في جمع المتفرقات، ولهذا تتحد الأقوال المتفرقة في النكاح والبيع وسائر العقود باتحاد المجلس، وكذلك التلاوات المتعددة لآية السجدة تتحد باتحاد المجلس. ولمحمد رحمه الله أن الحكم يثبت على حسب ثبوت السبب من الصحة والفساد فيتحد باتحاده ألا : تری أنه إذا جراحات ومات جرح منها قبل البرء يتحد الموجب وإن تخلل البرء اختلف . قال المصنف في الكافي : والأصح قول محمد لأن الأصل إضافة الأحكام إلى الأسباب، وإنما ترك في بعض الصور للضرورة كما في سجدة التلاوة إذا لو اعتبر السبب لانتفى التداخل لأن كل تلاوة سبب. وفي الأقارير اعتبر المجلس للعرف وفي الايجاب والقبول لدفع الضرر ا هـ . ثم هذه المسألة على أربعة أوجه : إما أن يتحد السبب والمجلس أو يتعدد أو يتحد الأول دون الثاني أو على العكس. ففي الأول يجمع اتفاقاً، وفي الثاني لا يجمع وفي الثالث يجمع عند محمد، وفي الرابع يجمع عند أبي يوسف. وقد نقلوا في كتاب الغصب مسألة اعتبر فيها محمد المجلس، وأبو يوسف اعتبر السبب وهي رجل نزع خاتماً من أصبع نائم ثم أعادها، إن أعادها في ذلك النوم يبرأ من الضمان إجماعاً، وإن استيقظ قبل أن يعيدها ثم نام في موضعه ولم يقم منه فأعادها في النومة الثانية لا يبرأ من الضمان عند أبي يوسف لأنه لما انتبه وجب ردها إليه فما لم يردها إليه حتى نام لم يبرأ بالرد إليه وهو نائم بخلاف الأولى، لأن هناك وجب الرد إلى نائم، وهنا لما استيقظ وجب الرد إلى مستيقظ فلا يبرأ بالرد إلى النائم . وعند محمد يبرأ لأنه ما دام في مجلسه ذلك لا ضمان عليه وإن تكرر نومه ويقظته، فإن قام عن مجلسه ذلك ولم يردها إليه ثم نام في موضع آخر فردها إليه لم يبرأ من الضمان إجماعاً لاختلاف المجلس والسبب، كذا في السراج الوهاج معزياً إلى الواقعات ولم يذكر لأبي حنيفة فيها قولاً. وقال قاضيخان في فتاواه من الغصب ولم يذكر في هذه المسائل قول أبي حنيفة فإن الصحيح من مذهبه أنه لا يضمن إلا بالتحويل ا هـ. والذي يظهر أن الخلاف في مسألة الغصب ليس بناء على اتحاد السبب أو المجلس فإن النوم ليس سبباً في براءته، بل السبب فيها إنما هو رده إلى صاحبه لكن أبو يوسف نظر إلى أنه أخذه العين كذا في شرح المهذب .
His statement: (And the cause gathers the scattered) i.e., scattered vomit. Its example: if he vomits multiple times each time less than a mouthful, and if collected they would fill the mouth, they are gathered and nullify wudu if the cause is one, which is nausea (ghathayān), which is the source of the soul when it surges. If the cause differs, they are not gathered. Explanation of its unity: that he vomits a second time before the tranquility of the soul from the nausea, if he vomits a second time after the tranquility of the soul, it is different, and this is according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: they are gathered if the sitting (majlis) is one, according to what the sitting contains, as al-Haddādī mentioned, because the sitting has an effect in gathering scattered things, and for this reason scattered statements in marriage, sale, and all contracts are united by the unity of the sitting, and likewise multiple recitations of the verse of prostration are united by the unity of the sitting. And for Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, the ruling is established according to the establishment of the cause in terms of validity and invalidity, so it is united by its unity. Do you not see that if there are wounds and death from one of them before healing, the obligation is united, and if healing intervenes, it differs. The author said in al-Kāfī: the most correct is the saying of Muhammad because the origin is attaching rulings to causes, and it is only left in some situations for necessity, as in the prostration of recitation, for if the cause were considered, the overlapping would be negated because each recitation is a cause. And in acknowledgments (iqrār), the sitting is considered due to custom, and in offer and acceptance to repel harm. Then this issue has four aspects: either the cause and the sitting are united or multiple, or the first is united without the second, or vice versa. In the first, they are gathered by agreement; in the second, they are not gathered; in the third, they are gathered according to Muhammad; in the fourth, they are gathered according to Abu Yusuf. And they transmitted in the book of usurpation (ghaṣb) an issue in which Muhammad considered the sitting and Abu Yusuf considered the cause: a man removed a ring from the finger of a sleeping person and then returned it. If he returned it during that sleep, he is absolved from liability by consensus. If he woke up before returning it, then slept in his place without leaving it, and returned it during the second sleep, he is not absolved from liability according to Abu Yusuf, because when he woke up, returning it to him became obligatory, and since he did not return it until he slept, he is not absolved by returning it to him while sleeping, unlike the first case because there the return was obligatory to a sleeping person, and here when he woke up, the return was obligatory to an awake person, so he is not absolved by returning to the sleeping person. According to Muhammad, he is absolved because as long as he is in that sitting, there is no liability on him, even if his sleep and wakefulness repeat. If he left that sitting without returning it, then slept in another place and returned it to him, he is not absolved from liability by consensus due to the difference of sitting and cause. Thus in al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj, attributed to al-Wāqi'āt, and he did not mention for Abu Hanifa a saying in it. Qāḍīkhān said in his Fatāwā on usurpation: he did not mention in these issues the saying of Abu Hanifa, and the correct from his madhhab is that he is not liable except by transfer (taḥwīl). And it appears that the disagreement in the issue of usurpation is not based on the unity of cause or sitting, for sleep is not a cause for his absolution; rather, the cause therein is only his return to its owner. But Abu Yusuf considered that he took it while he was sleeping, then returning it to him became obligatory while he was awake, so when he did not return it until he slept again, he is not absolved. Muhammad considered that as long as he is in his sitting, he is not liable. And the word 'ma'ida' (stomach) has been repeated, so there is no harm in recording it: it is with fatḥa of mīm and kasra of 'ayn, and with kasra of mīm and sukūn of 'ayn, thus in Sharḥ al-Muhadhdhab.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq explains the rule for combining scattered vomit to determine whether it nullifies wuḍū'. The matn states 'al-sabab yujammi'u mutafarriqata' (the cause unites the scattered). The commentator clarifies that this applies to vomit: if a person vomits multiple times, each less than a mouthful, and if they were combined they would fill the mouth, then they are combined (and nullify wuḍū') only if the cause (the urge to vomit, ghathayān) is the same. If the cause differs, they are not combined. Muhammad holds that combination depends on the single cause: if the second vomit occurs before the first urge subsides, they are combined; after subsiding, they are not. Abu Yusuf holds that combination depends on the same sitting (majlis): as long as one remains in the same sitting, all vomit episodes combine. The commentator then presents the four permutations and which imam agrees. He then cites a parallel case from the book of Ghaṣb (usurpation) where Muhammad and Abu Yusuf differ on the same principle. The note on the word 'ma'ida' gives its pronunciation.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): The cause; here it refers to the urge to vomit (ghathayān).
الغثيان (al-ghathayān): Nausea, the urge to vomit.
المجلس (al-majlis): The sitting session; the physical location where one remains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, when do two separate vomit episodes combine to constitute a mouthful?
A) If they occur in the same sitting.
B) If they occur before the nausea subsides between them.
C) If they occur in the same day.
D) They never combine.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on combining scattered vomit depends on whether the cause (nausea) is continuous (Muhammad) or the sitting is the same (Abu Yusuf); the preferred view is Muhammad's.

Sleep as a Nullifier: Posture and the Dispute of the Two Companions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sleep and posture as nullifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on which sleeping postures nullify wudu and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the sitting posture.

Core Idea
Complete relaxation determines nullification; sleep itself is not the nullifier.

Sleep is a natural state that removes one from full alertness, but the Hanafi school does not consider it a direct nullifier of wudu. Instead, the accompanying event (like passing wind) is the nullifier. However, since sleep often leads to such events, the ruling is tied to the posture that indicates complete relaxation. This lesson unpacks the two scholarly views on sleep and the specific disagreement between Imams Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding a sitting posture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two approaches to sleep
Recognize that some scholars view sleep itself as a nullifier, while others view what inevitably accompanies sleep as the nullifier. The latter is preferred.
The text cites al-Mabsūṭ and al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj for the preferred view.
2
Understand complete relaxation (istirkhā') as the criterion
Sleep nullifies wudu only when it involves complete relaxation, which typically occurs in lying-down postures. This is the operative cause (ʿilla) for nullification.
The ruling is tied to postures that guarantee loss of control.
3
Apply the criterion to different postures
Sleeping on the side, back, or face involves full relaxation and nullifies wudu. Sleeping while sitting upright without leaning does not.
The text lists these postures from al-Nihāyah and other sources.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the sitting posture
For the posture of sitting with buttocks on heels, Muhammad holds that wudu is required, while Abu Yusuf holds it is not required. The difference hinges on whether this posture involves sufficient relaxation to conceal a possible event.
The text from al-Muḥīṭ declares Abu Yusuf's view as more correct (aṣaḥḥ).
5
Determine the muftā bihī and practical consequence
The preferred (muftā bihī) view is that no wudu is needed for the sitting-on-heels posture. This has practical implications for those who pray while sitting, and for individuals with conditions like continuous flatulence.
The text of Ibn al-Shilbī illustrates the application for those with continuous flatulence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (ونوم مضطجع ومتورك) بيان للنواقض الحكمية بعد الحقيقية. والنوم فترة طبيعية تحدث في الإنسان بلا اختيار منه وتمنع الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة عن العمل مع سلامتها واستعمال العقل مع قيامه فيعجز العبد عن في وضوئه فإنه ينتقض وضوءه الجريان العادة عند الدخول في الخلاء بالتبرز . الثانية أن عينه ناقض وصحح في السراج الوهاج الأول فاختاره الزيلعي مقتصراً عليه لأنه لو كان ناقضاً لاستوى وجوده في الصلاة وخارجها بالأرض واضطجع مثله في الصحاح ويلحق به المستلقي على قفاه والنائم المستلقي على وجهه، وأما من نام واضعاً أليتيه على عقبيه وصار شبه المنكب على وجهه واضعاً بطنه على فخذيه لا ينتقض وضوءه، كذا في النهاية والمعراج وعزاه في فتح القدير إلى الذخيرة ثم قال : وفي غيرها نام متربعاً ورأسه على فخذذيه نقض وهذا يخالف ما في الذخيرة ا هـ. وفي المحيط : لو نام قاعداً واضعاً أليتيه على عقبيه شبه المنكب . قال محمد: عليه الوضوء. وقال أبو يوسف : لا وضوء عليه وهو الأصح ا هـ . فأفاد أن في المسألة اختلافاً بين الصاحبين وأن ما في النهاية وغيرها الأصح أطلق في المضطجع فشمل المريض إذا نام في صلاته مضطجعاً وفيه خلاف والصحيح النقض. وقيل: لا لأن نومه قاعداً كنوم الصحيح قائماً، وأما التورك فلفظ مشترك، فإن كان بمعنى أن جلسته تكشف عن المخرج كما إذا نام على أحد وركيه أو معتمداً على أحد مرفقيه فهذا ناقض وهو مراد المصنف بدليل ما علل به في الكافي، وإن كان ناقضاً الجوف المختلط بالبزاق إذ حكمه حكم الخارج من الفم كما قدمه قوله: (وصحح في السراج الوهاج الأول) وقد سأل العلامة ابن الشلبي عن شخص به انفلات ریح هل ينتقض وضوءه بالنوم؟ فأجاب بعدم النقض بناء على هذا قال ومن ذهب إلى أن النوم نفسه ناقض لزم نقض وضوء من به انفلات الريح بالنوم .اهـ. أقول: وهذا أحسن من قول النهر ينبغي أن يكون عينه أي النوم اتفاقاً فيمن فيه انفلات ،به ويلوح ذلك من تقييد صاحب النهاية والمحيط المسألة بقوله واضعاً أليتيه على عقبيه وإطلاق مسألة التربع فتأمل قوله : (وقيل لا لأن نومه قاعداً وجود
His statement: (And sleep of one lying on side and sitting on one hip) is an explanation of the legal nullifiers after the real ones. Sleep is a natural state that occurs in a person involuntarily and prevents the external and internal senses from functioning while they are sound, and the use of intellect while it is present, so the servant becomes incapable of fulfilling rights. There are two ways among scholars regarding sleep mentioned in al-Mabsūṭ and followed by the commentators of al-Hidāyah: one of them is that sleep is not a nullifier; rather, the nullifier is what a sleeping person cannot avoid, so the apparent cause is placed in its stead as in travel, and as when one enters the toilet and doubts his wuḍū', his wuḍū' is nullified due to the customary habit of relieving oneself when entering the lavatory. The second is that its essence is a nullifier, and al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj corrected the first, and al-Zayla'ī chose it, restricting himself to it, because if it were a nullifier, its existence in prayer and outside it would be equal. So what is in al-Tawshīḥ that its essence is not a nullifier by consensus is debatable. And since sleep is a locus of ḥadath, the ruling is based on what is accompanied by complete relaxation (istirkhā'), and that is in the one lying on his side. Iḍṭijā' is placing the side on the ground; it is said 'ḍaja'a al-rajul' when he places his side on the ground, and 'iḍṭaja'a' is similar, as in al-Ṣiḥāḥ. And attached to it is one lying on his back and the one sleeping on his face. As for one who sleeps placing his buttocks on his heels and becomes like a leaning one on his face, placing his stomach on his thighs, his wuḍū' is not nullified. Thus in al-Nihāyah and al-Mi'rāj, and attributed in Fatḥ al-Qadīr to al-Dhakhīrah. Then he said: and in other than it, one who sleeps sitting cross-legged with his head on his thighs nullifies, and this contradicts what is in al-Dhakhīrah. In al-Muḥīṭ: if he sleeps sitting, placing his buttocks on his heels like a leaning one, Muhammad said: wuḍū' is required. Abu Yusuf said: there is no wuḍū', and it is more correct. So it indicates that there is a difference of opinion between the two Companions, and that what is in al-Nihāyah and others is more correct. It is absolute in the one lying on his side, including the sick person who sleeps in his prayer lying on his side, and there is a difference, and the correct is nullification. And it is said: no, because his sleeping while sitting is like the sleeping of a healthy person standing. As for tawarruk, it is a homonym. If it means that his sitting reveals the private part, as when he sleeps on one of his hips or leaning on one of his elbows, then this is a nullifier, and this is what the author intended, as evidenced by what he reasoned in al-Kāfī. And if it is a nullifier, the matter is mixed with spittle, for its ruling is like that which exits from the mouth, as he presented. His statement: (And he corrected in al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj the first position). And the scholar Ibn al-Shilbī asked about a person who has continuous flatulence: does sleep nullify his wuḍū'? He answered that it does not, based on this. He said: and anyone who holds that sleep itself is a nullifier would necessarily hold that the wuḍū' of one with continuous flatulence is nullified by sleep. I say: This is better than the statement of al-Nahr that it should be its essence, i.e., sleep, by agreement in one with continuous flatulence, since what a sleeping person cannot avoid—if its existence were confirmed—would not nullify, so the hypothetical is more appropriate. His statement: (So it indicates that there is a difference between the two Companions. Al-Ramlī said: it should be that nullification depends on retention or not, and thus reconcile the two sayings. And this is evident from the restriction of the author of al-Nihāyah and al-Muḥīṭ of the issue by his saying 'placing his buttocks on his heels' and the absolute nature of the cross-legged issue, so ponder. His statement: (And it is said: no, because his sleeping while sitting...) [Text cut off].
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage discusses sleep as a nullifier of wuḍū'. The commentator first defines sleep and presents two scholarly approaches: (1) sleep itself is not a nullifier; rather, what inevitably accompanies sleep is the nullifier, and sleep is a sign. (2) sleep itself is a nullifier. The first view is preferred. The ruling is attached to complete relaxation (istirkhā'), which occurs in lying down. Various postures are discussed. The key point: if a person sleeps while sitting with buttocks on heels (like a leaning posture), there is a difference between Muhammad (who says wuḍū' is required) and Abu Yusuf (who says no wuḍū', and this is considered the more correct view according to the text). The commentator also mentions the case of a person with continuous flatulence and the opinion that sleep does not nullify his wuḍū' because the condition of 'what sleep does not lack' is already present.

Key Terms
النوم (al-nawm): Sleep, a natural state that prevents senses from functioning.
المضطجع (al-muḍṭaji'): One who sleeps lying on his side.
التورك (al-tawarruk): Sitting on one hip, possibly revealing the private part.
الاسترخاء (al-istirkhā'): Complete relaxation of the body, the criterion for nullification of wuḍū' during sleep.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, his wuḍū' is valid.
B) No, his wuḍū' is nullified.

Sleep Postures and Nullification of Wudu: Hanafi Rulings and Proof
Commentary on the ruling that sleep nullifies wudu in certain postures, with evidence and intra-Hanafi disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which sleeping postures nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school and explain the evidentiary basis, including the hadith and the preferred methodological approach.

Core Idea
Wudu is nullified by sleep only when complete relaxation occurs, because sleep itself is not a direct nullifier.

The Hanafi school has a nuanced ruling on sleep and wudu. Not all sleep breaks wudu; it depends on the posture and whether sphincter control is lost. This lesson unpacks the commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, clarifying the evidence, the two scholarly methods, and the specific postures that trigger nullification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine which sleeping postures are being ruled upon and whether each nullifies wudu. Note that the ruling is not on sleep itself but on the presumed hadath.
The nullifier is the presumed release of wind, not sleep.
2
Locate and understand the dalil
The hadith 'The eyes are the string of the anus' is cited. Analyze how it serves as evidence that sleep relaxes the sphincter.
The hadith is ahad and zanni in transmission and meaning.
3
Trace the two methodological approaches
Scholars differ: some say sleep is a direct nullifier; others say sleep is a presumptive cause for the real nullifier (hadath). Note which is preferred and why.
The preferred view is that sleep is not a direct nullifier.
4
Apply to specific postures
Work through the postures: mudtaji' (lying on side), mutawarik (leaning on hip), sitting with buttocks on heels, cross-legged, prostration, etc. Determine nullification based on retention or release.
Key factor: whether the seat (maq'adah) is firmly on the ground.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on the shibh al-munakib posture. Understand each position and which is mufta bihi.
Abu Yusuf's view (no nullification) is the more correct (asahh).

العينان انطلق الوكاء» (رَوَاهُ أَبُو دَاوُدَ فِي كِتَابِ الطَّهَارَةِ بَاب 79 ابْنَ مَاجَهْ فِي كِتَابِ الطَّهَارَةِ بَاب 62 الدَّارِمِيّ فِي كِتَابِ الْوُضُوءِ بَاب 48 أَحْمَدَ فِي مُسْنَدِهِ (97/4) بِلَفْظِ «الشه» بَدَلَ «الستة»).
His statement: 'And sleep of one lying on his side and leaning on his hip' is an explanation of the legal nullifiers after the real ones. Sleep is a natural state that occurs in a person involuntarily and prevents the external and internal senses from functioning while they are sound and the use of reason while it exists, so the servant becomes unable to fulfill rights. Scholars have two methods regarding sleep, mentioned in al-Mabsut and followed by the commentators of al-Hidayah: One is that sleep is not a nullifier; rather, the nullifier is what the sleeper cannot avoid, so the apparent cause is substituted, as in travel, and as when one enters the lavatory and doubts his ablution—his ablution is nullified due to the habit of relieving oneself when entering the toilet. The second is that sleep itself is a nullifier, and the first was preferred in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj; al-Zayla'i chose it, confining himself to it, because if it were a nullifier, its existence in prayer and outside would be equal. So what is in al-Tawshih that sleep itself is not a nullifier by consensus is questionable. Since sleep is a presumptive cause for hadath, the ruling is attached to what realizes complete relaxation, which is in the one lying on his side. Lying on one's side is placing the side on the ground. It is said: 'the man lay down' if he places his side on the ground, and similarly 'idtaja'a' in al-Sihah. The one lying on his back and the one lying on his face are appended. As for one who sleeps placing his buttocks on his heels, becoming like one prostrating, placing his belly on his thighs, his ablution is not nullified. Thus in al-Nihayah and al-Mi'raj, and it is attributed in Fath al-Qadir to al-Dhakhirah, then he said: 'In other than it, if one sleeps cross-legged with his head on his thighs, it nullifies.' This contradicts what is in al-Dhakhirah. In al-Muhit: 'If one sleeps sitting, placing his buttocks on his heels, like one prostrating, Muhammad said: He must perform ablution. Abu Yusuf said: No ablution is required, and this is the more correct.' So it indicates that there is a difference between the two Companions, and that what is in al-Nihayah and others is the more correct. He applied it absolutely in the case of one lying on his side, so it includes the sick person if he sleeps in his prayer lying on his side, and there is a difference of opinion; the correct view is nullification. It was said: No, because his sleeping sitting is like the healthy person's sleeping standing. As for tawakkur (leaning on one hip), it is a homonym. If it means that his sitting exposes the exit, as when he sleeps on one of his hips or leaning on one of his elbows, then it is a nullifier, and this is what the author intended, as evidenced by what he reasoned in al-Kafi; if it is a nullifier, the stomach contents mixed with saliva, since its ruling is like that which exits from the mouth, as he previously stated. His statement: 'And it was corrected in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj the first.' The scholar Ibn al-Shalabi was asked about a person who has continuous flatulence: does his sleep nullify his ablution? He answered that it does not nullify, based on this. He said: 'Whoever holds that sleep itself is a nullifier must deem the ablution of one with continuous flatulence nullified by sleep.' I say: This is better than the statement of al-Nahr: 'It should be that sleep itself is a nullifier by consensus in the case of one with continuous flatulence, because what the sleeper cannot avoid, if its existence were verified, would not nullify, so the imagined case is more appropriate.' His statement: 'So it indicates that there is a difference between the two Companions.' al-Ramli said: 'I say: Nullification should be based on retention or not, and this reconciles the two statements; this is indicated by the author of al-Nihayah and al-Muhit restricting the case with 'placing his buttocks on his heels' and the absolute case of sitting cross-legged, so ponder.' His statement: 'It was said: No, because his sleeping sitting nullifies.' al-Khulasa: He spreads his feet to one side and sticks his buttocks to the ground, this is not a nullifier, as in al-Khulasa. The author did not mention leaning on something such that if it were removed he would fall, because it does not nullify in the apparent madhhab of Abu Hanifah, if his seat is not removed from the ground, as in ... and the general mashayikh adopted this, and it is the most correct as in al-Bada'i', even though al-Quduri chose nullification. If his seat is removed, then it is by consensus a nullifier, and that is the meaning of tawakkur, so he left it. In al-Khulasa: If he sleeps on the edge of a furnace while sitting with his legs dangling, it is hadath. In al-Muntaha: If he sleeps squatting with his head on his knees, it does not nullify. In al-Muhit: If he sleeps on a barebacked animal, they said: If in the state of ascent and level, it is not hadath; if in the state of descent, it is hadath because his seat is raised from the animal's back. In these cases where it is hadath, it is in the sense of tawakkur, so it does not depart from the author's speech. The author restricted it to sleep of one lying on his side and leaning on his hip because sleep of the standing and sitting does not nullify, even in a litter or howdah as in al-Khulasa; nor does the sleep of the bowing or prostrating absolutely if in prayer, and if outside prayer it is the same, except in prostration where it is required that it be in the sunnah posture, i.e., raising his belly from his thighs and keeping his arms away from his sides. If he prostrates in other than this posture, his ablution is nullified because in the first case retention remains and release is absent, contrary to the second case. This is the analogical reasoning in prayer except that we abandoned it therein. ... (like sleep of a healthy person) correct is 'because his sleeping is lying on his side' because the discussion is about it. His statement: 'and not the prostrator absolutely' means whether in the sunnah posture or not, as interpreted by what follows. His statement: 'because in the first case' refers to prostration in the sunnah posture. By 'release' is meant what is narrated in the hadith: 'The eyes are the string of the anus; when the eyes sleep, the string is released.' Reported by Abu Dawud in the Book of Purification, chapter 79; Ibn Majah in the Book of Purification, chapter 62; al-Darimi in the Book of Wudu, chapter 48; Ahmad in his Musnad (4/97) with the wording 'ashshuh' instead of 'al-sat'.'
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the ruling of sleep as a nullifier of wudu. The author begins by explaining that sleep is a natural state that suspends the senses, and scholars have two main approaches: (1) sleep itself is not a nullifier; rather, the nullifier is what inevitably accompanies sleep (like passing wind), and sleep is merely a presumptive cause (zann al-hadath). This is the preferred view (mufta bihi). (2) Sleep itself is a nullifier, but this is weaker. The ruling then details specific postures: sleeping on one's side (mudtaji') or leaning on one hip (mutawarik) nullifies wudu because complete relaxation (istirkha') occurs, leading to loss of sphincter control. Sitting positions where the buttocks are firmly on the ground (like squatting on heels) do not nullify because retention (istimsak) remains. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the sitting position like one prostrating (shibh al-munakib): Muhammad says it nullifies; Abu Yusuf says it does not, and the latter is more correct (asahh). The passage also addresses special cases: sleeping on a barebacked animal (if the seat is raised, it nullifies), sleeping in prostration (if in the sunnah posture, no nullification; otherwise, nullification due to loss of retention). The hadith 'The eyes are the string of the anus' is cited as evidence that when the eyes sleep, the 'string' is released, implying release of wind. The commentary reconciles differences by linking nullification to whether the seat (maq'adah) is firmly placed or not—if it is not firmly on the ground, nullification occurs; if it is, wudu remains intact. The author emphasizes that the relied-upon position is that sleep itself is not a direct nullifier; rather, it is the presumed occurrence of hadath (like passing wind) that nullifies wudu.

Key Terms
مضطجع (mudtaji'): One who sleeps lying on his side, with his side on the ground.
متورك (mutawarik): One who sleeps leaning on one hip or elbow, such that the private part is exposed or the seat is not firmly on the ground.
ناقض (naqid): That which invalidates wudu (hadath).
وكاء (wika'): A string or tie used to secure a waterskin; metaphorically, the sphincter muscle that controls the release of wind.
استطلاق (istitlaq): Release of the sphincter, leading to passage of wind; the opposite of retention (istimsak).
استرخاء (istirkha'): Complete relaxation, especially of the body, leading to loss of sphincter control.
استمساك (istimsak): Retention; the ability to hold the sphincter closed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following sleeping postures does NOT nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school?
A) Sleeping lying on one's side (mudtaji')
B) Sleeping leaning on one hip (mutawarik)
C) Sleeping with buttocks on heels and belly on thighs (shibh al-munakib)
D) Sleeping lying on one's back (mustalqi)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that sleep itself does not directly nullify wudu; rather, nullification depends on the sleeping posture's effect on sphincter control, with the key criterion being whether the seat is firmly on the ground.

Sleep While Sitting: When Does It Nullify Wudu?
Ruling statement with expansion on nullification conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion of posterior contact to determine whether sleep while sitting nullifies wudu in various postures.

Core Idea
Sleep nullifies wudu only when the posterior is elevated, removing muscle control.

Many assume that any sleep while sitting invalidates wudu, but the Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling based on posture. This lesson unpacks the key distinction between sitting with the posterior on the ground (no nullification) and tawarruk (elevated posterior, nullification). We will explore the evidence, the intra-madhhab difference with al-Quduri, and apply the principle to real-life scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the key condition
The act is sleep while sitting. The key condition for nullification is whether the posterior (buttocks) is touching the ground or not.
2
Understand the basic ruling
When the posterior is on the ground, sleep does not nullify wudu. This is the majority view, adopted by most shaykhs and deemed the most correct in al-Bada'i'.
Intra-Hanafi difference: al-Quduri held that any sleep while sitting nullifies.
3
Recognize the exception: tawarruk
Tawarruk (elevated posterior, e.g., sitting on one's hip) nullifies by consensus, as there is no muscle control.
This is the unanimous view.
4
Apply the principle to variant scenarios
The passage extends to leaning, tandoor, wrapping, and riding an animal – all resolved by the same condition: posterior contact with a stable surface.
Sleeping on a bareback animal during descent nullifies because the posterior is distended.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Al-Quduri held that all sleep while sitting nullifies, but the mufta bihi is the majority view from Abu Hanifa.
The tarjih is based on al-Bada'i' and the practice of the majority of shaykhs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينقض الخلاصة أن يبسط قدميه من جانب ويلصق أليتيه بالأرض فهذا غير ناقض كما في الخلاصة، بمعنى ولم يذكر المصنف الاستناد إلى شيء لو أزيل عنه لسقط لأنه لا ينقض في ظاهر المذهب عن أبي حنيفة. إذا لم تكن مقعدته زائلة عن الأرض كما في وبه أخذ عامة المشايخ وهو الأصح كما في البدائع، وإن كان مختار القدوري النقض. وأما إذا كانت مقعدته زائلة فإنه اتفاقاً وهو بمعنى التورك فلذا تركه وفي الخلاصة ولو نام على رأس التنور وهو جالس قد أدلى رجليه كان حدثاً. وفي المبتغى ولو نام محتبياً ورأسه على ركبتيه لا ينقض. وفي المحيط : لو نام على دابة وهي عريانة قالوا : إن كان في حالة الصعود والاستواء لا يكون حدثاً، وإن كان في حالة الهبوط يكون حدثاً لأن مقعدته متجافية عن ظهر الدابة ا هـ. وفي هذه المواضع التي يكون فيها حدثاً فهو بمعنى التورك فلم يخرج عن كلام المصنف.
The conclusion: that he spreads his feet from one side and sticks his buttocks to the ground, this is not a nullifier, as in al-Khulasa. In meaning, and the author did not mention leaning on something which if removed he would fall, because it does not nullify in the apparent madhhab from Abu Hanifa. If his seat is not leaving the ground, as in... And the majority of shaykhs adopted it and it is the most correct as in al-Bada'i', although al-Quduri's choice is nullification. But if his seat is leaving, then it is agreed, and it is in the meaning of tawarruk, so he left it. And in al-Khulasa: if he sleeps on top of a tandoor while sitting, having dangled his legs, it is a hadath. And in al-Mubtagha: if he sleeps in a wrapping position with his head on his knees, it does not nullify. And in al-Muhit: if he sleeps on an animal and it is barebacked, they said: if in the state of ascent and levelness, it is not a hadath; if in the state of descent, it is a hadath because his seat is distanced from the animal's back, end. And in these places where it is a hadath, it is in the meaning of tawarruk, so it does not leave the author's speech.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding sleep while sitting (qa'id) and its effect on wudu nullification in the Hanafi school. The author of the commentary clarifies that the matn word 'ynqḍ' (nullifies) is not absolute. The first case: a person sits with legs spread apart and buttocks firmly on the ground—this sleep does not nullify wudu because the posterior is not elevated, ensuring some muscular control. This is the apparent view of the madhhab according to Abu Hanifa, adopted by most shaykhs and deemed the most correct in al-Bada'i', though al-Quduri held nullification. The second case: if the seat (posterior) is elevated from the ground (like in tawarruk sitting), it nullifies by consensus, as there is no muscle control. Then the commentary lists additional scenarios: sleeping on a tandoor (with legs dangling) nullifies; sleeping with knees drawn up and head on knees does not nullify; sleeping on a barebacked animal: during ascent or level motion, no nullification; during descent, nullification because the buttocks are distanced from the back. All nullifying cases revert to the meaning of tawarruk, thus consistent with the author's intended ruling.

Key Terms
التورك (al-tawarruk): Sitting posture where one's posterior is elevated off the ground, e.g., sitting on one's hip
الاستناد (al-istinad): Leaning on something for support while sleeping

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on wudu if a person sleeps while sitting with his feet extended and buttocks on the ground?
A) Wudu is nullified because sleep is a hadath.
B) Wudu is not nullified because the posterior is on the ground, ensuring control.
C) Wudu is nullified only if he intends to sleep.
D) Wudu is not nullified only if he is leaning against something.

Key Takeaway
Sleep while sitting only nullifies wudu when the posterior is elevated, as in tawarruk; sitting firmly on a surface preserves wudu.

Sleep in Prayer and Wudu Nullification: The Hanafi Position and Abu Yusuf's Objection
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wudu nullification by sleep in prayer, especially sujud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wudu nullification by sleep in prayer, distinguish between prayer and non-prayer contexts, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Sleep in prayer does not nullify wudu, even in prostration — analogy is abandoned for a specific text.

Does falling asleep during prayer nullify your wudu? The answer depends on whether you are inside or outside prayer, and which Hanafi authority you follow. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the dispute between the majority of the school (Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and Abu Yusuf, focusing on the prostration posture. The key is that a specific hadith overrides the general analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general Hanafi position
Find the statement that sleep while standing, sitting, bowing, or prostrating in prayer does not nullify wudu. Note that this applies absolutely inside prayer.
The text says 'لا ينقض نوم القائم ولا القاعد ولا الراكع ولا الساجد مطلقاً إن كان في الصلاة'.
2
Contrast with the ruling outside prayer
Outside prayer, prostration has a condition: it must be in the sunnah posture (raising belly, arms away). If not, wudu is nullified. Understand that this follows the general analogy based on muscular control (istimsak vs. istitlaq).
The author says 'فإن سجد على غير هذه الهيئة انتقض وضوءه'.
3
Identify the intra-school dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that intentional sleep in prayer, especially in sujud, nullifies wudu. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold it does not. Locate the reference to Abu Yusuf's narration.
The text mentions 'روي عن أبي يوسف أنه إذا تعمد النوم في السجود ينقض'.
4
Analyze the dalil for each side
The majority relies on a hadith: 'No wudu for one who sleeps standing, bowing, or prostrating.' Abu Yusuf uses qiyas to sleep outside prayer. See how the text says 'وهذا هو القياس في الصلاة إلا أنا تركناه فيها' – the analogy was abandoned.
The hadith is referenced: 'لا وضوء على من نام قائماً أو راكعاً أو ساجداً'.
5
Determine the tarjih and mufta bihi
The text indicates that the majority view is the chosen (mukhtar) and the relied-upon position. Abu Yusuf's view is a minority narration. The practical consequence: even intentional sleep in sujud during prayer does not nullify wudu, though it may invalidate the prayer itself.
The commentator states 'المختار الأول' (the first is chosen) and notes the hadith of boasting by Allah for the sleeping prostrator.

الصحيح .اهـ وكذلك ذكره الشرنبلالي في متنه نور الإيضاح حيث في الأشياء التي لا تنقض الوضوء : ومنها نوم مصلي ولو راكعاً أو ساجداً إذا كان على جهة السنة في ظاهر المذهب. قال في النهر إلا أن هذا لم يوجد في المحيط الرضوي اهـ. قوله: (وأطلق في الهداية الصلاة صوابه النوم بدل الصلاة قوله : ( وينبغي حمل ما في الخانية على رواية أبي يوسف) وحينئذ الذي تقدم من رواية أبي يوسف أنه إذا تعمد النوم في الصلاة نقض، وكذا في الفتح كما ترى غير مقيدة بالسجود تأمل. ثم رأيت في غاية البيان ما نصه: وروي عن أبي يوسف رحمه الله تعالى في الإملاء أنه إذا تعمد النوم في السجود ينقض، وإن غلبت عيناه فلا ينقض اهـ. وبه يترجح الحمل المذكور ويكون المراد حينئذ مما تقدم من قوله في الصلاة» أي في سجودها فقط فافهم. ثم في شرح الشيخ إسماعيل اعترض هذا الحمل بقوله :أقول : ولا يخفى أنه لا يلزم من فساد
The author restricted by the sleep of the reclining and the mutawarrik because the sleep of the standing and the sitting does not nullify, even in a litter or howdah, as in al-Khulasa; nor does the sleep of the bowing or prostrating absolutely, whether in prayer or outside it, except in prostration, where it is required that it be in the sunnah manner: raising his belly from his thighs, keeping his arms away from his sides. If he prostrates in other than this manner, his wudu is nullified because in the first case there remains holding and release is absent, contrary to the second case. This is the analogy in prayer except that we abandoned it therein. ... The intended meaning of al-istitlaq is what is narrated in the hadith: 'The two eyes are the drawstring of the anus; when the eyes sleep, the drawstring is released.' ... He said: (And this is the analogy in prayer) i.e., nullification during sleep in prostration other than the sunnah manner is the analogy in prayer due to lack of holding, just as outside prayer; they abandoned analogy therein and considered it outside due to the text: 'No wudu is required for one who sleeps standing, bowing, or prostrating; wudu is only required for one who sleeps reclining.' ... Al-Zayla'i said: 'If outside prayer, then likewise in the correct view if he is in the posture of prostration – raising his belly from his thighs, keeping his arms from his sides – otherwise his wudu is nullified.' ... The commentator: 'Al-Zayla'i declared it the most correct'; the pronoun refers back to his statement 'if outside prayer, then likewise except in prostration.' ... He said: (He made it absolute in al-Hidayah regarding prayer) the correct wording is 'sleep' instead of 'prayer'. ... He said: (And it is appropriate to interpret the statement in al-Khaniyah according to the narration of Abu Yusuf) ... Then I saw in Ghayat al-Bayan: It is narrated from Abu Yusuf in al-Imla' that if he intentionally sleeps in prostration, it nullifies; if his eyes overcome him, it does not nullify. ... Then in the commentary of Shaykh Isma'il, he objected to this interpretation by saying: 'It is not hidden that nullification does not necessarily follow from invalidity.'
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about wudu nullification by sleep during prayer, focusing on the prostration (sujud) posture. The author first states the general ruling: sleep while standing, sitting, bowing, or prostrating in prayer does not nullify wudu. However, for prostration outside prayer, the sunnah posture (belly raised, arms apart) preserves wudu; any other posture nullifies it. Inside prayer, by analogy, non-sunnah prostration should nullify, but the school abandoned the analogy in favor of a text (hadith) stating no wudu for one sleeping while bowing or prostrating. The text referenced is likely the hadith: 'No wudu for one who sleeps standing, bowing, or prostrating; wudu is only for one sleeping reclining.' Al-Zayla'i clarifies that outside prayer, the ruling depends on the sunnah posture of sujud. The dispute with Abu Yusuf arises: in his view, intentional sleep in prayer (especially sujud) nullifies wudu, while the majority (including Abu Hanifa) hold it does not. The mufta bihi is the majority view. Abu Yusuf's narration in al-Imla' specifies that intentional sleep in sujud nullifies, but an overwhelming doze does not. The passage further discusses the harmonization of these views and the distinction between nullification of wudu and invalidation of prayer (fasād al-salah). The preferred interpretation is that Abu Yusuf's ruling applies only to sleep in sujud, not to other postures in prayer.

Key Terms
الاستمساك (al-istimsak): The ability to hold or control the sphincter muscles during sleep, preventing the release of wind
الاستطلاق (al-istitlaq): The release of the anal sphincter, resulting in potential nullification of wudu
الوكاء (al-wika'): A string or tie used to close a waterskin; metaphorically, the sphincter muscle

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, does sleep while prostrating in prayer nullify wudu?
A) Yes, because prostration involves muscular relaxation.
B) No, because a specific text exempts sleep in prayer postures.
C) Yes, if the prostration is not in the sunnah manner.
D) Only if one intends to sleep deliberately.

Key Takeaway
Sleep in prayer does not nullify wudu due to a specific textual exemption; outside prayer, only sunnah posture sujud preserves wudu.

Sleep in Prayer: Wudu and Validity - Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Expansion on sleep rulings in al-Hidayah with intra-Hanafi differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on sleep during prayer regarding wudu nullification and prayer validity, and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Sleep does not nullify wudu unless control is lost; deliberate sleep may invalidate prayer.

Sleep during prayer is a common occurrence, but its legal effects vary. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the Hidayah, presenting the nuanced disagreements within the Hanafi school. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for the mufti and the practicing Muslim, as it determines whether wudu and prayer need repetition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the passage deals with two separate legal consequences of sleep during prayer: nullification of wudu (naqd al-wudu) and invalidation of the prayer (fasad al-salah). The rulings are not identical.
The text emphasizes that these are distinct.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
Extract from the text that Abu Hanifa holds: sleep during prayer does not nullify wudu regardless of posture or intention, as long as the person does not lose control (e.g., fall). However, deliberate sleep invalidates the prayer.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
3
State the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf distinguishes: deliberate sleep in prostration nullifies wudu, but in bowing it does not. Muhammad ties nullification to whether the seat leaves the ground before awakening.
These are the two main intra-Hanafi opponents.
4
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The text provides evidence: Abu Yusuf's view is based on the vulnerability of prostration; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad emphasize actual loss of control. Note the hadith of the Companions sleeping while waiting for prayer, which is interpreted to apply only to lying down outside prayer.
The argument type is talil (reasoning).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: If one deliberately sleeps in prostration, according to Abu Yusuf both wudu and prayer are invalidated; according to Abu Hanifa only the prayer is invalid. The later authorities prefer Abu Hanifa's view.
The tarjih is supported by al-Zayla'i, al-Halwani, and al-Shurunbulali.

كتاب الطهارة من بالنص، كذا في البدائع وصرح الزيلعي بأنه الأصح. وسجدة التلاوة في هذا كالصلبية وكذا سجدة الشكر عند محمد خلافاً لأبي حنيفة. كذا في فتح القدير وكذا في سجدتي السهو كذا في الخلاصة. وأطلق في الهداية الصلاة فشمل ما كان عن تعمد وما عن غلبة. وعن أبي يوسف : إذا تعمد النوم في الصلاة نقض والمختار الأول وفي فصل ما يفسد الصلاة . فتاوی قاضيخان. وعن أبي يوسف : إذا تعمد النوم في الصلاة نقض والمختار الأول. وفي فصل ما يفسد الصلاة من فتاوى قاضيخان لو نام في ركوعه أو سجوده إن لم يتعمد لا تفسد، وإن تعمد فسدت في السجود دون الركوع هـ. كأنه مبني على قيام المسكة حينئذ في الركوع دون السجود ومقتضى النظر أن يفصل في ذلك السجود إن كان متجافياً لا تفسد وإلا تفسد كذا في فتح القدير. وقد يقال مقتضى الأصح المتقدّم أن لا ينتقض بالنوم في السجود مطلقاً وينبغي حمل ما في الخانية على رواية أبي يوسف وفي جامع الفقه أن النوم في الركوع والسجود لا ينقض الوضوء ولو تعمده ولكن تفسد صلاته كذا في شرح منظومة ابن وهبان. وفي الخلاصة : لو نام قاعداً فسقط على الأرض عن أبي حنيفة أنه إن انتبه قبل أن الأسرار من المشاهير. ثم إن الزيلعي قال بعد ما ذكر النص السابق : وإن كان خارج الصلاة فكذلك في الصحيح إن كان على هيئة السجود بأن كان رافعاً بطنه عن فخذيه مجافياً عضديه عن جنبيه وإلا ينقض وضوءه اهـ. فقول الشارح وصرح الزيلعي بأنه الأصح الضمير المنصوب فيه يعود إلى قوله وإن كان خارجها فكذلك إلا في السجود الخ. خلاف ما يوهمه ظاهر العبارة من أنه راجع إلى قوله «وهذا هو القياس» إذ هو أقرب والأحسن إرجاعه إلى قوله كذا في البدائع» لأن ما في البدائع من التفصيل هو ما ذكره الزيلعي ومما يؤيد أن الضمير ليس راجعاً إلى ما هو القياس قوله الآتي «مقتضى الأصح المتقدم الخ وبه سقط نسبة السهو إلى المؤلف التي ذكرها في النهر. ثم إنه يفهم من كلام الزيلعي من كلام الشارح أيضاً أن عدم الفساد في سجود الصلاة مطلقاً متفق عليه مع أنه نقل في النهر عن عقد الفرائد ما نصه : إنما لا يفسد الوضوء بنوم الساجد في الصلاة إذا كان على الهيئة المسنونة . قيد به في المحيط وهو الصحيح .اهـ وكذلك ذكره الشرنبلالي في متنه نور الإيضاح حيث في الأشياء التي لا تنقض الوضوء : ومنها نوم مصلي ولو راكعاً أو ساجداً إذا كان على جهة السنة في ظاهر المذهب. قال في النهر إلا أن هذا لم يوجد في المحيط الرضوي اهـ. قوله: (وأطلق في الهداية الصلاة صوابه النوم بدل الصلاة قوله : ( وينبغي حمل ما في الخانية على رواية أبي يوسف) وحينئذ الذي تقدم من رواية أبي يوسف أنه إذا تعمد النوم في الصلاة نقض، وكذا في الفتح كما ترى غير مقيدة بالسجود تأمل. ثم رأيت في غاية البيان ما نصه: وروي عن أبي يوسف رحمه الله تعالى في الإملاء أنه إذا تعمد النوم في السجود ينقض، وإن غلبت عيناه فلا ينقض اهـ. وبه يترجح الحمل المذكور ويكون المراد حينئذ مما تقدم من قوله في الصلاة» أي في سجودها فقط فافهم. ثم في شرح الشيخ إسماعيل اعترض هذا الحمل بقوله :أقول : ولا يخفى أنه لا يلزم من فساد قال وهي . كتاب الطهارة يصيب جنبه الأرض أو عند إصابة جنبه الأرض بلا فصل لم ينتقض وضوءه، وعن أبي يوسف أنه ينتقض، وعن محمد أنه إن انتبه قبل أن تزايل مقعدته الأرض لم ينتقض وضوءه، وإن زايل مقعدته الأرض قبل أن ينتبه انتقض والفتوى على رواية أبي حنيقة، قال شمس الأئمة الحلواني ظاهر المذهب عن أبي حنيفة كما روي عن محمد قيل هو المعتمد، وسواء سقط أو لم يسقط. وإن نام جالساً وهو يتمايل ربما تزول مقعدته عن الأرض وربما لا تزول. قال شمس الأئمة الحلواني: ظاهر المذهب أنه لا يكون حدثاً. ولو وضع يده على الأرض فاستيقظ لا ينتقض الوضوء، سواء وضع بطن الكف أو ظهر الكف ما لم يضع جنبه على الأرض قبل التيقظ ا.هـ. وقيد بالنوم لأن النعاس مضطجعاً لا ذكر له في المذهب، والظاهر أنه ليس بحدث وقال أبو علي الدقاق وأبو علي الرازي : إن كان لا يفهم عامة ما قيل عنده كان حدثاً كذا في شروح الهداية. وبهذا تبين أن ما في التبيين على قول الشيخين لا على الظاهر، وعليه يحمل ما في سنن البزار بإسناد صحيح كان أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ينتظرون الصلاة فيضعون جنوبهم فمنهم من ينام ثم يقوم إلى الصلاة. فإن النوم مضطجعاً ناقض إلا في حق النبي . صرح في القنية بأنه من خصوصياته ولهذا ورد في الصحيحين أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نام نفخ ثم قام إلى الصلاة ولم يتوضأ لما ورد في حديث آخر أن عيني : الصلاة انتقاض الوضوء لما في السراج لو قرأ أو ركع وسجد وهو نائم تفسد نقض الوضوء بما ذكره هنا من عبارة جوامع الفقه لكن قد يقال : إن الظاهر أن ما في الخانية . الفساد من مبني على نقض الوضوء لتفريقه بين الركوع والسجود تأمل قوله : ( والظاهر أنه ليس بحدث) قال في معراج
