Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of sleep during prayer on wudu nullification and the validity of the prayer. The author, Ibn Nujaym, expands upon the rulings in al-Hidayah, presenting the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, as well as later authorities like al-Zayla'i and al-Halwani. The core issue is whether sleep nullifies wudu and whether it invalidates the prayer. The passage clarifies that the ruling depends on the posture (sitting, bowing, prostrating, lying down) and the depth of sleep (light drowsiness vs. heavy sleep). Abu Hanifa's position is that sleep does not nullify wudu as long as the sitting posture is maintained, even if the person falls or tilts, as long as they awaken before the buttocks leave the ground. Abu Yusuf holds that sleep in prostration nullifies wudu if deliberate, while Muhammad distinguishes based on the timing of awakening relative to the seat leaving the ground. The commentator prefers the view that sleep in prayer does not nullify wudu absolutely, but it may invalidate the prayer if deliberate, especially in prostration. The passage also addresses the hadith of the Companions sleeping while waiting for prayer, interpreting that sleep in a lying-down posture (mudtaji') is considered a hadath (ritual impurity) except for the Prophet ﷺ. The discussion includes a critique of al-Tabyin's classification of drowsiness, reconciling it with the views of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq and Abu Ali al-Razi. The tarjih (preferred opinion) is that the mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the narration from Abu Hanifa as transmitted by Muhammad, and that light drowsiness without loss of awareness does not nullify wudu, while heavy sleep does. The practical takeaway for the student is that a praying person's wudu remains valid if they doze off lightly while in a proper posture (sitting, bowing, or prostrating in the sunna manner), but intentional deep sleep in a non-sunna posture nullifies both wudu and prayer.

Key Terms
النوم (al-nawm): Sleep that overcomes a person, distinguished from light drowsiness (nāʾis). The passage discusses its effect on wudu and prayer validity.
النعاس (al-nuʿās): Light drowsiness where one still hears and understands most of what is said. It is not considered a hadath.
الوضوء (al-wuḍūʾ): Ritual purification required before prayer. The passage discusses whether sleep nullifies it.
النقض (al-naqḍ): Nullification of wudu, meaning the need to renew it before prayer.
مضطجع (muḍṭajiʿ): Lying down on one's side. Sleep in this posture is considered a hadath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on wudu if a person deliberately sleeps in prostration during prayer according to Abu Yusuf?
A) Wudu is nullified.
B) Wudu remains valid.
C) Prayer is invalid but wudu valid.
D) It depends on the person's intention.

Key Takeaway
Sleep in prayer does not nullify wudu unless the person loses control (e.g., falls), but deliberate sleep may invalidate the prayer.

Sleep, Drowsiness, and Wudu: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sleep nullifying wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on sleep nullifying wudu and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, only deep sleep with full relaxation nullifies wudu; drowsiness does not.

Not all sleep is equal in the Hanafi school. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a detailed intra-madhhab dispute among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on exactly when sleep breaks wudu. The debate hinges on the interpretation of the Companions' practice and the Prophet's special status. Understanding this ruling is essential for daily worship, as many situations arise where one may doze off before prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Hanafi positions
Read the text carefully to extract the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. Note the condition each uses: touching the ground, seat leaving the ground, etc.
Abu Hanifa requires the side to touch the ground without gap; Abu Yusuf nullifies for any sleep; Muhammad differentiates based on seat leaving ground.
2
Locate the tarjih (preference)
Find where the author states the fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's narration and who supports it (Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani).
The mufta bihi is that sleep nullifies only when joints fully relax and one loses awareness.
3
Understand the ruling on drowsiness (nu'as)
Distinguish between drowsiness and deep sleep. The text says drowsiness is not a nullifier. Note the additional condition from Abu Ali al-Daqqaq: if one does not understand most speech, it becomes a nullifier.
The author reconciles this by interpreting 'hearing' as 'understanding' and adding the word 'most'.
4
Analyze the dalil (proof)
The hadith of the Companions sleeping and then praying without wudu is cited. Explain how the Hanafi school restricts its application to light sleep, except for the Prophet (sleep of the prophets does not nullify).
The hadith is ahad, so zanni al-thubut, which supports Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a person dozes off while sitting upright and understands most of what is said, does he need to repeat wudu? Answer: No, because it is drowsiness, not deep sleep.
This is the practical thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصيب جنبه الأرض أو عند إصابة جنبه الأرض بلا فصل لم ينتقض وضوءه، وعن أبي يوسف أنه ينتقض، وعن محمد أنه إن انتبه قبل أن تزايل مقعدته الأرض لم ينتقض وضوءه، وإن زايل مقعدته الأرض قبل أن انتبه انتقض والفتوى على رواية أبي حنيفة، قال شمس الأئمة الحلواني ظاهر المذهب عن أبي حنيفة كما روي عن محمد قيل هو المعتمد، وسواء سقط أو لم يسقط. وإن نام جالساً وهو يتمايل ربما تزول مقعدته عن الأرض وربما لا تزول. قال شمس الأئمة الحلواني: ظاهر المذهب أنه لا يكون حدثاً. ولو وضع يده على الأرض فاستيقظ لا ينتقض الوضوء، سواء وضع بطن الكف أو ظهر الكف ما لم يضع جنبه على الأرض قبل التيقظ ا.هـ. وقيد بالنوم لأن النعاس مضطجعاً لا ذكر له في المذهب، والظاهر أنه ليس بحدث وقال أبو علي الدقاق وأبو علي الرازي : إن كان لا يفهم عامة ما قيل عنده كان حدثاً كذا في شروح الهداية. وبهذا تبين أن ما في التبيين على قول الشيخين لا على الظاهر، وعليه يحمل ما في سنن البزار بإسناد صحيح كان أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ينتظرون الصلاة فيضعون جنوبهم فمنهم من ينام ثم يقوم إلى الصلاة. فإن النوم مضطجعاً ناقض إلا في حق النبي . صرح في القنية بأنه من خصوصياته ولهذا ورد في الصحيحين أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نام نفخ ثم قام إلى الصلاة ولم يتوضأ لما ورد في حديث آخر أن عيني تنامان ولا ينام قلبي . ولا يشكل عليه ما ورد في الصحيح من أنه نام ليلة التعريس حتى طلعت الشمس لأن القلب يقظان يحس بالحدث وغيره مما يتعلق بالبدن ويشعر به القلب، وليس طلوع الفجر والشمس من ذلك ولا هو مما يدرك بالقلب وإنما يدرك بالعين وهي نائمة، وهذا هو المشهور في كتب المحدثين والفقهاء كذا في شرح المهذب.
قوله : ( والظاهر أنه ليس بحدث) قال في معراج الدراية : لأنه نوم قليل قوله : وبهذا تبين أن ما في التبيين على قول الشيخين) أي الدقاق والرازي وعبارة التبيين هكذا والنعاس نوعان : ثقيل وهو حدث في حالة الاضطجاع، وخفيف وهو ليس بحدث فيها، والفاصل بينهما أنه إن كان ما قيل عنده فهو خفيف وإلا فهو ثقيل يسمع انتهت وليس فيها التقييد بالفهم فهو غير ما ذكره الشيخان إلا أن يعتبر تقييد السماع بالفهم فيمكن حمله عليه لكن ليس فيه لفظ عامة المشعرة بفهم البعض، بل ظاهره عدم سماع الجميع إلا أن يقال: «عامة) بمعنى الجميع لكن يبقى فيه إشكال وهو أنه إذا كان المراد به أنه لا يسمع ولا يفهم جميع ما قيل عنده فهو نائم لا ناعس وإلا فما الفرق بينهما؟ على أن الذي في معراج الدراية من كلام الشيخين وإن كان يسهو حرفاً أو حرفين فلا اهـ .
فعامة ليس بمعنى الجميع ويمكن أن يحمل السماع على الفهم كما قال شيخنا، ويقدر لفظ أكثر في كلام الزيلعي أي إن كان يفهم أكثر ما قيل عنده فهو خفيف وإلا فهو كثير فيتوافق الكلامان . هذا غاية ما يمكن في هذا المحل فليتأمل. قوله : وعليه يحمل ما في سنن البزار) أي يحمل النوم فيه
If his side touches the ground or at the moment his side touches the ground without any interval, his ablution is not nullified. According to Abu Yusuf, it is nullified. According to Muhammad, if he wakes up before his seat leaves the ground, his ablution is not nullified; if his seat leaves the ground before he wakes up, it is nullified. The fatwa is on the narration of Abu Hanifa. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: The apparent position of the school from Abu Hanifa is as narrated from Muhammad; it is said that is the relied-upon position. Whether he falls or not. If he sleeps sitting and is swaying, possibly his seat leaves the ground and possibly not. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: The apparent position is that it is not an event (hadath). If he places his hand on the ground and wakes up, his ablution is not nullified, whether he places the palm or the back of the hand, as long as he does not place his side on the ground before waking up. It is restricted to sleep because drowsiness (nu'as) while lying down has no mention in the school, and the apparent position is that it is not an event. Abu Ali al-Daqqaq and Abu Ali al-Razi said: If he does not understand most of what is said to him, it is an event; so in the commentaries of al-Hidaya. With this it is clear that what is in al-Tabyin is according to the view of the two shaykhs (al-Daqqaq and al-Razi), not the apparent position. Upon this is carried what is in Sunan al-Bazzar with a sound chain: 'The companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to wait for prayer and place their sides (to lie down); some of them would sleep, then stand for prayer.' For sleep while lying down is a nullifier except in the case of the Prophet (peace be upon him). It is explicitly stated in al-Quniyya that it is one of his special characteristics. For this reason, it is reported in the two Sahihs that the Prophet (peace be upon him) snored in sleep, then stood for prayer without performing ablution, due to another hadith: 'My eyes sleep but my heart does not sleep.' The hadith in Sahih that he slept on the night of Ta'ris until the sun rose does not pose a problem, because the heart is awake and senses the event and other things related to the body, and the heart feels them. The rising of dawn and the sun are not among these, nor are they perceived by the heart; they are perceived by the eye, which is asleep. This is the well-known view in the books of hadith scholars and jurists, as in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab. His statement (and the apparent position is that it is not an event): He said in Mi'raj al-Diraya: because it is a little sleep. His statement: 'With this it is clear that what is in al-Tabyin is according to the view of the two shaykhs' — meaning al-Daqqaq and al-Razi. The wording of al-Tabyin is: 'Drowsiness is of two types: heavy, which is an event in the case of lying down; and light, which is not an event in that state. The distinction between them is that if what is said to him (he understands), it is light; otherwise it is heavy (he hears).' It ends. It does not contain the qualification of understanding, so it is different from what the two shaykhs mentioned, unless one considers the qualification of hearing as understanding, then it can be reconciled. But it does not contain the word 'most' indicating partial understanding; rather, its apparent meaning is not hearing all. Except if it is said that 'most' means 'all'. But there remains an issue: if what is meant is that he neither hears nor understands all that is said to him, then he is asleep, not drowsy; otherwise, what is the difference between them? Add to this that the statement in Mi'raj al-Diraya from the two shaykhs, even if he forgets a letter or two, is not (reliable). So 'most' does not mean 'all'. It is possible to interpret hearing as understanding, as our teacher said, and to assume the word 'most' in al-Zayla'i's statement: i.e., if he understands most of what is said to him, it is light; otherwise it is heavy. Thus the two statements agree. This is the utmost that can be said in this place, so reflect. His statement: 'Upon this is carried what is in Sunan al-Bazzar' — i.e., the sleep therein is carried upon this.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether sleep nullifies wudu. The author first states the ruling of Abu Hanifa: if a person sleeping on his side touches the ground without a gap, wudu is not nullified. Abu Yusuf holds it is nullified. Muhammad differentiates: if he wakes up before his seat leaves the ground, no nullification; if the seat leaves first, it nullifies. The fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani confirms that the apparent position (zahir al-madhhab) from Abu Hanifa is as narrated from Muhammad. The passage then clarifies that sleep while sitting or leaning does not nullify unless the side touches the ground. Drowsiness (nu'as) while lying down is not a nullifier according to the school. Two scholars, Abu Ali al-Daqqaq and Abu Ali al-Razi, however, say that if a person does not understand most of what is said to him, it is a nullifier. The author resolves the discrepancy by interpreting 'hearing' as 'understanding' and estimating the word 'most' in al-Zayla'i's statement. The hadith of the Companions sleeping while waiting for prayer is interpreted as referring to light sleep that does not nullify, except for the Prophet (ﷺ) whose sleep did not nullify wudu due to his heart being awake. Thus, the ruling is that deep sleep (with full relaxation and loss of awareness) nullifies wudu, while light sleep or drowsiness does not.

Key Terms
نوم (nawm): Sleep; in this text, it refers to deep sleep that nullifies wudu only under specific conditions (lying down, joints relaxed).
نعاس (nu'as): Drowsiness; a light state of sleep where one still hears and understands most speech. Does not nullify wudu.
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity that nullifies wudu; in this context, the event (sleep) that causes hadath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view (mufta bihi), when does sleep nullify wudu?
A) Any sleep regardless of position
B) Only sleep while lying down with full relaxation of joints and loss of awareness
C) Sleep while sitting but leaning
D) Drowsiness while lying down if one does not understand speech

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, deep sleep with full relaxation and loss of awareness nullifies wudu; light sleep and drowsiness do not.

Nullification of Wudu by Unconsciousness, Insanity, and Imbecility
Expanding on nullifiers of wudu: unconsciousness, insanity, and imbecility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on unconsciousness and insanity as nullifiers of wudu, citing the consensus, and distinguish the three opinions on imbecility ('utah) and identify the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness and insanity always nullify wudu; imbecility does not, but its obligations are suspended.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains three states that affect wudu: unconsciousness (ighmā'), insanity (junūn), and imbecility ('utah). While the first two are unanimously nullifiers, imbecility is a milder condition that the Hanafi school treats differently. Understanding the precise rulings and proofs helps you apply fiqh correctly to real-life cases involving loss of intellect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage lists unconsciousness, insanity, intoxication, laughter in prayer, and direct touching of private parts as nullifiers of wudu. Focus on the three states: ighmā', junūn, and 'utah. Determine whether each is a nullifier and at what level.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqā'iq; the commentary expands on the ruling and proof.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a consensus (ijmā') reported by al-Nawawī in Sharḥ al-Muhadhdhab that unconsciousness and insanity nullify wudu. This is a transmitted proof (dalīl naqlī). Identify the type and strength of the proof.
Consensus is a primary source; here it is transmitted via Nawawī's report, which has zannī thubūt but qat'ī dalālah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The rationale: unconsciousness and insanity involve loss of choice and inability to sense the occurrence of ḥadath (ritual impurity). Unlike sleep, which may allow for some awareness, these states completely prevent awareness, so the nullification is absolute.
The author contrasts with sleep, which only nullifies when joints are fully relaxed due to prevalence of wind exit.
4
Note the ikhtilāf on imbecility
The passage discusses three opinions on 'utah: (1) like a child – no obligation at all; (2) obligation for worship only; (3) not obligated but must make up light acts if the condition ceases. Identify each opinion and its proponents.
This is an intra-Hanafī dispute; the relied-upon view is the third, from al-Bustī.
5
Evaluate the tarjīḥ
The author of al-Bahr al-Rā'iq prefers al-Bustī's view as closer to verification (aqrab ilā al-taḥqīq). Explain why: it balances the imbecile's limited capacity with the importance of worship, requiring makeup only for light acts without hardship.
The muftā bihī position is that imbecility does not nullify wudu and obligations are suspended during the state.

وإغماء وجنون وسكر وقهقهة مصل بالغ ومباشرة فاحشة لا خروج دودة من جرح، تنامان ولا ينام قلبي . ولا يشكل عليه ما ورد في الصحيح من أنه نام ليلة التعريس حتى طلعت الشمس لأن القلب يقظان يحس بالحدث وغيره مما يتعلق بالبدن ويشعر به القلب، وليس طلوع الفجر والشمس من ذلك ولا هو مما يدرك بالقلب وإنما يدرك بالعين وهي نائمة، وهذا هو المشهور في كتب المحدثين والفقهاء كذا في شرح المهذب.

وحكمه. أما الأول فهو آفة توجب الاختلال بالعقل بحيث يصير مختلط الكلام فاسد التدبير لأنه لا يضرب ولا يشتم. وأما الثاني فقد اختلف فيه على ثلاثة أقوال : ففي أصول فخر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة والمنار والمغني And unconsciousness, insanity, intoxication, laughter of an adult in prayer, and direct touching of a private part [are nullifiers]; but not the exit of a worm from a wound. 'My eyes sleep but my heart does not sleep.' The hadith in Sahih that he slept on the night of Ta'ris until the sun rose does not pose a problem, because the heart is awake and senses the event and other things related to the body, and the heart feels them. The rising of dawn and the sun are not among these, nor are they perceived by the heart; they are perceived by the eye, which is asleep. This is the well-known view in the books of hadith scholars and jurists, as in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab. His statement: (And unconsciousness and insanity) meaning they nullify wudu. As for unconsciousness: it is a type of illness that weakens the faculties and does not remove understanding (al-hija), i.e., intellect, but rather veils it, unlike insanity which removes it. For this reason, the Prophet (peace be upon him) was not protected from unconsciousness as he was from illnesses, but he was protected from insanity. It is like sleep in the loss of choice and loss of use of ability, so that his utterances are invalid. Rather, it is more severe than sleep because sleep is an original period and when one is awakened, he wakes up; unconsciousness is an accidental state, and its sufferer does not wake up when aroused. So it is a hadath (ritual impurity) in every case. Hence it was made absolute in al-Mukhtasar, unlike sleep, which is not a hadath unless his joints are fully relaxed and thus exit (of wind) is prevalent, so the cause is made to stand in its place. Unlike other cases where non-exit is prevalent, so the cause is not made to stand in its place, and non-nullification is the original principle of qiyas, which requires that non-external matter does not nullify. This refutes what has occurred in many books that qiyas would make sleep a hadath in all circumstances. Al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab transmitted consensus on the nullification by unconsciousness and insanity. It is said: 'ghamiya 'alayhi' and 'ghummiya 'alayhi' — he is unconscious. A man is 'ghami' meaning unconscious; likewise for dual, plural, and feminine. Some have made dual and plural: 'raja'an aghmiyan' and 'rijal aghmā'. As for insanity: it is loss of intellect, and its nullification is apparent due to his lack of care and distinction between hadath and other things. Some shaykhs have explained it by predominance of relaxation, but it was refuted because insanity can be stronger than health; so what we said is better, as in al-'Inaya. As for imbecility ('utah): I have not seen anyone mention it among the nullifiers. It is necessary to explain its reality and ruling. As for the first: it is a defect that causes derangement of intellect such that he becomes confused in speech and corrupt in management, because he does not hit or insult. As for the second: there are three opinions: In the Usul of Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma and al-Manar and al-Mughni and al-Tawdih, he is like an intelligent child in all rulings, so address is removed from him. In al-Taqwim of Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, his ruling is like that of an intelligent child except in acts of worship; we do not drop obligation from him as a precaution at the time of address. Sadr al-Islam Abu al-Yusr refuted this, saying it is a type of insanity, so it prevents obligation because he does not consider consequences. In Usul al-Busti, the imbecile is not obligated to perform acts of worship like an intelligent child, except that if the imbecility ceases, the address to perform immediately falls upon him, and he must make up what passed if there is no hardship, such as a little. So it clearly states that he makes up a little but not a lot, even though he was not addressed before, like the sleeping and unconscious person, unlike the child when he reaches puberty. This is closer to verification, as in Sharh al-Mughni al-Hindi. The apparent speech of all agrees on the validity of his performance of acts of worship. As for the one who considers him obligated (to perform them),
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage begins by listing nullifiers of wudu from the matn: unconsciousness, insanity, intoxication, laughter in prayer, and direct touching of private parts. It then focuses on unconsciousness (ighma') and insanity (junun). Unconsciousness is a illness that weakens faculties and veils intellect, while insanity removes it. Both are unanimous nullifiers according to Nawawi. The rationale is that they involve loss of choice and inability to sense the hadath. Unlike sleep, unconsciousness is not a natural state, so it always nullifies. The passage then discusses imbecility ('utah), a mild intellectual disability. There are three opinions: (1) The imbecile is like an intelligent but non-pubescent child—no obligations at all. (2) He is like a child in transactions but still obligated for worship out of caution. (3) He is not obligated for worship unless the imbecility leaves, then he must make up light acts. All agree that if he performs worship, it is valid.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighmā'): Unconsciousness; a state of illness that veils intellect without removing it.
جنون (junūn): Insanity; complete loss of intellect.
عتة (`utah): Imbecility; a mild intellectual defect causing confusion in speech and poor management but not full loss of reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon Hanafi view regarding the obligation of worship on a person afflicted with imbecility ('utah)?
A) He is fully obligated like a sane adult
B) He is not obligated at all, like a child without intellect
C) He is obligated only for acts of worship, but not transactions
D) He is not obligated during imbecility but must make up light acts after recovery

Key Takeaway
Unconsciousness and insanity nullify wudu by consensus, while imbecility does not; the imbecile's worship obligations are suspended during his state, with limited makeup upon recovery.

Unconsciousness and Insanity Nullify Wudu: Ruling, Proof, and the Case of 'Atah
Expansion probe on nullifiers of wudu with ijma proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that unconsciousness (ighmā') and insanity (junūn) nullify wudu, cite the proof of ijma, and distinguish the ruling on 'atah.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness and insanity nullify wudu by consensus, and 'atah does not—despite intra-Hanafi debate on obligation.

This lesson unpacks the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. We explore why unconsciousness and insanity break wudu even more definitively than sleep, based on loss of control. The passage also clarifies the status of 'atah (mental weakness), which does not nullify wudu, and discusses three Hanafi opinions on whether a ma'tuh is obligated to worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage addresses nullification of wudu. The ruling: unconsciousness and insanity nullify wudu, making it obligatory to renew.
This is a wajib ruling due to hadath.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites ijma (consensus) transmitted by al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab as the proof for nullification by ighmā' and junūn.
Ijma is a qat'i dalil in Hanafi usul.
3
Distinguish from sleep
Sleep only nullifies when joints are completely relaxed (gainb al-istirkhā'), but unconsciousness nullifies in all cases because it involves a total lack of awareness and control.
The Prophet's heart remained awake, so his sleep did not nullify.
4
Define 'atah
'Atah is an affliction that disturbs the intellect (confused speech, poor management) but does not remove it. It does not nullify wudu.
None of the Hanafi scholars list 'atah among nullifiers.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'atah
Three opinions exist on whether a ma'tuh is obligated to perform worship, but all agree that 'atah does not nullify wudu.
The preferred view is that the ma'tuh is not obligated but should make up a little if cured.

جعله مكلفاً بها فظاهر، وكذا من لم يجعله مكلفاً لأنه جعله كالصبي العاقل وقد صرحوا بصحة عباداته فيفهم منه أن العتة لا ينقض الوضوء والله سبحانه الموفق .
And unconsciousness, insanity, intoxication, loud laughter of a praying adult, and direct obscenity, but not a worm exiting from a wound. 'They both sleep, but my heart does not sleep.' And it is not problematic that it is authentically reported that he slept the night of al-Ta'ris until the sun rose, because the heart is awake, sensing the event and other things related to the body, and the heart feels it. The dawn and the sun are not from that, nor are they perceived by the heart; they are perceived by the eye, which is asleep. This is what is well-known in the books of hadith scholars and jurists, as in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab. His statement: 'and unconsciousness and insanity' means they nullify it. As for unconsciousness, it is a kind of illness that weakens the faculties and does not remove intellect, but rather veils it, unlike insanity, which removes it. Hence the Prophet (peace be upon him) was not protected from unconsciousness as from illnesses, but was protected from insanity. It is like sleep in lacking choice and the use of ability, so his expressions become invalid, but even more so, because sleep is an original period, and when awakened, he awakens. Unconsciousness is an accident, and its possessor does not awaken when stimulated, so it is an event in all cases. Hence it was made absolute in al-Mukhtasar. Unlike sleep, it is only an event if his joints relax completely, so excretion becomes predominant; then the cause is established in its place. In other than this state, the predominant is its absence, so the cause is not established in its place. Thus non-nullification is on the basis of analogy, which requires that what does not exit does not nullify. And by this is refuted what is found in many books that analogy would require sleep to be an event in all states. Al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab has transmitted consensus on the nullifying nature of unconsciousness and insanity. It is said: 'he was unconscious' and 'he is unconscious' and 'he is unconscious', etc. As for insanity, it is the removal of intellect, and its nullification is apparent considering his lack of care and distinction of the event from other things. Some shaykhs explained it by prevalence of relaxation, but it was refuted because an insane person may be stronger than a healthy one, so what we said is better, as in al-'Inayah. As for 'atah (mental weakness), I have not seen anyone mentioning it among the nullifiers, and it is necessary to clarify its reality and ruling. As for the first, it is an affliction that causes disturbance in the intellect such that his speech becomes confused and his management corrupt, because he does not hit or insult. As for the second, there are three opinions: In Usul Fakhr al-Islam, Shams al-A'immah, al-Manar, al-Mughni, and al-Tawdih, it is like a sane child in all rulings, so address is removed from him. In al-Taqwim by Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, his ruling is that of a sane child except in worship; we do not drop obligation from him as a precaution at the time of address. Sadr al-Islam Abu al-Yusr refuted it saying it is a type of insanity, so obligation is prevented because he does not understand consequences. In Usul al-Busti, the ma'tuh is not obligated to perform worship like a sane child, except that if the 'atah is removed, the address for performance becomes due immediately and for making up what passed if there is no hardship, like a little; he stated that he makes up a little but not a lot, even if he was not addressed previously like the sleeping and unconscious person, unlike the child when he reaches puberty. This is closest to verification, as in Sharh al-Mughni by al-Hindi. The apparent words of all agree on the validity of his performance of worship. As for those who consider him obligated, it is apparent; likewise those who do not consider him obligated, because they consider him like a sane child, and they have stated the validity of his worship. It is understood from this that 'atah does not nullify wudu, and Allah is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the ruling that unconsciousness and insanity nullify wudu. The author begins by clarifying the hadith of the Prophet's sleep, explaining that his heart remained awake, so sleep does not always nullify wudu. He then defines unconsciousness (ighmā') as a veiling of intellect, not removal, and distinguishes it from insanity (junūn) which removes intellect. Both are nullifiers because they involve loss of control, even more so than sleep. The text cites consensus (ijma') from al-Nawawi to confirm this. It also discusses 'atah (mental weakness), clarifying that it is not a nullifier of wudu, and presents three Hanafi opinions regarding whether a ma'tuh is obligated to perform worship. The author concludes that all agree on the validity of his worship and that 'atah does not nullify wudu.

Key Terms
الإغماء (al-ighmā'): A type of illness that weakens the faculties and veils the intellect without removing it.
الجنون (al-junūn): Removal of intellect; causes complete loss of discrimination.
العتة (al-'atah): An affliction that disturbs the intellect, causing confused speech and poor management, but without violent behavior.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 'Atah nullifies wudu only if it involves loss of consciousness.
D) 'Atah nullifies wudu except for a ma'tuh who is mukallaf.

Key Takeaway
Unconsciousness and insanity break wudu by consensus; 'atah does not, even though its status regarding worship is debated.

Intoxication as a Nullifier of Wudu: Definition and Threshold
Commentary on intoxication as a nullifier of wudu with definitions and disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of intoxication (sukr) for wudu nullification, distinguish between the two thresholds, and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Intoxication nullifies wudu; the relied-upon threshold is disturbance in gait, not loss of gender distinction.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses intoxication (sukr) as a nullifier of wudu. It defines sukr as a pleasure that overcomes the intellect without removing it, and discusses two thresholds: one based on the hadd penalty (not knowing man from woman) and another based on visible disturbance (gait). The author clarifies that the latter is the corrected view for wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is intoxication (sukr). The ruling: it nullifies wudu, making it invalid (batil).
The passage begins with 'wa sukr' meaning 'and intoxication' as a nullifier.
2
Understand the definition of sukr
Sukr is a pleasure that overcomes the intellect without removing it. The person remains eligible for divine address (ahliyyah li al-khitab).
Al-Hakim al-Tirmidhi's explanation: intellect resides in the head, its ray in the chest; wine creates a barrier.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
There are two definitions: (1) not knowing a man from a woman (same as hadd penalty), (2) disturbance in gait.
The first view is from Sadr al-Islam; the second from Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani.
4
Analyze the tarjih
The second definition (gait disturbance) is corrected and relied upon in works like al-Mujtaba and Sharh al-Wiqayah.
This is the mufta bihi position for wudu nullification.
5
Apply to practical cases
For a hermaphrodite (khuntha) swearing about intoxication, the same gait criterion applies. If intoxication occurs during prayer, the prayer is also invalidated.
Ibn Wahban's poem is interpreted to mean the nullification occurs if the state arises during the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وسكر) أي وينقضه سكر وهو سرور يغلب على العقل بمباشرة بعض الأسباب الموجبة له فيمتنع الإنسان عن العمل بموجب عقله من غير أن يزيله، ولذا بقي أهلاً للخطاب. وقيل إنه يزيله وتكليفه مع زوال عقله بطريق الزجر عليه، والتحقيق الأول لما ذكره الحكيم الترمذي في نوادره العقل في الرأس وشعاعه في الصدر والقلب، فالقلب يهتدي بنوره لتدبير الأمور وتمييز الحسن من القبيح، فإذا شرب الخمر خلص أثرها إلى الصدر فحال بينه وبين نور العقل فيبقى الصدر مظلماً فلم ينتفع القلب بنور العقل فسمي ذلك سكراً لأنه سكر حاجز بينه وبين العقل. وقد اختلف في حده هنا ففي الخلاصة والولوالجية والينابيع ونقله في المضمرات والتبيين عن صدر الإسلام وعزاه مسكين إلى شرح المبسوط أن حده هو وحده في وجوب الحد وهو من لا يعرف الرجل من المرأة. وقال شمس الأئمة الحلواني : هو من حصل في مشيته اختلال وصححه في المجتبى وشرح الوقاية والمضمرات وشرح مسكين. قالوا: وكذا الجواب في الخنث إذا حلف أنه ليس بسكران وكان على الصفة التي قلنا يحنث في يمينه وإن لم يكن بحال لا يعرف الرجل من المرأة. وقد ذكر ابن وهبان في منظومته أن السكر يبطل الوضوء والصلاة وهو محمول على أنه شرب المسكر فقام الصلاة قبل أن يصير إلى هذه الحالة ثم صار في أثنائها إلى حالة لو مشى فيها يتحرك.
His statement: 'and intoxication' meaning it nullifies it, and it is a pleasure that overcomes the intellect by pursuing some causes that necessitate it, so the person refrains from acting according to his intellect without removing it. Hence he remains eligible for address. And it was said that it removes it, and his obligation with the removal of his intellect is by way of deterrence. The verification is the first because of what al-Hakim al-Tirmidhi mentioned in his Nawadir: the intellect is in the head and its ray is in the chest and heart, so the heart is guided by its light to manage affairs and distinguish good from bad. When one drinks wine, its effect reaches the chest and intervenes between it and the light of the intellect, so the chest remains dark and the heart does not benefit from the light of the intellect. That is called intoxication because it is a barrier between him and the intellect. There is disagreement on its limit here. In al-Khulasah, al-Walwaljiyyah, and al-Yanabi', and it is transmitted in al-Mudmarat and al-Tabyin from Sadr al-Islam, and Masakin attributed it to Sharh al-Mabsut, that its limit is the same as the limit for the obligation of the hadd penalty: he who does not know the man from the woman. And Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani said: he is the one whose gait becomes disturbed. And this was corrected in al-Mujtaba, Sharh al-Wiqayah, al-Mudmarat, and Sharh Masakin. They said: likewise the answer in the case of a hermaphrodite: if he swore that he is not intoxicated, and he was in the condition we mentioned, he breaks his oath, even if he is not in a state where he does not know man from woman. And Ibn Wahban mentioned in his poem that intoxication invalidates wudu and prayer, and this is interpreted as: he drank an intoxicant, then he prayed before reaching that state, then during the prayer he reached a state where if he walked he would be unsteady.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage comments on intoxication (sukr) as a nullifier of wudu. It defines sukr as a pleasure that overcomes the intellect without removing it, using al-Hakim al-Tirmidhi's explanation of the intellect and its ray. There is a dispute on the threshold for sukr: one view is that it is when the person cannot distinguish a man from a woman (the same as for the hadd penalty); another view, endorsed by Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani, is that it is when the person's gait becomes disturbed. The text notes that this latter view is adopted in several works. It also discusses the practical application for a hermaphrodite who swears he is not intoxicated. Finally, it cites Ibn Wahban's poem and interprets it to mean that the nullification of wudu and prayer occurs if the intoxication reaches the threshold during prayer, not before.

Key Terms
السكر (al-sukr): A pleasure that overcomes the intellect without removing it, acting as a barrier between the heart and the intellect.
الخنثى (al-khunthā): A hermaphrodite; the ruling on an oath about intoxication applies to them based on the same criteria.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The mufta bihi definition of intoxication for wudu nullification is disturbance in gait, not the hadd threshold of not knowing man from woman.

Is Loud Laughter in Prayer a Hadath or a Punishment?
Ruling on loud laughter in prayer as a nullifier of wudu, with an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi positions on loud laughter (qahqaha) in prayer and state the practical consequence regarding touching the Quran.

Core Idea
Loud laughter in prayer requires wudu, but its nature affects whether you can touch the Quran afterward.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq delves into an important intra-Hanafi dispute: does loud laughter (qahqaha) during prayer nullify wudu as a ritual impurity (hadath), or does it obligate wudu as a disciplinary measure? The apparent wording of the matn suggests it is a hadath, but Abu Zayd al-Dabusi and others argue from qiyas that it is a sound like crying, not an impurity. The practical difference matters for acts like touching the Mushaf.

Guided Path
1
Define qahqaha
Identify the linguistic and terminological meaning: audible laughter in prayer, whether teeth appear or not.
The definition is key to understanding why it might or might not be a hadath.
2
Identify the two positions
One view: qahqaha is a hadath. The other view: it is not a hadath; wudu is obligatory as punishment.
Both are within the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the proofs
The first view relies on apparent wording of the matn. The second uses qiyas: it is a sound, not impure excretion, similar to crying or drowsiness.
Qiyas is central to the second view.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
If a hadath, touching the Quran is forbidden. If a punishment, touching is permitted after wudu.
This is the practical consequence that affects daily practice.
5
Determine the tarjih
The text prefers the second view because it agrees with qiyas and avoids considering a sound as impurity.
The author of al-Nahr supports this tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وقهقهة مصل (بالغ أي وينقضه قهقهة وهي في اللغة معروفة وهو أن يقول قهقه وقهقه بمعنى واصطلاحاً ما يكون مسموعاً له والجيرانه بدت أسنانه أو لا. وظاهر كلام المصنف وجماعة أن القهقهة من الأحداث. وقال بعضهم: إنها ليست حدثاً فإنما يجب الوضوء بها عقوبة وزجراً وهو ظاهر كلام جماعة منهم القاضي أبو زيد الدبوسي في الأسرار وهو موافق للقياس لأنها ليست خارجاً نجساً بل هي صوت كالبكاء والكلام. وفائدة الخلاف أن من جعلها حدثاً منع جواز مس المصحف معها كسائر الأحداث ومن أوجب الوضوء عقوبة جوز مس المصحف معها هكذا نقل الخلاف وفائدته في معراج الدراية. وينبغي ترجيح الثاني لموافقته القياس وسلامته مما يقال من أنها ليست نجاسة ولا سببها وموافقة الأحاديث على النعاس. قول : (المصنف وسكر قال الرملي : أطلق السكر فشمل السكر من مباح لقولهم السكر من مباح كالإغماء. قوله : (وظاهر كلام المصنف وجماعة الخ فيه كما قال في النهر : إن ظاهر كلامه الثاني بدليل قوله «بالغ» إذ لو كانت حدثاً لاستوى فيها البالغ وغيره قوله : (وفائدة الخلاف الخ) قال في النهر : وينبغي أن يظهر أيضاً . في كتابة القرآن وأما حل الطواف بهذا الوضوء ففيه تردد، وإلحاق الطواف بالصلاة يؤذن بأنه لا يجوز فتدبر قوله : ( وينبغي ترجيح الثاني الخ) أيده في النهر جوز مس
His statement: 'and loud laughter of a praying adult' meaning it nullifies it. It is known in language, meaning one says 'qahqaha' and it is understood. Terminologically, it is what is audible to him and his neighbors, whether his teeth appear or not. The apparent meaning of the author's statement and a group is that loud laughter is from the events. And some said: it is not an event; rather, wudu becomes obligatory by it as a punishment and deterrent. This is the apparent statement of a group, including al-Qadi Abu Zayd al-Dabusi in al-Asrar, and it is in accordance with analogy because it is not an impure excretion, but rather a sound like crying and speech. The benefit of the difference is that whoever makes it an event forbids touching the Mushaf with it like other events, and whoever makes wudu obligatory as a punishment permits touching the Mushaf with it. Thus the difference and its benefit are transmitted in Mi'raj al-Dirayah. The second should be preferred because it agrees with analogy and is safe from what is said that it is not impurity nor its cause, and it agrees with the hadiths on drowsiness. The statement of the author: 'and intoxication' al-Ramli said: he made intoxication absolute, so it includes intoxication from permissible substances because they say intoxication from permissible things is like unconsciousness. His statement: 'The apparent meaning of the author's statement and a group' etc. And as said in al-Nahr: the apparent meaning of his second statement is evidenced by his saying 'adult', because if it were an event, the adult and other would be equal. His statement: 'the benefit of the difference' etc. Al-Nahr said: and it should also appear in writing the Quran. As for the permissibility of tawaf with this wudu, there is hesitation, and analogizing tawaf to prayer indicates it is not permissible, so reflect. His statement: 'The second should be preferred' etc. He supported it in al-Nahr: it is permissible to touch.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage discusses loud laughter (qahqaha) during prayer and whether it nullifies wudu. It presents two views: the apparent view of the author and many scholars that qahqaha is a hadath (ritual impurity) and thus nullifies wudu, and the view of Abu Zayd al-Dabusi and others that it is not a hadath but wudu becomes obligatory as a punishment. The practical difference is: if it is a hadath, one cannot touch the Quran; if a punishment, one can. The text prefers the second view because it aligns with analogy (qiyas) and is consistent with hadiths on drowsiness. It also notes that the author's word 'adult' (baligh) indicates it is not a hadath, because a child's laughter would also nullify if it were. The passage ends with additional notes on tawaf and touching the Quran.

Key Terms
القهقهة (al-qahqaha): Loud laughter in prayer that is audible to the person and neighbors, whether teeth appear or not.
الحدث (al-hadath): Ritual impurity that nullifies wudu and prohibits acts like touching the Quran and prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a hadath that nullifies wudu.
B) It requires wudu as a punishment but is not a hadath.
C) It requires ghusl.
D) It has no effect on wudu.

Key Takeaway
Loud laughter in prayer obligates wudu, but the preferred Hanafi view treats it as a disciplinary measure, not a ritual impurity, allowing the touching of the Quran after wudu.

Laughter of a Sleeping Person in Prayer – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qahqaha of sleeping person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi Hanafi position on the effect of a sleeping person's laughter during prayer and explain the reasoning behind the three intra-Hanafi views.

Core Idea
Laughter in prayer invalidates both wudu and prayer—even for a sleeping person—as a precaution.

This passage explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: does a sleeping person's laughter (قهقهة) during prayer invalidate wudu and/or prayer? The text traces the evolution of the ruling from an original narration that only commands repetition, through different scholarly views, to the cautious mufta bihi view adopted by the generality of later scholars. Understanding this dispute refines your ability to trace how ikhtilaf within a madhhab leads to a preferred position through tarjih based on caution (ihtiyat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The issue is whether a sleeping person's audible laughter (qahqaha) during prayer invalidates wudu, nullifies prayer, both, or neither.
Laughter is distinguished from smiling (tabassum), which has no effect.
2
State the original narration and its limitation
The original riwayah (narration) only commands repeating wudu and prayer, without explicitly declaring laughter a hadath (legal impurity).
This ambiguity gives rise to the disagreement.
3
List the three intra-Hanafi positions
There are three views: (1) Neither wudu nor prayer is invalidated; (2) Only prayer is nullified but not wudu; (3) Both wudu and prayer are invalidated.
Each view has a basis in early and later Hanafi authorities.
4
Analyze the reasoning behind each position
Position 1: Laughter is a deterrent penalty (zajr), not a hadath, and the sleeping person is not liable for penalties. Position 2: Laughter is like speech without intent, which nullifies prayer (since speech is mufsid), but wudu remains intact because intent is required for hadath. Position 3: Laughter is a hadath occurring in prayer; later scholars adopt this out of caution (ihtiyat).
The reminding nature of prayer (hala mudhakkira) is used to justify invalidation for the forgetful, but not for the sleeping person.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its tarjih rationale
The mufta bihi is that sleeping person's laughter invalidates both wudu and prayer, adopted by the generality of later scholars (عامة المتأخرين) based on caution (احتياطاً).
This view is preferred despite the earlier resistance, because it is the cautious and comprehensive position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنها على ما رووا ليس فيها إلا الأمر بإعادة الوضوء والصلاة ولا يلزم منه كونها من الأحداث ولذا وقع الاختلاف في قهقهة النائم في الصلاة وصححوا في الأصول والفروع أنها لا تنقض الوضوء ولا تبطل الصلاة بناء على أنها إنما أوجبت إعادة الوضوء بطريق الزجر والعقوبة والنائم ليس من أهلها وهذا يرجح ما ذكرناه لكن سوّى فخر الإسلام بين كلام النائم وقهقهته في أن كلاً منها لا يبطل الصلاة، والمذهب أن الكلام مفسد للصلاة كما صرح به في النوازل بأنه المختار فحينئذ تكون القهقهة من النائم مفسدة للصلاة لا الوضوء وهو مختار ابن الهمام في تحريره لأن جعلها حداثاً للجناية ولا جناية من النائم فتبقى كلاماً بلا قصد فيفسد كالساهي به. وفي النصاب وعليه الفتوى وفي الولوالجية وهو المختار وفي المبتغى: تكلم النائم في الصلاة مفسد في الأصح بخلاف القهقهة. ولا يخفى ما فيه فإن القهقهة كلام على ما صرحوا به وفي المعراج أن قهقهة النائم تبطلهما وبه أخذ عامة المتأخرين احتياطاً، وكذا وقع الاختلاف في الناسي كونه في الصلاة فجزم الزيلعي بأنه لا فرق بين الناسي والعامد. وذكر في المعراج أن في الساهي والناسي روايتين، ولعل وجه الرواية القائلة بعدم النقض أنه كالنائم إذ لا جناية إلا بالقصد ولا يخفى ترجيح الرواية القائلة بالنقض لما أن للصلاة حالة مذكرة لا يعذر بالنسيان فيها ألا ترى أن الكلام ناسياً مفسد لها بخلاف النوم، ولا فرق بين كونه متوضئاً أو متيمماً، واتفقوا على أنها لا تبطل الغسل. واختلفوا هل تنقض الوضوء الذي في ضمن الغسل فعلى قول عامة المشايخ لا تنقض وصحح المتأخرون كقاضيخان النقض عقوبة له مع اتفاقهم على بطلان صلاته كما نبه عليه في المضمرات، وفي قهقهة الباني في الطريق بعد الوضوء روايتان كذا في المعراج وجزم الزيلعي بالنقض، قيل وهو الأحوط ولا نزاع في بطلان صلاته.
For it is, according to what they narrated, only the command to repeat the ablution and prayer, and it does not necessarily follow from it that it is among the [minor] hadath (impurities). Hence the disagreement occurred regarding the laughter of a sleeping person during prayer, and they corrected in the principles and branches that it does not invalidate ablution nor nullify prayer, based on the fact that it only necessitated repeating ablution by way of deterrence and punishment, and the sleeping person is not among those subject to it. And this supports what we mentioned. However, Fakhr al-Islam equated the speech of the sleeping person and his laughter in that neither nullifies prayer. The madhhab is that speech invalidates prayer, as stated in al-Nawazil that it is the preferred view. Then the laughter of the sleeping person invalidates prayer, not ablution, and this is the choice of Ibn al-Humam in his Tahrire, because making it a hadath for an offense, and there is no offense from the sleeping person, so it remains as speech without intent, thus it invalidates like that of one who speaks inadvertently. And in al-Nisab, and upon it is the fatwa, and in al-Walwaljiyya it is the preferred view. In al-Mubtagha: The speech of a sleeping person during prayer invalidates according to the most sound view, contrary to laughter. And its implication is not hidden, for laughter is speech as they stated. In al-Mi'raj that the laughter of a sleeping person invalidates both, and the generality of later scholars adopted it as a precaution. Likewise, disagreement occurred regarding the forgetful person being [unaware] that he is in prayer. Al-Zayla'i affirmed that there is no difference between the forgetful and the intentional. It was mentioned in al-Mi'raj that concerning the inadvertent and the forgetful there are two narrations. Perhaps the basis of the narration that says no invalidation is that he is like the sleeping person, since there is no offense except with intent. And the preference for the narration that says invalidation is not hidden, because prayer has a reminding state and one is not excused for forgetfulness in it. Do you not see that speech while forgetful invalidates it, unlike sleep. There is no difference between one who has ablution or one who performs tayammum. They agreed that it does not nullify the ritual bath. They differed whether it invalidates the ablution that is part of the ritual bath. According to the statement of the generality of the mashayikh, it does not invalidate. The later scholars like Qadi Khan corrected that it does invalidate as a punishment, with their agreement on the nullification of his prayer, as pointed out in al-Mudmarat. And regarding the laughter of the builder on the road after ablution, there are two narrations, as in al-Mi'raj. Al-Zayla'i affirmed the invalidation, and it is said that this is the more cautious. There is no dispute on the nullification of his prayer.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of laughter (قهقهة) during prayer, specifically of a sleeping person (نائم). The author begins by referencing a transmitted narration (رووا) that only commands repeating wudu and prayer, and that this does not necessarily mean that laughter is a hadath (impurity). This leads to disagreement about the sleeping person. Some early scholars held that such laughter does not invalidate wudu or nullify prayer because it is a deterrent penalty, and the sleeping person is not liable for such penalties. Fakhr al-Islam equated speech and laughter of the sleeping person, saying neither nullifies prayer. However, the madhhab (as in al-Nawazil) is that speech invalidates prayer. Thus, according to Ibn al-Humam, laughter invalidates prayer but not wudu, because it is considered speech without intent, like one who speaks inadvertently. Several sources—al-Nisab, al-Walwaljiyya, al-Mubtagha—favor this view. Yet, in al-Mi'raj, it is stated that laughter of a sleeping person invalidates both wudu and prayer, and this is adopted by the generality of later scholars (عامة المتأخرين) out of caution (احتياطاً). This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). The passage then discusses the forgetful person (ناسي) who laughs while unaware he is in prayer. Al-Zayla'i affirmed no difference between forgetful and intentional. Al-Mi'raj mentions two narrations: one saying no invalidation (like the sleeping person, no intent), and the other saying invalidation. The author prefers the latter because prayer has a reminding quality and one is not excused for forgetfulness, unlike sleep. The ruling applies equally to one with wudu or tayammum. Laughter does not nullify the ritual bath (غسل), but there is a dispute regarding the wudu within ghusl. Later scholars like Qadi Khan, following caution, hold that it does invalidate that wudu as a punishment. Finally, regarding a person (builder) who laughs after performing wudu on the way to prayer, there are two narrations; al-Zayla'i affirms invalidation, and it is said this is the cautious view. Throughout, the text shows that prayer is nullified (بطلان صلاته) by consensus, but the effect on wudu is disputed. The overall relied-upon position is that laughter during prayer invalidates both wudu and prayer, as adopted by the majority of later scholars.

Key Terms
قهقهة (qahqaha): Laughter with audible voice, specifically in the context of prayer, as opposed to a smile (تبسم).
نائم (na'im): A person who is asleep; the legal status of his actions during prayer is disputed.
ناسي (nasi): A person who forgets he is in prayer; his laughter is treated differently from the sleeping person.
إعادة الوضوء (i'adat al-wudu): Repeating the ablution; commanded as a result of laughter in prayer, either as a penalty (زجر) or because the laughter nullifies the previous wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the effect of a sleeping person's laughter (قهقهة) during prayer on wudu and prayer?
A) It invalidates both wudu and prayer.
B) It invalidates only prayer.
C) It invalidates only wudu.
D) It does not invalidate anything.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position on a sleeping person's laughter in prayer is that it invalidates both wudu and prayer, adopted out of caution by the generality of later scholars.

The Effect of Laughter on Wudu: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether loud laughter breaks wudu for a rider in voluntary prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ on whether loud laughter breaks wudu for a rider in voluntary prayer, and identify the underlying condition that determines the ruling.

Core Idea
Laughter breaks wudu only within a valid prayer; an invalid prayer renders laughter inconsequential.

This lesson explores a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of loud laughter (qahqaha) during prayer on the validity of wudu. The scenario involves an imam performing voluntary prayer by gestures while riding in a city – a practice not permitted according to Abu Hanifa but considered valid by Abu Yusuf. The disagreement hinges on whether the prayer itself is valid, which determines whether laughter nullifies wudu. We will also examine a related case involving forgetfulness of wudu components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two positions
The passage presents an imam riding and performing voluntary prayer by gestures in a city/village. Abu Hanifa says laughter does not break wudu; Abu Yusuf says it does.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa considers the prayer invalid from the start because gesturing for voluntary prayer is not allowed in a settled area. Since there is no valid prayer, the laughter occurs outside a valid act of worship and thus does not affect wudu.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf holds the prayer valid, so the laughter occurs within a valid prayer and therefore breaks wudu, as per the general rule that qahqaha nullifies wudu.
4
Analyze the underlying condition
The key condition is the validity of the prayer. If the prayer is invalid, laughter has no effect; if valid, it breaks wudu. The disagreement thus reduces to whether the specific act of prayer is valid.
5
Apply to the second scenario
The passage also mentions a person who forgets wiping (masah) and laughs. If he laughs before standing for prayer, the prayer has not yet begun in a valid manner, so laughter breaks wudu. If he laughs after standing, the prayer is invalid due to the missing wipe, so laughter does not break wudu. This reinforces the principle: laughter's effect depends on the presence of a valid prayer.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدث أنه الإمام راكباً يومىء بالتطوّع في المصر أو القرية فقهقه لا ينتقض وضوءه لعدم جواز صلاته عند أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف : ينتقض لصحة صلاته عنده . ولو نسي الباني المسح فقهقه قبل القيام إلى الصلاة نقض وبعده لا ينقض لبطلان الصلاة بالقيام إليه وهو من مسائل الامتحان، كذا في المعراج.
One said that if the imam, riding and gesturing for voluntary prayer in the city or village, laughs loudly, his wudu is not broken due to the impermissibility of his prayer according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: his wudu is broken because his prayer is valid according to him. And if the builder forgets the wiping and laughs loudly before standing for prayer, it breaks; after standing it does not break due to the invalidity of the prayer by standing. This is among the test questions, as in al-Mi'raj.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of loud laughter (qahqaha) in prayer on the validity of wudu. The specific scenario: an imam riding an animal and performing voluntary prayer (nafl) by gestures (ima'), as permitted for travelers, but here in a city or village—a context where such prayer is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Hanifa rules that the laughter does not break wudu because the prayer itself is invalid from the start, so laughter within an invalid act has no effect. Abu Yusuf, on the other hand, considers the prayer valid, so the laughter occurs within a valid prayer and thus breaks wudu. The text then gives a second scenario: a person (here called al-bani, possibly meaning the one performing prayer) who forgets to wipe (masah) part of his wudu, then laughs loudly. If the laughter occurs before standing for prayer, it breaks wudu; if after standing, it does not, because standing itself invalidates the prayer (due to the missing wipe). This is noted as a test question. The passage demonstrates how the underlying validity of the prayer determines the ruling on qahqaha.

Key Terms
القهقهة (al-qahqaha): Loud laughter audible to others within prayer, which has specific rulings regarding nullification of wudu and prayer.
الباني (al-bani): Probably the person performing the prayer; the term is used in this passage to refer to the one who forgets the masah and then laughs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if an imam rides an animal and gestures for voluntary prayer in a city, then laughs loudly, what is the ruling on his wudu?
A) It is broken because laughter always breaks wudu.
B) It is not broken because his prayer is not valid.
C) It is broken only if he laughs after standing.
D) It is not broken but his prayer is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The effect of loud laughter on wudu hinges on whether the prayer is valid; if the prayer is invalid from the start, laughter does not break wudu.

The Ruling of Qahqaha in Salah: Hanafi Position and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Explains the Hanafi ruling on qahqaha in salah, its definition, and the inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qahqaha in salah, explain the distinction between qahqaha, dahk, and tabassum, and evaluate the inter-madhhab dispute regarding its effect on wudu.

Core Idea
Qahqaha in salah nullifies both wudu and salah in the Hanafi school, based on a specific hadith that overrides qiyas.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position on loud laughter (qahqaha) during prayer. It distinguishes qahqaha from ordinary laughter and smiling, addresses the case of a child, and engages with the opposing view of the three other imams who rely on qiyas. Understanding this ruling requires grappling with the interplay of hadith and analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what qahqaha is and what the Hanafi school says about its effect on wudu and salah.
Focus on the definition: qahqaha is audible laughter.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
Which hadith is cited? How does it prove the ruling? Trace the wajh al-dalalah.
The Prophet ordered those who laughed to repeat wudu and prayer.
3
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
What do the three imams hold? What is their dalil? Identify the opposing proof (qiyas).
Their position: no nullification of wudu, by analogy to outside prayer.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the qiyas? Why is the hadith given priority?
Qiyas is abandoned because the hadith is a direct textual command (dalil naqli).
5
Apply the definitions
Distinguish qahqaha, dahk, and tabassum. What are the rulings for each?
Only qahqaha nullifies wudu; dahk invalidates salah; tabassum has no effect.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالبلوغ لأن قهقهة الصبي لا تنقض وضوءه لكن تبطل صلاته، كذا في كثير من الكتب. ونقل في السراج الوهاج الإجماع على عدم نقض وضوئه وفيه نظر، فقد ذكر في معراج الدراية أن في المسألة ثلاثة أقوال الأوّل ما ذكرناه الثاني عن نجم الأئمة البخاري سلمة بن شداد أنها تنقض الوضوء دون الصلاة. الثالث عن أبي القاسم أنها تبطلهما إلا أن يقال : لما كان القولان الأخيران ضعيفين كانا كالعدم ووجه الأوّل أنها إنما أوجبت إعادة الوضوء عقوبة وزجراً والصبي ليس من أهلها والأثر ورد في صلاة كاملة فيقتصر عليها فلا تتعدى إلى صلاة الجنازة وسجدة التلاوة وصلاة الصبي وصلاة الباني بعد الوضوء على إحدى الروايتين، وصلاة النائم على أحد القولين وهذا كله مذهبنا وقالت الأئمة الثلاثة : لا تنقض أصلاً قياساً على عدم نقضها خارج الصلاة، ولنا أن القياس ذلك لكن تركناه فيما إذا كانت القهقهة في ذات ركوع وسجود بما ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مرسلاً ومسنداً: بينما رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يصلي بالناس إذ دخل رجل فتردى في حفرة وكان في بصره ضرر فضحك كثير من القوم وهو في الصلاة فأمر رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من ضحك أن يعيد الوضوء والصلاة. وتمامه في فتح القدير : وما قيل بأنه لا يظن الضحك بالصحابة خلفه قهقهة، أجيب عنه ، بأنه كان يصلي خلفه الصحابيون والمنافقون والأعراب الجهال فالضاحك لعله كان بعض الأحداث أو المنافقين أو بعض الأعراب لغلبة الجهل عليهم كما بال اعرابي في مسجد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو نظير قوله تعالى وتركوك قائماً ﴾ [الجمعة : ۱۱] فإنه لم يتركه كبار الصحابة باللهو. قال في العناية : وهذا من باب حسن الظن بهم رضي الله عنهم وإلا فليس الضحك كبيرة وهم ليسوا من الصغائر بمعصومين ولا عن الكبائر على تقدير كونه كبيرة ا اه. في الأصول أن الصحابة عدول فهم محفوظون من المعاصي. وقيد بالقهقهة لأن الضحك بفتح الضاد وكسر الحاء هذا أصله ويجوز إسكان الحاء مع فتح الضاد وكسرها فهي أربعة أوجه، كذا في شرح المهذب، وهو في اللغة أعم من القهقهة وهي من أفراده. وفي الاصطلاح ما مسموعاً له فقط وحكمه أنه لا ينقض الوضوء بل يبطل الصلاة، وأما التبسم وهو ما لا صوت فيه أصلاً بأن تبدو أسنانه فقط فحكمه أنه لا يبطلهما لأنه لا تبسم في الصلاة حين أناة جبريل عليه السلام وأخبره أن من صلى عليك مرة صلى الله عليه بها عشراً كما البدائع. وقال جابر بن عبد الله : ما رآني رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إلا تبسم ولو في الصلاة كما في النهاية والعناية وظاهر كلامهم أن التبسم في الصلاة غير مكروه ولذا قال في الاختيار : ولا حكم للتبسم : وقد رأيت في كلام بعضهم أنه لو أتى بحرفين من القهقهة انتقض وضوءه عملاً بعدم تبعيض الحدث لأنه إذا وقع بعضه وقع كله قياساً لوقوعه على ارتفاعة بجامع أن كلا منهما لا يتبعض ا هـ . وقد يقال إن الحكم وهو النقض معلق بالقهقه فإذا وجد بعضها لا يوجد الحكم ولا بعضه لما عرف في الأصول أن المشروط لا يتوزع على أجزاء الشرط فقوله «لأنه إذا وقع بعضه» ممنوع كما لا يخفى .
And it is restricted by puberty because the qahqaha of a child does not nullify his wudu but invalidates his prayer, as stated in many books. And it is reported in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that there is consensus on not nullifying his wudu, but there is consideration; it has been mentioned in Mir‘at al-Dirayah that there are three opinions: first what we mentioned, second from Najm al-A’immah al-Bukhari Salamah ibn Shaddad that it nullifies wudu but not prayer, third from Abu al-Qasim that it nullifies both. But it may be said: Since the last two opinions are weak, they are like non-existent. The justification of the first is that it only necessitated repeating wudu as a punishment and deterrent, and the child is not subject to it. And the athar came regarding a complete prayer, so it is restricted to it and does not extend to funeral prayer, prostration of recitation, the prayer of a child, the prayer of a builder after wudu according to one of two narrations, and the prayer of a sleeping person according to one of two sayings. This is all our madhhab. And the three imams said: It does not nullify at all, by analogy to its not nullifying outside prayer. We have that analogy, but we abandoned it when qahqaha occurs in an act containing bowing and prostration, because of what is established from the Messenger of Allah [ﷺ] as both mursal and musnad: “While the Messenger of Allah [ﷺ] was leading the people in prayer, a man entered and fell into a hole, and there was a defect in his sight; many people laughed while in prayer, so the Messenger of Allah [ﷺ] ordered those who laughed to repeat their wudu and prayer.” And the rest is in Fath al-Qadir. And what was said that it is unlikely that the companions behind him laughed qahqaha is answered by saying that there were among those praying behind him companions, hypocrites, and ignorant Bedouins; so the laugher might have been some of the young ones, hypocrites, or some Bedouins because of their overwhelming ignorance, just as a Bedouin urinated in the mosque of the Prophet [ﷺ]. And it is analogous to His statement “and they leave you standing” (62:11), for the senior companions did not leave him for amusement. Al-Inayah said: This is a case of good assumption about them, may Allah be pleased with them; otherwise laughter is not a major sin, and they are not infallible from minor sins nor from major ones assuming it is major. In usul, the companions are upright, so they are preserved from sins. And it is restricted by qahqaha because ad-dahk (laughter) with fatḥ al-dad and kasr al-ḥa is its origin; it is permissible to sukun the ha with fatah of the dad and its kasr; thus there are four forms, as in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab. In language, it is more general than qahqaha, and qahqaha is one of its species. In terminology, it is only that which is audible. Its ruling is that it does not nullify wudu but invalidates prayer. As for tabassum (smiling), which is that which has no sound at all, such that only the teeth appear, its ruling is that it does not invalidate either, because there is no tabassum in prayer when Jibril (peace be upon him) was gentle and informed him that whoever prays upon you once, Allah prays upon him ten times, as in al-Bada’i. And Jabir ibn Abdullah said: The Messenger of Allah [ﷺ] never saw me except that he smiled, even in prayer, as in al-Nihayah and al-Inayah. And the apparent meaning of their statements is that tabassum in prayer is not disliked. Hence al-Ikhtiyar said: There is no ruling for tabassum. And I have seen in the speech of some that if he produced two letters of qahqaha his wudu is nullified, acting upon the non-divisibility of the hadath, because if part of it occurs, all of it occurs, by analogy of its occurrence to its removal, based on the common point that neither is divisible. It may be said that the ruling (nullification) is attached to qahqaha; if part of it occurs, the ruling does not occur nor does part of it, because it is known in usul that the conditional does not distribute over parts of the condition; so his statement “because if part of it occurs” is refutable, as is evident.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute detailing the Hanafi ruling on qahqaha (loud laughter) in salah and its effect on wudu and salah. The author begins by addressing the case of a child's laughter: the child's qahqaha does not nullify his wudu (due to lack of accountability) but does invalidate his salah, which is the relied-upon position. He notes three opinions on whether a child's qahqaha nullifies wudu, but considers the last two weak. The Hanafi position is that qahqaha in a prayer that includes ruku' and sujud nullifies both wudu and salah, based on a hadith where the Prophet ordered those who laughed to repeat wudu and prayer. The three other imams (Shafi‘i, Maliki, Hanbali) argue that qahqaha does not nullify wudu at all, by qiyas to laughter outside prayer. The Hanafi response is that qiyas is abandoned due to the hadith. The author then discusses the definition: qahqaha is audible laughter; ordinary laughter (dahk) in general language includes qahqaha, but in fiqh terminology, only audible laughter (qahqaha) nullifies wudu, while ordinary laughter (without sound) only invalidates salah. Tabassum (smiling without sound) does not nullify wudu nor invalidate salah. The author also addresses a nuance: if a person produces two letters of qahqaha, some say it nullifies wudu due to the indivisibility of hadath, but the author refutes this by noting that the ruling is attached to the complete qahqaha, not to parts, based on usul principles.

Key Terms
القهقهة (al-qahqaha): Loud, audible laughter, distinguished from dahk (ordinary laughter) and tabassum (smiling). It is the specific type of laughter that, when occurring in salah (a prayer with ruku' and sujud), nullifies both wudu and salah according to the Hanafi school.
التبسم (al-tabassum): Smiling without any audible sound; the teeth may appear. It has no legal effect on wudu or salah according to the Hanafi school.
الضحك (al-dahk): Laughter in general. In this passage, it is broader than qahqaha. Linguistically, it includes audible and silent laughter. In fiqh terminology, the specific term 'qahqaha' is used for the laughter that nullifies wudu; ordinary 'dahk' (without sound) only invalidates salah if it occurs in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of qahqaha (loud laughter) during a prayer that includes ruku' and sujud?
A) It nullifies wudu only.
B) It invalidates salah only.
C) It nullifies both wudu and salah.
D) It has no effect.

Key Takeaway
Qahqaha nullifies wudu and salah in Hanafi fiqh; dahk only invalidates salah; tabassum has no effect; the dispute with other schools hinges on the priority of hadith over qiyas.
