(2) رواه مسلم في كتاب الفطرة حديث ٥٦ أبو داود في كتاب الطهارة باب ٢٩. الترمذي في كتاب الأدب باب ١٤. النسائي في كتاب الزينة باب .. أحمد في مسنده (١٣٧/٦).
(3) رواه البخاري في كتاب الجنائز باب ٩٢ أبو داود في كتاب السنة باب ١٧. الترمذي في كتاب القدر باب ٥. الموطأ في كتاب الجنائز حديث ٥٢ أحمد في مسنده (٢٣٣/٢، ٢٧٥).
From the Book of Purification. His saying: 'The obligatory act of ghusl is washing his mouth, nose, and body' — there is a prior explanation of the reason for preferring wudu over ghusl. The 'waw' in 'wa-fard' is either for resumption or for conjunction with his saying 'fard al-wudu'. 'Al-fard' is a verbal noun meaning 'the obligatory', because the verbal noun is used to indicate time, place, agent, or object, as in al-Kashshaf. His saying 'al-ghusl' means ghusl for janabah, hayd, and nifas, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Its apparent meaning is that madmadah and istinshaq are not conditions for the sunnah ghusl such that it would be invalid without them. Then know that discourse on ghusl occurs in places: in its linguistic and legal interpretation, its cause, pillar, conditions, sunan, etiquettes, description, and ruling. As for its linguistic interpretation, it is, with a dammah, a noun from 'ightisal', meaning complete washing of the body, and also a name for the water used for washing, as in the hadith of Maymunah: 'She placed for him ghusl' — as in al-Mughrib. Al-Nawawi said: 'It has two pronunciations: with fatha on the ghayn and with dammah; fatha is more eloquent and famous among linguists, while dammah is used by the jurists or most of them.' Terminologically, it is the first linguistic meaning, which is washing the body. Its pillar is causing water to flow over every part of the body that can possibly have water flow on it without hardship, once, such that if a spot remains untouched by water, the ghusl is not valid, even if it is small, because of Allah's saying: 'And if you are in a state of janabah, purify yourselves' [4:43]. Allah Almighty commanded with 'at'tahharu' with a dammah on the ha, because its origin is 'tatahhara'; the ta was assimilated into the ta due to proximity of articulation, so a connecting hamza was introduced to allow pronunciation, becoming 'ittahharu'. Some ignorant people read it as 'at'tahharu', which is only due to their lack of Arabic. This is from Ghayat al-Bayan. It is purification of the entire body. The term 'body' applies to the external and internal, except that what is impossible to reach with water is excluded from the scope of the text, as is what is extremely difficult, because hardship is negated like impossibility, such as the interior of the eyes, for washing them involves obvious hardship, since the eye is fat that does not accept water. Those who have eyesight have refrained from undertaking this, like Ibn 'Umar and Ibn 'Abbas, and for this reason the eye is not washed if it is lined with impure kohl. For this reason, madmadah and istinshaq are obligatory in ghusl because there is no hardship in washing them, so they are included in the text of the Book without opposition, just as his saying 'Under every hair is janabah, so wet the hair and clean the skin' (1) includes them, reported by al-Tirmidhi without opposition. 'Al-basharah' is the outer skin. This differs from wudu, because the obligatory act in wudu is washing the face, and the interior of the mouth and nose are not faced. As for his saying 'Ten are from the fitrah', mentioning madmadah and istinshaq (2), it does not oppose, because being from the fitrah does not negate obligation, as fitrah is religion, which is more general. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Every child is born upon the fitrah' (3), meaning the highest of obligations according to the strongest opinion. On this basis, there is no need to interpret the narrated hadith as related to the state of hadath, due to his saying: 'They are two fards in janabah and two sunnahs in wudu.' As for Abu Hurayrah that he made madmadah and istinshaq for the junub three times as a fardah, but he meant... It is as if consensus has been established on the exclusion of two, but that is weak, as in Fath al-Qadir. The highest of obligations is Islam. But Abu Nasr al-Dabusi, as narrated by al-Hawi al-Hasiri, said: 'It is not correct that what is meant is that it is a verbal noun from the form taf'il, originally tatahhara, the ta was changed to ta then assimilated, then a connecting hamza was introduced for pronunciation.' His saying: 'And the term body applies to the external and internal, etc.' Shaykh 'Ala' al-Din al-Haskafi narrated from al-Mughrib and others that the body is from the shoulder to the buttock. He said: 'Then the head, neck, hand, and foot are linguistically excluded, but included legally as followers.' His saying: 'without opposition' is attached to his second saying 'they are included'. His saying: 'It is as if from Abu Hurayrah, etc.' Apparently, the agent of 'mean' is a pronoun referring to the person who understood the word from the source in his saying 'there is no need to interpret the narrated hadith'. The meaning: It is as if the person who understood intended by the hadith used as evidence what was narrated from Abu Hurayrah (may Allah be pleased with him) to reflect upon his saying: 'The correct view is that they are not a condition.' It would be better to use masculine pronouns because... (footnotes)
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling of the obligatory acts of ghusl as stated in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author begins by affirming that the obligatory acts (fard) of ghusl are washing the mouth, nose, and entire body. He then clarifies that this is a statement of the ruling, and the 'waw' in 'wa-fard' could be either for resumption or conjunction with the earlier discussion of wudu's fard. He defines 'fard' as the obligatory act itself. The text specifies that ghusl refers to washing for janabah, hayd, and nifas. Importantly, the author notes that the apparent meaning of the base text (Kanz) is that madmadah (rinsing mouth) and istinshaq (sniffing water into nose) are not conditions for the sunnah ghusl, meaning that if they are omitted, the ghusl is still valid though lacking sunnah. However, the commentator then proceeds to prove they are actually obligatory (fard) in ghusl, not just sunnah. He then divides the discussion of ghusl into topics: linguistic and legal definition, cause, pillar, conditions, sunan, etiquettes, description, and ruling. For the linguistic definition, it is a noun from ightisal meaning complete washing of the body. The pillar (rukn) is causing water to flow over every possible part of the body without hardship, once. If any spot remains dry, ghusl is invalid, based on Quran 4:43: 'And if you are in a state of janabah, purify yourselves.' The author explains the linguistic form of 'it'tahharu' (with tashdid and dammah) as derived from tatahhara with assimilation and a connecting hamza. He then states that the Quranic command includes the entire body, but excludes areas that are impossible or extremely difficult to wash, like the interior of the eyes. This exclusion is based on the principle that hardship negates the obligation. Since washing the mouth and nose is not hardship, they are included in the command. He supports this with the hadith: 'Under every hair is janabah, so wet the hair and clean the skin' (reported by al-Tirmidhi). The term 'basharah' means the outer skin. He contrasts this with wudu, where the mouth and nose are not obligatory because the obligation is to wash the face, and the interior is not part of the face in the legal sense. Addressing an objection: even though madmadah and istinshaq are listed among the acts of fitrah (innate nature), this does not negate obligation, as fitrah here refers to the religion of Islam, which includes obligatory acts. The commentator quotes the hadith 'Every child is born upon the fitrah' to show that fitrah can encompass obligations. He then cites another hadith explicitly stating that these two acts are 'two fards in janabah and two sunnahs in wudu'. The reference to Abu Hurayrah is noted as weak. This establishes that the Hanafi position is that madmadah and istinshaq are obligatory (fard) in ghusl. The passage concludes with a discussion on the definition of 'body' according to scholars like al-Haskafi, who states that linguistically the body is from shoulder to buttock, but legally the head, neck, hands, and feet are included by way of following (taba`an).

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): Here, ghusl means the ritual bath for major impurity (janabah, hayd, nifas). It is defined linguistically as the complete washing of the body, and terminologically as the same.
المضمضة (al-madmadah): Rinsing the mouth with water. In this passage, it is discussed as an obligatory part of ghusl and a sunnah part of wudu.
الاستنشاق (al-istinshaq): Sniffing water into the nose and then expelling it. Same ruling as madmadah in this passage.
الفرض (al-fard): The obligatory act. In this context, it refers to what must be performed in ghusl for it to be valid.
البدن (al-badan): The body. The passage notes that linguistically it may refer to the torso from shoulder to buttock, but legally it includes the head, neck, hands, and feet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling of madmadah (rinsing the mouth) in ghusl for janabah?
A) It is sunnah, and the ghusl is valid without it.
B) It is fard, and the ghusl is invalid if omitted intentionally.
C) It is wajib, and the ghusl is valid but requires compensation.
D) It is mustahabb, and the ghusl is valid but deficient.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, madmadah and istinshaq are obligatory (fard) in ghusl because the Quran and explicit hadith command complete purification without hardship, and the fitrah argument does not negate obligation.

intermediate

Wujub of Madmada and Istinshaq in Ghusl
Establishes obligation with proofs and refutes opposing view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on madmada and istinshaq in ghusl, identify the evidence from the Quran and hadith, and explain why the fitra hadith does not negate obligation.

Core Idea
Madmada and istinshaq are wajib in ghusl because they are part of the body's exterior washed without hardship; the fitra hadith does not contradict.

This lesson focuses on a key point of difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: the obligation of rinsing the mouth and nose during ghusl. The Hanafi school considers these acts obligatory (wajib) based on the general command to wash the entire body and a specific hadith. The Shafi'i school views them as recommended (sunnah) based on the hadith on acts of fitra. We will examine the Hanafi proofs and their refutation of the opposing argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
Note that the Hanafi school rules madmada and istinshaq as wajib in ghusl, but only sunnah in wudu. This distinction is based on the scope of the command: ghusl requires washing the whole body, while wudu only requires washing the face.
The ruling in ghusl is wajib, not fard, because the proof is from ahad hadith.
2
Locate the primary evidence
The author cites the Quranic command to purify the whole body and the hadith 'Under every hair is janabah, so wet the hair and clean the skin.' The imperative 'clean' (anqoo) indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
The hadith is from Tirmidhi and has zanni thubut.
3
Analyze the scope of the body
The mouth and nose are part of the body's exterior that can be washed without hardship, unlike the interior of the eyes. Therefore, they are included in the general command.
This contrasts with wudu where they are not wajib because they are not part of the face that is 'faced'.
4
Examine the opposing proof
The opposing argument uses the hadith listing madmada and istinshaq among ten acts of fitra, implying they are sunnah. The Hanafi response: fitra here means the religion of Islam, which includes both wajib and mandub acts.
The Prophet's statement 'Every child is born upon fitra' is interpreted as Islam, the highest of obligations.
5
Confirm the tarjih
The author dismisses a weak narration from Abu Hurairah and upholds the position that madmada and istinshaq are wajib in ghusl. The practical consequence is that omitting them requires repeating the bath.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text

أمر الله سبحانه وتعالى بالأطهر بضم الهاء لأن أصله تطهر فادغمت التاء في الطاء لقرب المخرج فجيء بحرف الوصل ليتوصل بها إلى النطق فصار اطهروا وبعض من لا خبرة له ولا دراية يقرأ بالأطهار وما ذاك إلا لحرمانه من العربية كذا في غاية البيان. وهو تطهير جميع البدن واسم البدن يقع على الظاهر والباطن إلا أن ما يتعذر إيصال الماء إليه خارج عن قضية النص، وكذا ما يتعسر لأن المتعسر منفي كالمتعذر كداخل العينين فإن في غسلهما من الحرج ما لا يخفى فإن العين شحم لا تقبل الماء، وقد كف تكلف له بصر من كابن عمر وابن عباس ولهذا لا تغسل العين إذا اكتحل بكحل نجس، ولهذا وجبت المضمضة والاستنشاق في الغسل لأنه لا حرج في غسلهما فشملهما نص الكتاب من غير معارض كما شملهما قوله لا تحت كل شعرة جنابة فبلوا الشعر وانقوا البشر رواه الترمذي من غير معارض. والبشرة ظاهر الجلد بخلافهما في الوضوء لأن الواجب فيه غسل الوجه ولا تقع المواجهة بداخلهما، وأما قوله له عشر من الفطرة وذكر منها المضمضة والاستنشاق لا يعارضه إذ كونهما من الفطرة لا ينفي الوجوب لأنها الدين وهو أعم منه قال صلى الله عليه وسلم «كل مولود يولد على الفطرة» والمراد أعلى الواجبات على ما هو أعلى الأقوال، وهو على هذا فلا حاجة إلى حمل المروي على حالة الحدث بدليل قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «إنهما فرضان في الجناية سنتان في الوضوء» ما عن أبي هريرة أنه جعل المضمضة والاستنشاق للجنس ثلاثاً فريضة لكن يعني
كأنه

The Book of Taharah. From the companions: Allah Most High commanded 'at-thahhara' with damm on the ha, because its origin is tathahhara, so the ta was assimilated into the ta due to proximity of articulation, so a vowel of connection was brought to enable pronunciation, thus it became 'ittahharu'. And some who have no expertise or knowledge read 'at-tahhara' – and that is only due to their deprivation of Arabic, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. It is the purification of the entire body, and the name 'body' applies to the apparent and the hidden, except that what is impossible to deliver water to is excluded from the requirement of the text, and likewise what is extremely difficult, because that which is extremely difficult is negated like the impossible, such as the inside of the eyes, for washing them involves obvious hardship, for the eye is a fatty substance that does not accept water, and the sight of even Ibn 'Umar and Ibn 'Abbas refrained from burdening it, and therefore the eye is not washed if one applies kohl with impure kohl. And for this reason, madmada and istinshaq are obligatory in ghusl, because there is no hardship in washing them, so they are covered by the text of the Book without any contradictory evidence, just as they are covered by his statement: 'Under every hair is janabah, so wet the hair and clean the skin' narrated by al-Tirmidhi without any contradictory. And 'al-basharah' is the apparent skin, unlike them in wudu, because what is obligatory in it is washing the face, and the inside of the mouth and nose are not faced (in the face). As for his statement 'He has ten from al-fitrah' and he mentioned among them madmada and istinshaq, it does not contradict, because their being from al-fitrah does not negate obligation, since fitrah is the religion, which is more general than it. The Prophet said: 'Every child is born upon al-fitrah' and what is intended is the highest of obligations according to the highest of statements. And upon this, there is no need to carry the narrated hadith to the state of hadath (minor impurity) due to the Prophet's statement: 'Both are two obligations in janabah and two sunnahs in wudu.' As for what is narrated from Abu Hurairah that he made madmada and istinshaq for the junub three times a fard, but 'he meant' as if consensus was reached on excluding two of them, and it is weak, as in Fath al-Qadir. And what is meant by the highest of obligations is Islam, but Abu Nasr al-Dabusi said as narrated from him by al-Hawi al-Husri: It is not valid that 'they mean' – the source word 'tahara' from the taf'il form, its origin is 'tathahhara', the ta was turned into ta then assimilated, then a hamzat wasl was brought for articulation of the saakin. His statement: ('For the two of them' in the second) his statement: ('It is as if' about Abu Hurairah etc.) It appears that the doer of 'ya'ni' is a pronoun referring to the bearer understood from the verbal noun in his statement 'So there is no need to carry the narrated hadith' and the meaning is: it is as if the bearer intended by the hadith that he used as proof what was narrated from Abu Hurairah May Allah be pleased with him. Consider his statement: 'And the correct view is that it (maj/muj) is not a condition' – it would be better to make both pronouns masculine because they refer to the male. It is said that the child is born upon Islam because one who is judged to be Muslim once is never transferred to another religion nor is it accepted from him, but the meaning is that he is born upon a disposition capable of Islam such that if he looks at his creation and reflects upon it as is necessary, it will guide him to His Lordship and Oneness.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the obligation of madmada (rinsing the mouth) and istinshaq (sniffing water into the nose) in ghusl (ritual bath). The author, al-Marghinani in his commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, begins by explaining the linguistic form of the divine command 'at-thahhara' (purify yourselves). He notes that it is derived from 'tathahhara' (to purify) and the correct reading is with damm on the ha, as some mistakenly read 'at-tahhara'. Then he defines the scope of the command: washing the entire body, both apparent and hidden, except what is impossible or extremely difficult to wash, like the inside of the eyes, as washing them would cause hardship. He then establishes the obligation of madmada and istinshaq in ghusl, because there is no hardship in washing them, and they are included under the general command to wash the body, reinforced by the hadith: 'Under every hair is janabah, so wet the hair and clean the skin' (Sunan al-Tirmidhi). The author distinguishes ghusl from wudu: in wudu, madmada and istinshaq are not wajib because the obligation is washing the face, and the interior of the mouth and nose are not part of the face in terms of facing. The passage then addresses a potential objection: a hadith lists madmada and istinshaq among ten acts of fitra (primordial nature), which some use to argue they are sunnah, not wajib. The Hanafi response is that fitra here means the religion (Islam), which includes wajib acts, so being from fitra does not negate obligation. This is supported by the general hadith 'Every child is born upon al-fitrah', which is interpreted as Islam. Thus, the obligation stands. The author dismisses as weak the narration from Abu Hurairah that he considered madmada and istinshaq three times as fard for the junub. The passage concludes with a discussion of the meaning of 'the highest of obligations' as Islam, and a note from Abu Nasr al-Dabusi. The commentary also includes a linguistic note about the root word. The overall aim is to prove that madmada and istinshaq are wajib in ghusl, against those who consider them merely sunnah.

Key Terms
المضمضة (al-madmada): Rinsing the mouth with water, specifically in the context of ritual purification (wudu and ghusl).
الاستنشاق (al-istinshaq): Sniffing water into the nose and then expelling it, as part of ritual purification.
الفطرة (al-fitrah): Primordial nature or natural disposition, often referring to the acts of fitra (like circumcision, trimming nails, etc.). In this context, it is interpreted as the religion (Islam) by the Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because they are listed among the ten acts of fitra, which are all obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Madmada and istinshaq are wajib in ghusl because the command to wash the entire body includes them, and the fitra hadith does not negate this obligation.

Madmada in Ghusl: Drinking Water vs. Gargling
Ruling on madmada via drinking water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether drinking water by gulping suffices for madmada in ghusl, identify the ikhtilaf with Abu Yusuf, and explain the precautionary recommendation of gargling.

Core Idea
Gulping water suffices for madmada; gargling is precautionary, not required.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author addresses a practical scenario: a person drinks water during ghusl. Does this action fulfill the obligation of madmada (rinsing the mouth)? He clarifies the correct view and the precautionary approach, with a mention of an opinion from Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being evaluated: drinking water during ghusl. Then classify the ruling: suffices for madmada if done by gulping (عباً), not by sucking (مصاً).
2
Note the ikhtilaf with Abu Yusuf
Recognize that Abu Yusuf held a different view: drinking does not suffice at all. This is mentioned but the preferred view is that gulping suffices.
The text quotes al-Waqi'at stating that gargling is required to exit janabah, but this is precautionary.
3
Understand the concept of احتياط (precaution)
The author explains that it is more precautionary to gargle (المج) because some scholars considered it a condition, though the correct view is that it is not. Precaution means acting upon the stronger proof.
Here, the stronger proof is the correct view that gargling is not a condition, but precaution suggests doing it to be certain.
4
Apply the mufta bihi
The operative ruling (mufta bihi) is that drinking by gulping suffices without gargling. However, to be cautious, one may gargle.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of ihram (precaution) without contradicting the established ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شرب الماء عباً أجزأه عن المضمضة لا مصاً. وعن أبي يوسف لا إلا أن وفي الواقعات: لا يخرج بالشرب على وجه السنة أو غيره ما لم يمجه وهو أحوط كذا في الخلاصة. وقد يقال : إن الأحوط الخروج. ووجه كونه أحوط أنه قيل إن المج من شرط المضمضة والصحيح أنها ليست بشرط فكان الاحتياط الخروج عن الجنابة لأن الاحتياط العمل بأقوى الدليلين وأقواهما هنا الخروج بناء على الصحيح كما لا يخفى. ولو كان سنه مجوفاً أو طعام أو درن رطب يجزيه لأن الماء لطيف يصل إلى كل موضع غالباً كذا في التجنيس ثم قال : ذكر الصدر الشهيد حسام الدين في موضع آخر إذا كان في أسنانه كوات يبقى فيها الطعام لا يجزيه ما لم يخرجه ويجري الماء عليها. وفي فتاوى الفضلي والفقيه أبي الليث خلاف هذا فالاحتياط أن يفعل اهـ. وفي معراج الدراية : الأصح أنه يجزيه والدرن اليابس في الأنف كالخبز الممضوغ والعجين يمنع تمام الاغتسال وكذا جلد السمك والوسخ والدرن لا يمنع، والتراب والطين في الظفر لا يمنع لأن الماء ينفذ فيه، وما على ظفر الصباغ يمنع، وقيل لا يمنع للضرورة. قال في المضمرات وعليه الفتوى. والصحيح أنه لا فرق بين القروي والمدني ا هـ . ولو بقي على جسده خرء برغوث أو ونيم ذباب أي ذرقه لم يصل الماء تحته جازت طهارته. ويجب تحريك القرط الخاتم الضيقين ولو لم يكن قرط فدخل الماء الثقب عند مروره أجزأه كالسرة وإلا أدخله كذا في فتح القدير ولا يتكلف في إدخال شيء من خشب ونحوه كذا في شرح الوقاية، ويدخل القلفة استحباباً على ما نبينه
And if he drinks water by gulping (soaking) it suffices him instead of madmada, but not by sucking. And from Abu Yusuf: 'No, unless...' And in al-Waqi'at: 'He does not exit (from janabah) by drinking in the manner of the sunnah or otherwise unless he gargles (yumajjuhu), and that is more precautionary' as in al-Khulasa. And it may be said that the precautionary is to exit (i.e., gargle). And the reason it is more precautionary is that it has been said that gargling (al-maj) is a condition of madmada, and the correct view is that it is not a condition, so the precautionary is to exit from janabah, because precautionary means acting upon the stronger of the two proofs, and the stronger here is exit based on the correct view, as is not hidden. And if his tooth is hollow, or there is food or moist dirt (inside), it suffices him, because water is subtle and reaches every place usually, as in al-Tajnish. Then he said: Sharif Sadr al-Shahid Husam al-Din mentioned in another place: if there are cavities in his teeth in which food remains, it does not suffice him unless he removes it and runs water over them. And in the Fatawa of al-Fadli and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, contrary to this, so the precautionary is to do (i.e., to remove). End. And in Mir'aj al-Dirayah: the most correct is that it suffices him. And dry dirt in the nose, like chewed bread and dough, prevents complete ghusl. Likewise, fish skin, and filth and dirt do not prevent. And earth and clay on the nail do not prevent because water penetrates through it. And what is on the dyer's nail prevents, and it is said it does not prevent due to necessity. He said in al-Mudmarat: and upon it is the fatwa. And the correct view is that there is no difference between the villager and the city dweller. End. And if there remains on his body the excrement of a flea or the droppings of a fly, i.e., its excrement, and water does not reach under it, his purification is valid. And it is obligatory to move the earring and the tight ring. And if there is no earring and water enters the hole when it passes, it suffices him like the navel; otherwise he must make it enter. And one does not burden himself by inserting something like a stick and the like. And he inserts the prepuce (qulfah) as a recommendation, as we will explain.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings (ahkam) concerning the details of performing ghusl, particularly regarding the manner of madmada, obstacles that prevent water from reaching the skin, and the movement of jewelry. The author addresses several specific cases:

1. Drinking water: If a person drinks water by gulping (عباً, swallowing in one go) without gargling, that suffices for the obligation of madmada (rinsing the mouth), because the water passes through the mouth. However, if he drinks by sucking (مصاً, sipping through a straw or the like), it does not suffice. There is a narration from Abu Yusuf that drinking does not suffice at all, but the relied-upon view is that it does if done in a way that washes the mouth. The text mentions that the more precautionary approach is to gargle (المج, i.e., swish the water in the mouth and expel it) to ensure that the obligation is fulfilled, even though the correct position is that gargling is not a condition. This is because by gargling, one is certain to have washed the mouth.

2. Teeth with cavities: If a tooth has a cavity or there is food or moist dirt (درن رطب) stuck in it, the ghusl is valid without removing it, because water is subtle and will most likely reach the area. However, Sadr al-Shahid Husam al-Din ruled that if food remains in cavities (كوات), it must be removed and water must run over the area for the ghusl to be valid. There is a difference of opinion, and the author notes that the precautionary view is to remove it and wash the cavity. The generally accepted (sahih) view in Mir'aj al-Dirayah is that it suffices without removal, but caution is advised.

3. Dry dirt in the nose: Dry dirt (درن يابس) like chewed bread or dough in the nose prevents the water from reaching the skin, thus invalidating the ghusl until it is removed. However, ordinary dirt (وسخ, درن) does not prevent, as water can penetrate through it. Similarly, earth and clay under the fingernail do not prevent because water seeps through. But the dye of a dyer on the nail does prevent; however, due to necessity (الصباغ cannot easily remove it), the fatwa is that it does not prevent. There is no difference between a villager and a city dweller in this ruling.

4. Remnants of insects: If flea excrement or fly droppings remain on the body and water cannot reach the skin beneath them, the purification is still valid because these are negligible impurities (عفو).

5. Jewelry: It is obligatory to move tight earrings (القرط) and rings (الخاتم الضيقين) during ghusl to ensure water reaches under them. If there is no earring, but water enters the hole of the piercing, it suffices; otherwise, one must make water enter it. One does not have to insert anything like a stick to check; this is stated in Sharh al-Wiqayah.

6. Prepuce (القلفة): It is recommended (استحباباً) to pull back the foreskin during ghusl to ensure water reaches the glans, as will be explained later.

All these rulings are based on the principle that ghusl requires water to reach the entire body, and any obstruction that is thick or dry prevents validity, while thin or moist obstructions that water can penetrate are excused.

Key Terms
المج (al-maj): Gargling: the act of swishing water in the mouth and then expelling it. In this context, it is discussed whether it is a condition (shart) for madmada.
عب (abb): Gulping: drinking water in one draught without sipping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following methods of drinking water suffices for madmada in ghusl?
A) Sipping slowly
B) Gulping (عباً)
C) Sucking through a straw
D) Drinking with a spoon

Key Takeaway
Drinking water by gulping suffices for madmada; gargling is precautionary, not required.

Barriers to Water in Ghusl: Teeth, Nose, Nails, and Body Remnants
Rulings on various barriers in ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which substances prevent water from reaching the body during ghusl and which do not, and apply the rulings to specific cases.

Core Idea
Dry dirt and thick barriers invalidate ghusl; thin and moist barriers do not; dyer's nail is excused due to necessity.

This passage examines various obstacles that may prevent water from contacting the skin during ghusl. The author distinguishes between substances that must be removed and those that are excused, with particular attention to teeth cavities, nose dirt, and nail dye.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Ghusl requires water to reach every part of the body. Any barrier that prevents water contact may invalidate the ghusl.
2
Categorize barriers by texture
Moist or thin items (e.g., moist food, ordinary dirt) generally do not prevent water penetration. Dry, thick items (e.g., dry dough) must be removed.
The passage uses examples like chewed bread (خبز ممضوغ) and dough (عجين) as preventers.
3
Apply to specific cases
Teeth cavities: moist food is fine, but dry food in cavities may need removal according to some views. Nose: dry dirt prevents. Nails: earth and clay are fine; dyer's dye is preventer but excused due to necessity. Body: fly droppings are negligible.
The text says 'والدرن اليابس في الأنف كالخبز الممضوغ والعجين يمنع'.
4
Note the ikhtilaf on teeth cavities
Some scholars (Husam al-Din) require removal of food from cavities; others (Fadli, Abu al-Layth) say it suffices without removal. The preferred view (al-sahih) is that it suffices.
The text says 'وفي معراج الدراية : الأصح أنه يجزيه'.
5
Apply the mufta bihi on dyer's nail
The dye normally prevents water, but due to necessity (ضرورة), the fatwa is that it does not prevent. This applies universally, not just in villages.
The text says 'وعليه الفتوى' and 'والصحيح أنه لا فرق بين القروي والمدني'.

Source Text

ولو شرب الماء عباً أجزأه عن المضمضة لا مصاً. وعن أبي يوسف لا إلا أن وفي الواقعات: لا يخرج بالشرب على وجه السنة أو غيره ما لم يمجه وهو أحوط كذا في الخلاصة. وقد يقال : إن الأحوط الخروج. ووجه كونه أحوط أنه قيل إن المج من شرط المضمضة والصحيح أنها ليست بشرط فكان الاحتياط الخروج عن الجنابة لأن الاحتياط العمل بأقوى الدليلين وأقواهما هنا الخروج بناء على الصحيح كما لا يخفى. ولو كان سنه مجوفاً أو طعام أو درن رطب يجزيه لأن الماء لطيف يصل إلى كل موضع غالباً كذا في التجنيس ثم قال : ذكر الصدر الشهيد حسام الدين في موضع آخر إذا كان في أسنانه كوات يبقى فيها الطعام لا يجزيه ما لم يخرجه ويجري الماء عليها. وفي فتاوى الفضلي والفقيه أبي الليث خلاف هذا فالاحتياط أن يفعل اهـ. وفي معراج الدراية : الأصح أنه يجزيه والدرن اليابس في الأنف كالخبز الممضوغ والعجين يمنع تمام الاغتسال وكذا جلد السمك والوسخ والدرن لا يمنع، والتراب والطين في الظفر لا يمنع لأن الماء ينفذ فيه، وما على ظفر الصباغ يمنع، وقيل لا يمنع للضرورة. قال في المضمرات وعليه الفتوى. والصحيح أنه لا فرق بين القروي والمدني ا هـ . ولو بقي على جسده خرء برغوث أو ونيم ذباب أي ذرقه لم يصل الماء تحته جازت طهارته. ويجب تحريك القرط الخاتم الضيقين ولو لم يكن قرط فدخل الماء الثقب عند مروره أجزأه كالسرة وإلا أدخله كذا في فتح القدير ولا يتكلف في إدخال شيء من خشب ونحوه كذا في شرح الوقاية، ويدخل القلفة استحباباً على ما نبينه
And if he drinks water by gulping (soaking) it suffices him instead of madmada, but not by sucking. And from Abu Yusuf: 'No, unless...' And in al-Waqi'at: 'He does not exit (from janabah) by drinking in the manner of the sunnah or otherwise unless he gargles (yumajjuhu), and that is more precautionary' as in al-Khulasa. And it may be said that the precautionary is to exit (i.e., gargle). And the reason it is more precautionary is that it has been said that gargling (al-maj) is a condition of madmada, and the correct view is that it is not a condition, so the precautionary is to exit from janabah, because precautionary means acting upon the stronger of the two proofs, and the stronger here is exit based on the correct view, as is not hidden. And if his tooth is hollow, or there is food or moist dirt (inside), it suffices him, because water is subtle and reaches every place usually, as in al-Tajnish. Then he said: Sharif Sadr al-Shahid Husam al-Din mentioned in another place: if there are cavities in his teeth in which food remains, it does not suffice him unless he removes it and runs water over them. And in the Fatawa of al-Fadli and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, contrary to this, so the precautionary is to do (i.e., to remove). End. And in Mir'aj al-Dirayah: the most correct is that it suffices him. And dry dirt in the nose, like chewed bread and dough, prevents complete ghusl. Likewise, fish skin, and filth and dirt do not prevent. And earth and clay on the nail do not prevent because water penetrates through it. And what is on the dyer's nail prevents, and it is said it does not prevent due to necessity. He said in al-Mudmarat: and upon it is the fatwa. And the correct view is that there is no difference between the villager and the city dweller. End. And if there remains on his body the excrement of a flea or the droppings of a fly, i.e., its excrement, and water does not reach under it, his purification is valid. And it is obligatory to move the earring and the tight ring. And if there is no earring and water enters the hole when it passes, it suffices him like the navel; otherwise he must make it enter. And one does not burden himself by inserting something like a stick and the like. And he inserts the prepuce (qulfah) as a recommendation, as we will explain.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings (ahkam) concerning the details of performing ghusl, particularly regarding the manner of madmada, obstacles that prevent water from reaching the skin, and the movement of jewelry. The author addresses several specific cases:

1. Drinking water: If a person drinks water by gulping (عباً, swallowing in one go) without gargling, that suffices for the obligation of madmada (rinsing the mouth), because the water passes through the mouth. However, if he drinks by sucking (مصاً, sipping through a straw or the like), it does not suffice. There is a narration from Abu Yusuf that drinking does not suffice at all, but the relied-upon view is that it does if done in a way that washes the mouth. The text mentions that the more precautionary approach is to gargle (المج, i.e., swish the water in the mouth and expel it) to ensure that the obligation is fulfilled, even though the correct position is that gargling is not a condition. This is because by gargling, one is certain to have washed the mouth.

2. Teeth with cavities: If a tooth has a cavity or there is food or moist dirt (درن رطب) stuck in it, the ghusl is valid without removing it, because water is subtle and will most likely reach the area. However, Sadr al-Shahid Husam al-Din ruled that if food remains in cavities (كوات), it must be removed and water must run over the area for the ghusl to be valid. There is a difference of opinion, and the author notes that the precautionary view is to remove it and wash the cavity. The generally accepted (sahih) view in Mir'aj al-Dirayah is that it suffices without removal, but caution is advised.

3. Dry dirt in the nose: Dry dirt (درن يابس) like chewed bread or dough in the nose prevents the water from reaching the skin, thus invalidating the ghusl until it is removed. However, ordinary dirt (وسخ, درن) does not prevent, as water can penetrate through it. Similarly, earth and clay under the fingernail do not prevent because water seeps through. But the dye of a dyer on the nail does prevent; however, due to necessity (الصباغ cannot easily remove it), the fatwa is that it does not prevent. There is no difference between a villager and a city dweller in this ruling.

4. Remnants of insects: If flea excrement or fly droppings remain on the body and water cannot reach the skin beneath them, the purification is still valid because these are negligible impurities (عفو).

5. Jewelry: It is obligatory to move tight earrings (القرط) and rings (الخاتم الضيقين) during ghusl to ensure water reaches under them. If there is no earring, but water enters the hole of the piercing, it suffices; otherwise, one must make water enter it. One does not have to insert anything like a stick to check; this is stated in Sharh al-Wiqayah.

6. Prepuce (القلفة): It is recommended (استحباباً) to pull back the foreskin during ghusl to ensure water reaches the glans, as will be explained later.

All these rulings are based on the principle that ghusl requires water to reach the entire body, and any obstruction that is thick or dry prevents validity, while thin or moist obstructions that water can penetrate are excused.

Key Terms
الدرن اليابس (al-daran al-yabis): Dry dirt; solid, dry substances that prevent water penetration.
كوات (kawat): Cavities (in teeth) that may trap food.
صباغ (sabbagh): Dyer; a person whose profession involves dyeing, resulting in permanent stains on nails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on flea excrement remaining on the body during ghusl?
A) Must be removed
B) Purification is valid without removal
C) It depends on the size
D) It is not excused

Key Takeaway
Dry barriers must be removed in ghusl; moist barriers and minor impurities are excused.

Movement of Jewelry and Retraction of Prepuce in Ghusl
Rulings on moving jewelry and retracting prepuce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligation to move tight earrings and rings during ghusl, explain the ruling on piercings, and identify the recommendation regarding the prepuce.

Core Idea
Tight jewelry must be moved to ensure water reaches the skin; prepuce retraction is recommended, not obligatory.

The final portion of the passage addresses physical obstructions caused by jewelry and the prepuce. The author rules that tight items must be moved, and piercings require water to enter the hole if possible. For the prepuce, it is recommended to retract it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is moving tight jewelry during ghusl. The ruling is wajib (obligatory).
The text says 'ويجب تحريك القرط الخاتم الضيقين'.
2
Apply to earrings and rings
Both earrings (قرط) and rings (خاتم) that are tight must be moved so water reaches the skin beneath.
If the jewelry is loose, water may reach without moving, but the obligation is to ensure contact.
3
Apply to piercings
If there is an earring or ring that is not present (i.e., the hole is empty), water entering the hole suffices; otherwise, one must make water enter it.
No need to insert a stick or similar object.
4
Note the ruling on prepuce
The prepuce (القلفة) should be retracted as a recommendation (استحباباً), not an obligation.
The author says 'يدخل القلفة استحباباً'.
5
Understand the method
One does not have to burden oneself by inserting something like a stick to check water entry; simple movement suffices.
This is from Sharh al-Wiqayah.

Washing External Genitals: Ghusl vs Wudu
States ruling on washing external genitals in ghusl and wudu with analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for washing the external genitals in ghusl and wudu, and explain the qiyas (analogy) to the mouth.

Core Idea
Washing external genitals: wajib in ghusl, sunnah in wudu — the mouth analogy shows its rationale.

This brief passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a common point of confusion: the difference between ghusl and wudu regarding washing private parts. Understanding this ruling hinges on recognizing the legal analogy (qiyas) drawn between the private parts and the mouth, which also has different statuses in the two acts of purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its contexts
The act is washing the external genitals (for women). The two contexts are ghusl (ritual bath) and wudu (ablution).
The text specifies 'الفرج الخارج' (external private parts).
2
State the ruling for each context
In ghusl it is obligatory (wajib); in wudu it is sunnah (recommended). The passage says: 'وجوباً في الغسل وسنة في الوضوء'.
Obligatory here means part of the fard requirements of ghusl; in wudu it is not required but recommended.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy)
The text says 'لأنه كالفم' (because it is like the mouth). The mouth is washed in ghusl (obligatory as part of the body) and in wudu rinsing the mouth (madmadah) is sunnah. The same applies to the external genitals.
The illa (effective cause) is that both are external openings that require cleansing.
4
Note the explicit prohibition
The passage forbids inserting fingers into the vagina during washing, saying 'ولا تدخل أصابعها في قبلها' (and she does not insert her fingers into her vagina). This is to avoid harm or excess.
The text ends with 'وبه يفتى' meaning this is the relied-upon fatwa.
5
Recognize the mufta bihi
The prohibition of insertion is the operative fatwa (mufta bihi). The passage indicates that the ruling on washing and the prohibition are established.
This is a clear ruling with no contrary view mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتغسل فرجها الخارج وجوباً في الغسل وسنة في الوضوء - كذا في المحيط - لأنه كالفم . ولا تدخل أصابعها في قبلها وبه يفتى
And she washes her external private parts; it is obligatory in ghusl and sunnah in wudu—as in al-Muhit—because it is like the mouth. And she does not insert her fingers into her vagina, and according to this, fatwa is given.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage states rulings on washing the private parts in ghusl and wudu. It clarifies that for a woman, washing the external genitalia (the visible part) is obligatory during ghusl (ritual bath) because it is part of the body that must be purified. However, in wudu, it is only sunnah (recommended) because wudu does not require washing the private parts; the analogy compares it to the mouth, which is washed in wudu but not obligatory. The passage also prohibits inserting fingers into the vagina during washing, as that could be considered excessive or harmful; the relied-upon fatwa follows this prohibition.

Key Terms
فرج (farj): The external private parts (genitals)
غسل (ghusl): Ritual full-body wash
وضوء (wudu): Ablution for prayer
الفم (al-fam): The mouth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for washing the external genitals in wudu?
A) Obligatory (fard)
B) Sunnah
C) Forbidden
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, washing external genitals is wajib in ghusl (as part of the body) and sunnah in wudu (by analogy to the mouth), with a prohibition on inserting fingers.

Validity of Wudu and Ghusl with a Healed Scab
Ruling on healed wound scab in wudu and ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on wudu and ghusl when a healed scab prevents water from reaching the skin, and identify the condition that makes it valid.

Core Idea
A healed scab that prevents water does not invalidate wudu or ghusl—the obligation is met despite the barrier.

You might think that if water cannot reach the skin, the purification is incomplete. But Islamic law considers ease: removing a healed scab can cause harm. This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling that wudu and ghusl are valid even when a scab blocks water, as long as it is naturally attached. We will examine the source text from al-Nawazil and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that if a person had a wound that healed and a scab formed, and the edges are attached to the skin except the tip, then his wudu suffices. The same applies to ghusl. So the act is wudu (and ghusl) and the ruling is that it is valid (ajza'ahu).
The ruling does not require water to reach under the scab.
2
Understand the condition for validity
The text specifies that the edges of the scab are connected to the skin. This attachment is the key condition: water cannot reach under it, but it is not considered a removable barrier because removing it would cause harm. The scab is like a natural covering.
If the scab were loose and easily removable, the ruling might differ.
3
Note the analogy to ghusl
The author explicitly says 'wa fi ma'nāh al-ghusl' meaning the same ruling applies to ghusl by legal analogy. So both major and minor purification are valid despite the scab.
This extends the ruling from wudu to full-body washing.
4
Recognize the source authority
The ruling is cited from al-Nawazil of Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi, and also transmitted by al-Hindi. This shows the ruling is well-established in the Hanafi tradition, not a marginal opinion.
Al-Nawazil is a collection of juristic cases (nawāzil) providing practical rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان في الإنسان قرحة فبرأت وارتفع قشرها وأطراف القرحة متصلة بالجلد إلا الطرف الذي كان يخرج منه القيح فإنه يرتفع ولا يصل الماء إلى ما تحت القشرة أجزأه وضوءه وفي معناه الغسل كذا في النوازل لأبي الليث ونقله الهندي أيضاً.
And if a person had a wound and it healed and its scab rose and the edges of the wound are connected to the skin, except the tip from which pus used to come out, it rises and water does not reach what is under the scab, his wudu suffices, and in its meaning is ghusl—this is in al-Nawazil of Abu al-Layth, and al-Hindi also narrated it.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage addresses a common scenario: a wound that has healed with a scab (qishra). The scab is attached at the edges but the middle part where pus was might be raised. Water cannot reach the skin under the scab, yet the ruling is that wudu (and likewise ghusl) is valid—the water does not need to reach under the scab. This is based on necessity because removing the scab could cause harm. The ruling is taken from al-Nawazil by Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi and also transmitted by al-Hindi. The same applies to ghusl by analogy (fi ma'nāh).

Key Terms
قرحة (qarha): A wound or ulcer

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a scab prevents water from reaching the skin underneath, what is the ruling on wudu according to the Hanafi school?
A) Wudu is invalid
B) Wudu is valid but must be repeated
C) Wudu is valid
D) Wudu requires removing the scab

Key Takeaway
A healed scab that prevents water under it does not invalidate wudu or ghusl because it is a natural barrier excused by necessity.

Ruling for Junub: Eating, Drinking, and Mentioning Allah
Ruling on junub's permissibility with rinsing debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a junub eating, drinking, and mentioning Allah, and explain how the permissibility depends on the purity of used water.

Core Idea
A junub may eat, drink, and mention Allah after rinsing; permissibility hinges on whether used water is pure.

The state of janaba imposes some restrictions, but not all actions are prohibited. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that a junub can mention Allah, eat, and drink, but with a condition: rinsing the mouth. However, a deeper investigation reveals that this condition is tied to a disputed ruling on used water. Understanding this nuance is key to correctly applying the Hanafi fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage discusses three acts for a person in janaba: mentioning Allah, eating, and drinking. Determine the default ruling.
The text says 'يجوز' (it is permissible) after rinsing.
2
Analyze the condition of rinsing
Note that the permissibility is explicitly tied to rinsing the mouth. Understand why this condition exists according to the text.
The texts from Fath al-Qadir and al-Bazzaziyya show a link to the ruling on used water.
3
Trace the dispute on used water
The passage explains that the condition of rinsing is based on a narration that used water (ma' musta'mal) is impure. Identify the two positions.
One narration: used water is najis; another (mufta bihi): used water is tahir.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
According to the relied-upon view, used water is pure. Therefore, the condition of rinsing is not required for permissibility.
The text says: 'So according to the chosen, corrected, and fatwa-given narration of the purity of used water, drinking should be absolutely permissible.'
5
Apply to a practical scenario
Consider a person in janaba who wishes to have breakfast. Should they rinse first? According to the mufta bihi, no; they may eat and drink immediately.
Also, the passage mentions that it is recommended (mustahabb) to wash hands and mouth, but not obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز للجنب أن يذكر اسم الله تعالى ويأكل ويشرب إذا تمضمض هكذا قيد في فتح القدير، وظاهره أنه لا يجوز له قبل المضمضة لكن ذكر في البزازية ما يفيد أن هذا على رواية نجاسة الماء المستعمل ولفظها ويحل للجنب شرب الماء قبل المضمضة على وجه السنة وأن لا على وجهها لا لأنه شارب الماء المستعمل وأنه نجس ا هـ . فينبغي على الرواية المختارة المصححة المفتى بها من طهارة الماء المستعمل أن يباح الشرب مطلقاً، ويستفاد منه أن انفصال الماء عن العضو أعم من أن يكون إلى الباطن أو إلى الظاهر والمنقول في فتاوى قاضيخان الجنب إذا أراد أن يأكل أو يشرب فالمستحب له أن يغسل يديه وفاه وإن ترك لا بأس. لا يستحب ههنا لأن واختلفوا في الحائض قال بعضهم هي والجنب سواء، وقال بعضهم بالغسل لا تزول نجاسة الحيض عن الفم واليد بخلاف الجنابة ا.هـ. فاحفظه.
And it is permissible for the junub to mention the name of Allah Almighty and to eat and drink if he rinses his mouth; thus it is restricted in Fath al-Qadir. And its apparent meaning is that it is not permissible for him before rinsing, but there is mentioned in al-Bazzaziyya what indicates that this is according to the narration of impurity of used water, and its wording: 'And it is permissible for the junub to drink water before rinsing in the manner of sunnah, but not in a non-sunnah manner, because he would be drinking used water and it is impure—end quote.' So according to the chosen, corrected, and fatwa-given narration of the purity of used water, drinking should be absolutely permissible. And it is understood from this that the separation of water from the limb is more general, whether it goes inward or outward. And transmitted in Fatawa Qadikhan: when a junub wants to eat or drink, it is recommended for him to wash his hands and mouth, and if he omits it, there is no harm. It is not recommended here because... And they differed regarding a menstruating woman: some said she and the junub are equal; some said that by ghusl, the impurity of menstruation does not leave the mouth and hand, unlike janaba—end quote. So memorize it.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage clarifies the rulings for a person in a state of major ritual impurity (junub). The main ruling: it is permissible for the junub to mention Allah, eat, and drink after rinsing the mouth. If he does not rinse, it is not permissible according to the restriction in Fath al-Qadir. However, a text in al-Bazzaziyya shows that this restriction is based on the narration that considers used water (al-ma' al-musta'mal) as impure. According to the stronger narration that used water is pure, drinking is absolutely permissible even without rinsing. The passage also notes that the separation of water from the limb (in istinja or ghusl) is general, whether it goes inward or outward. From Fatawa Qadikhan, it is recommended (mustahabb) for the junub to wash hands and mouth before eating, but it is not obligatory. There is also a difference of opinion regarding a menstruating woman: some equate her with a junub, while others say that ghusl does not remove menstrual impurity from the mouth and hand, unlike janaba. The teacher commands to memorize this difference.

Key Terms
ماء مستعمل (ma' musta'mal): Water that has been used for worship (wudu/ghusl) and has separated from the limb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon narration (mufta bihi), what is the ruling for a junub drinking water without rinsing?
A) Forbidden
B) Permissible absolutely
C) Permissible only if he intends sunnah
D) Recommended but must wash hands

Key Takeaway
A junub may eat, drink, and mention Allah absolutely according to the mufta bihi, as used water is pure.

Junub Repeating Intercourse Before Ghusl
Ruling on repeating intercourse for a junub, with an exception for wet dream and a commentary on hadith interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a junub repeating intercourse before ghusl and distinguish between janaba from intercourse and from wet dream.

Core Idea
A junub from intercourse may repeat without ghusl; a junub from wet dream must perform ghusl first.

The text clarifies a common scenario: a person in major impurity (janaba) due to intercourse may engage in intercourse again before bathing, but this flexibility does not apply if the janaba came from a wet dream without intercourse. The commentary also addresses a subtle point: the ahadith recommend ghusl before repeating, but do not make it obligatory, preserving the permissibility of the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of janaba
Distinguish between janaba from intercourse and from wet dream (ihtilam). The ruling differs based on the cause.
The key is the source of the impurity: intentional act vs. involuntary state.
2
State the ruling for each category
For janaba from intercourse: it is permissible to repeat intercourse before ghusl. For janaba from wet dream: one must not approach his wife until he performs ghusl.
This is the explicit ruling from the source.
3
Understand the commentary's refinement
The ahadith on this matter indicate that it is recommended (mustahabb) to perform ghusl before repeating, but they do not negate permissibility. Thus the permissibility remains, though doing ghusl is better.
This resolves an apparent objection from Sharh Munyat al-Musalli.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi nuance
While the ruling is agreed, there is a difference in expression: some sources state it as merely permissible, while others emphasize the recommendation. The mufta bihi is that it is permissible but recommended to do ghusl.
This shows how later scholars reconcile texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللجنب أن يعاود أهله قبل أن يغتسل إلا إذا احتلم فإنه لا يأتي أهله ما لم يغتسل كذا في المبتغى، وأقره عليه في فتح القدير، وتعقبه في شرح منية المصلي بأن ظاهر الأحاديث فيه يفيد الاستحباب لا نفي الجواز المفاد من ظاهر كلامه.
And for the junub it is permissible to repeat intercourse with his wife before he performs ghusl, except if he had a wet dream, for he should not approach his wife until he performs ghusl—this is in al-Mubtagha, and he approved it in Fath al-Qadir, but it was objected to in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli that the apparent meaning of the ahadith on this indicates recommendation, not the negation of permissibility derived from the apparent meaning of his words.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of a person in a state of janaba (major impurity) from intercourse to repeat intercourse before performing ghusl. The ruling: it is permissible, except if the janaba resulted from a wet dream (ihtilam) without actual intercourse—in that case, he should not approach his wife until he performs ghusl. This is stated in al-Mubtagha and affirmed in Fath al-Qadir. However, there is a critical comment from Sharh Munyat al-Musalli: the ahadith on this topic indicate that it is recommended (mustahabb) to perform ghusl before repeating, but they do not negate its permissibility. In other words, it is still permissible to repeat without ghusl, but it is better to do ghusl first. The objection is that the original source seemed to say it is disliked or not permissible, but the hadith only recommend ghusl.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person becomes junub due to intercourse, can he have intercourse again before performing ghusl?
A) Yes, it is permissible
B) No, it is forbidden
C) Only if he makes wudu first
D) Only after a wet dream

Key Takeaway
A junub from intercourse may repeat intercourse before ghusl; from a wet dream, ghusl is required first.

Transferring Moisture in Ghusl vs Wudu
States the ruling on transferring moisture between limbs in ghusl vs wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on transferring moisture (bulla) between limbs during ghusl and wudu, and identify the condition ('dripping') for its permissibility in ghusl.

Core Idea
In ghusl, transferring dripping moisture is allowed; in wudu, it is not.

This lesson clarifies a subtle but important difference between ghusl and wudu regarding the transfer of residual water from one limb to another. The Hanafi school permits this in ghusl under a specific condition—that the moisture is dripping—while strictly prohibiting it in wudu. Understanding this distinction is crucial for ensuring your purification is valid and complete.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the key term
Recognize that the passage discusses 'bulla'—the moisture left on a limb after washing. The act under consideration is transferring this moisture from one body part to another during ghusl.
2
Determine the ruling for ghusl
The text states 'yajuz' (it is permissible) to transfer bulla in ghusl, but only if the moisture is mutaqatiran (dripping). This condition is essential: the water must be in a state where it would drip if transferred.
If the moisture is merely damp but not enough to drip, the transfer may not be considered effective.
3
Contrast with wudu
The passage explicitly says 'bikhilaf al-wudu' (contrary to wudu). In wudu, transferring moisture between limbs is not permissible. Each limb must be washed with fresh water, not by transferring residual moisture.
This difference arises because ghusl aims at covering the entire body with water, while wudu requires fresh water for each specific limb.
4
Analyze the additional ruling about splashing
The text notes that water splashing from the body into the vessel (e.g., a bucket or basin) does not harm the purity of the water in that vessel. However, if all the water from a limb drips completely into the vessel, that water becomes 'used water' (ma' musta'mal) and is no longer pure for washing.
This nuance will be fully explained later in the discussion on used water.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز نقل البلة في الغسل من عضو إلى عضو إذا كان متقاطراً بخلاف الوضوء، ولا يضر ما انتضح من غسله في إنائه بخلاف ما لو قطر كله في الإناء وسيأتي تمامه في بحث الماء المستعمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
And it is permissible to transfer the moisture (bulla) in ghusl from one limb to another if it is dripping, contrary to wudu. And whatever splashes from his washing into his vessel does not harm, unlike if it all drips into the vessel; and its completion will come in the discussion of used water, God willing.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on transferring moisture (bulla) during ghusl and wudu. 'Bulla' refers to the water that remains on a limb after washing. In ghusl, it is permissible to transfer this moisture from one limb to another, provided the water is dripping (mutāqāṭiran) i.e., there is enough moisture to drip. This is because ghusl requires water to cover the entire body, and transferring moisture helps achieve that. In contrast, in wudu, transferring moisture is not allowed because wudu requires fresh water for each limb. Additionally, water that splashes from the person's washing into the vessel (e.g., during istinja or ghusl) does not harm the purity of the water in the vessel, as long as it is not all the water that drips completely into it. The full details will be covered later in the section on used water (al-ma' al-musta'mal).

Key Terms
بلة (bulla): Moisture or remaining water on a limb after washing

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is it permissible to transfer moisture (bulla) from one limb to another during ghusl?
A) Always, regardless of moisture amount
B) Only if the moisture is dripping (mutaqatiran)
C) Only if the water is not wasted
D) It is never allowed in ghusl

Key Takeaway
In ghusl, transferring dripping moisture between limbs is allowed; in wudu, it is not permitted, and each limb must be washed with fresh water.

Conditions and Ruling of Ghusl: Same as Wudu
Briefly lists conditions, ruling, and upcoming details of ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of ghusl according to the Hanafi school and explain the ruling of ghusl.

Core Idea
Ghusl's conditions mirror wudu; its ruling is to permit worship.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a concise summary: the conditions (shurūṭ) of ghusl are identical to those of wudu, and the ruling (ḥukm) of ghusl is that it makes permissible acts that are otherwise unlawful due to janābah. The text signals that details of sunan, adab, description, and cause will be elaborated later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parts of the passage
The passage is structured into three parts: conditions, ruling, and a note on future content. Locate each part and note the key terms: 'shurūṭ', 'ḥukm', 'sunan', 'ādāb', 'ṣifa', 'sabab'.
The phrase 'istibāḥa mā lā yaḥillu illā bihī' is the core definition of the ruling.
2
Connect conditions to wudu
The passage states the conditions of ghusl are 'what preceded from the conditions of wudu'. Recall the conditions of wudu in Hanafi fiqh: being Muslim, sane, using pure water, absence of physical or legal impediments, etc.
The implication is that the same prerequisites apply to ghusl.
3
Understand the ruling
The ruling of ghusl is 'istibāḥa mā lā yaḥillu illā bihī' — it permits what was prohibited due to janābah. Examples: performing Ṣalāh, touching the Muṣḥaf, entering a mosque, reciting Qur’an.
This ruling is the primary purpose of ghusl; it is not a summary of all its legal effects.
4
Anticipate the detailed discussion
The author promises to detail the sunan (recommended acts), adab (etiquettes), ṣifa (description), and sabab (cause) later. This sets up the structure for forthcoming lessons.
This is a common didactic technique: first give the core, then expand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما شرائطه فما تقدم من شرائط الوضوء، وأما حكمه فاستباحة ما لا يحل إلا به، وأما سننه وآدابه وصفته وسببه فستأتي مفصلة إن شاء الله تعالى.
As for its conditions, they are what preceded from the conditions of wudu. As for its ruling, it is the permission of what is not lawful except by it. As for its sunan, etiquettes, description, and cause, they will come in detail, God willing.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage serves as a transition and summary of ghusl. The author states that the conditions (shurūṭ) of ghusl are the same as those of wudu, such as being Muslim, sane, using pure water, etc. The ruling (ḥukm) of ghusl is that it makes lawful what was prohibited due to janaba, e.g., prayer, touching the Quran, etc. The sunan (recommended acts), adab (etiquettes), description (ṣifa), and cause (sabab) will be discussed in detail later, God willing. This brief note indicates that the text is organized and the full exposition is to come.

Key Terms
شرائط الغسل (shurūṭ al-ghusl): The conditions required for the validity of ghusl
استباحة (istibāḥa): Making lawful; permission
سنن الغسل (sunan al-ghusl): The recommended acts of ghusl
آداب الغسل (ādāb al-ghusl): The etiquettes of performing ghusl

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is recommended (sunnah) for Friday prayer.
B) It permits what is not lawful except by it.
C) It is obligatory (farḍ) for all Muslims daily.
D) It purifies the body from minor impurities.

Key Takeaway
The conditions of ghusl are identical to those of wudu, and its ruling is to make worship permissible.

The Ten Sunan al-Fitrah and Their Fiqh
Narrates hadith of ten fitrah practices, defines each, and discusses if they are sunnah or wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ten sunan al-fitrah, define each term, and explain the Hanafi position that they are sunnah muakkadah.

Core Idea
The ten fitrah practices are emphasized sunnahs; their status varies across madhhabs, but the Hanafi school holds them as strongly recommended, not obligatory.

In this lesson, we explore the famous hadith of the ten fitrah practices as narrated by Muslim. You'll learn the precise meaning of each practice, the scholarly debate over the interpretation of 'fitrah', and the Hanafi ruling that these acts are sunnah muakkadah (highly recommended but not mandatory). This passage also illustrates how a single hadith can generate nuanced fiqh discussions across madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
List the ten practices
From the hadith, identify each of the ten acts mentioned: trimming mustache, letting beard grow, using miswak, snuffing water, clipping nails, washing knuckles, plucking armpit hair, shaving pubic hair, istinja' with water, and rinsing mouth (the tenth according to one narrator).
The tenth is disputed; some say it is rinsing the mouth.
2
Define each key term
Understand the specific meaning of each term: 'intiqāṣ al-mā'' (istinja' with water), 'istihdād' (shaving pubic hair), 'i'fā' al-lihya' (letting beard grow), 'barājim' (knuckles), and the extension to ear folds.
Some scholars analogize dirt in ear folds to knuckle dirt.
3
Analyze the debate on 'fitrah'
The passage discusses whether 'fitrah' means 'dīn' (religion) or 'sunnah' (practice). The author argues for 'dīn' because if it were 'sunnah', all items would have to be sunnah, but some scholars consider certain practices wajib.
The Quranic example of eating and giving due shows an obligatory and non-obligatory act can be listed together.
4
Identify the Hanafi ruling
According to the Hanafi school, all ten practices are sunnah muakkadah. They are recommended but not fard or wajib. The author cites the hadith on istinshāq and notes that exaggeration is sunnah due to consensus on non-obligation.
The weak hadith about janaba under every hair is rejected.
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
In the Shafi'i school, some practices like khitan (circumcision) are considered wajib. This shows that the same hadith can yield different rulings based on usul principles.
The Hanafi position relies on the interpretation of 'fitrah' as dīn to allow variability.

ولا بأس بإيراد حديث مسلم بتمامه والتكلم على بعض معانيه . روى مسلم بإسناده عن عائشة قالت قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عشر من الفطرة : قص الشارب واعفاء اللحية والسواك واستنشاق الماء وقص الأظفار وغسل البراجم ونتف الإبط وحلق العانة وانتقاص الماء قال مصعب أحد رواته : ونسيت العاشرة إلا أن تكون المضمضة وانتقاص الماء بالقاف والصاد المهملة الاستنجاء ، وقيل انتقاص البول استعمال الماء في غسل مذاكيرة. وقال الجمهور : الانتضاح وهو نضح الفرج بماء قليل سمي استحداداً في لينفي عنه الوسواس فإذا أراه الشيطان ذلك أحاله على الماء، وقد صرح بذلك مشايخنا في كتبهم لكن قالوا: إن هذه الحيلة إنما تنفعه إذا كان العهد قريباً بحيث لم يجف البلل أما إذا كان بعيداً وجف البلل ثم رأى بللاً يعيد الوضوء. والاستحداد حلق العانة . لاستعمال الحديدة وهي الموسى وهو سنة. والمراد بالعانة الشعر فوق ذكر الرجل وحواليه إلى السرة. وإعفاء اللحية توفيرها، والبَراجَم بفتح الباء والجيم جمع بُرْجُمة بضم الباء والجيم وهي عقد الأصابع ومفاصلها كلها قال بعض العلماء ويلتحق بالبراجم ما يجتمع من الوسخ في معاطف الأذن وقعر الصماخ فيزيله بالمسح وكذلك جميع الأوساخ. وأما الفطرة فقد تقدم من المحقق الكمال أنها الدين وهو قول البعض، وذهب أكثر العلماء إلى أنها السنة وهي الأصل الخلقة. وفي بعض هذه الخصال ما هو واجب عند بعض العلماء ولا يمتنع قرن الواجب بغيره كما قال الله تعالى كلوا من ثمرة إذا أثمر وآتوا حقه يوم حصاده ﴾ [الأنعام : ١٤١] فإن الإيتاء واجب والأكل ليس بواجب كذا ذكر النووي. ولا يخفى ما فيه فإن العطف في الآية ليس نظير ما في الحديث فإن الفطرة إذا فسرت بالسنة يقتضي أن جميع المعدود من السنة فإنه إذا قيل جاء عشر من الرجال لا يجوز أن يكون فيهم من ليس منهم، فالأولى في الفطرة تفسيرها بالدين وقد تقدم معنى المضمضة والاستنشاق وأن المبالغة فيهما سنة في الوضوء وكذلك في الغسل لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «بالغ في الاستنشاق إلا أن تكون صائماً» وهو حديث صحيح ذكره النووي والصارف له عن الوجوب الاتفاق على عدمه كما نقله السراج الهندي. واعلم أن الحديث الذي ذكره في فتح القدير وهو تحت كل شعرة جنابة الخ» وإن رواه أبو داود الترمذي كما ذكره الهندي فقد ضعفه النووي ونقل ضعفه عن الشافعي ويحيى بن معين والبخاري وأبي داود وغيرهم والله أعلم.
There is no harm in presenting the hadith of Muslim in its entirety and discussing some of its meanings. Muslim narrated with his chain from 'A'isha who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Ten are from the fitrah: trimming the mustache, letting the beard grow, using the miswak, snuffing water, clipping the nails, washing the knuckles, plucking the armpit hairs, shaving the pubic hair, and istinja' with water.' Mus'ab, one of its narrators, said: 'I forgot the tenth unless it is rinsing the mouth.' And 'intiqāṣ al-mā'' (with qaf and sad) means istinja'. It is said: 'intiqāṣ al-bawl' means using water to wash the private parts. The majority said: 'al-intidāh' which is sprinkling the private parts with a little water; it is called istihdād to remove waswās: if the devil shows him that, he attributes it to water. Our mashaykh have stated this in their books, but they said: This trick only benefits him if the time is recent such that the moisture has not dried; if it is far and the moisture has dried and then he sees moisture, he should repeat wudu. And 'al-istihdād' is shaving the pubic hair, using the iron (i.e., razor), and it is sunnah. What is meant by al-'āna is the hair above the man's penis and around it up to the navel. 'I'fā' al-lihya' means lengthening it. 'al-barājim' (with fatha on ba and jim) is the plural of 'burjuma' (with damma on ba and jim), and they are the knuckles of the fingers and all their joints. Some scholars said: that which collects of dirt in the folds of the ear and the bottom of the ear canal is annexed to the barājim, so he removes it by wiping, and likewise all dirt. As for al-fitrah, it has preceded from al-Muhaqqiq al-Kamal that it is religion (dīn), and that is the saying of some; most scholars went that it is sunnah (the original disposition). And among these practices, there are some that are obligatory according to some scholars. There is no prevention of joining the obligatory with other than it, as Allah said: 'Eat of its fruit when it fruits and give its due on the day of harvest' (6:141); giving the due is obligatory while eating is not obligatory—thus mentioned al-Nawawi. And it is not hidden that this comparison is not parallel to the hadith, because if al-fitrah is interpreted as sunnah, it would necessitate that all the enumerated are sunnah; if one says 'ten men came', it is not permissible that among them is one who is not from them. So it is more appropriate to interpret al-fitrah as religion. The meaning of madmada and istinshāq has preceded, and that exaggerating in them is sunnah in wudu and similarly in ghusl, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Exaggerate in istinshāq unless you are fasting.' It is a sahih hadith mentioned by al-Nawawi, and what turns it away from being obligatory is the consensus on its non-obligation as transmitted by al-Siraj al-Hindi. And know that the hadith mentioned in Fath al-Qadir, 'Under every hair is janaba...' even though Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi narrated it as mentioned by al-Hindi, al-Nawawi declared it weak and transmitted its weakness from al-Shafi'i, Yahya ibn Ma'in, al-Bukhari, Abu Dawud, and others. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the famous hadith of the ten fitrah practices, narrated by Muslim. The author lists the practices: trimming the mustache, letting the beard grow, using miswak, snuffing water (istinshāq), clipping nails, washing the knuckles, plucking armpit hair, shaving pubic hair, and istinja' with water. The tenth is rinsing the mouth (madmada) according to one narrator. The author then defines each term: 'intiqāṣ al-mā'' is istinja' (cleaning after relieving oneself); 'istihdād' is shaving pubic hair with a razor; 'i'fā' al-lihya' means letting the beard grow long; 'barājim' are the knuckles. A special note: some scholars extend 'barājim' to include dirt in ear folds. The term 'fitrah' itself is debated: some say it means 'religion' (dīn), others say it means 'sunnah' (the original disposition or practice). The author leans toward 'dīn' because if fitrah is sunnah, then all enumerated must be sunnah, but some scholars consider some of these practices wajib (obligatory). The author discusses whether these practices are sunnah or wajib: most are sunnah, but some are wajib in the view of some scholars. The example of the Quranic verse shows that an obligatory act can be mentioned alongside a non-obligatory one. However, the author objects that the hadith lists items equally, so they should all have the same status. Therefore, interpreting fitrah as 'religion' allows for diversity in rulings. The author also clarifies that madmada and istinshāq are sunnah in wudu and ghusl, and the hadith commanding exaggeration in istinshāq is sahih but not obligatory due to consensus. Finally, the author warns about a weak hadith claiming janaba is under every hair, declared weak by major scholars.

Key Terms
فطرة (fitrah): Original human disposition; here interpreted as 'dīn' (religion) or 'sunnah' (practice)
انتقاص الماء (intiqāṣ al-mā'): Istinja' with water; also interpreted as sprinkling water on private parts after urination to prevent waswās
استحداد (istihdād): Shaving the pubic hair with a razor
براجم (barājim): Knuckles of the fingers

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the preferred interpretation of 'fitrah' in the hadith?
A) Sunnah (practice)
B) Din (religion)
C) Khalq (creation)
D) Iman (faith)

Key Takeaway
The ten fitrah practices are sunnah muakkadah in the Hanafi school, based on the interpretation of 'fitrah' as 'dīn'.

Hila for Waswas and Sunan al-Fitrah
Explaining a trick for waswas and rulings on fitrah-related acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on istihdad, i'fa' al-lihyah, and exaggeration in maddmadah/istinshaq, and apply the conditions of the hila for waswas.

Core Idea
Sunan al-fitrah are sunnah, not obligatory; waswas is overcome by assuming wetness is water if recent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses practical purification matters: a helpful trick for those troubled by whispers (waswas) about wetness, and the detailed rulings on the sunan al-fitrah. You will learn that acts like shaving pubic hair and letting the beard grow are sunnah, while the hila for waswas provides a mental shift to avoid unnecessary doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hila for Waswas
Recognize the trick: when Satan shows wetness as urine, the person should assume it is water, provided the wetness is recent (not dried). If dried, wudu must be repeated.
This is a psychological remedy rooted in fiqh.
2
List the Sunan al-Fitrah and Their Rulings
Identify istihdad (sunnah), i'fa' al-lihyah (sunnah), cleaning barajim (sunnah), and exaggeration in maddmadah/istinshaq (sunnah except for fasting).
Fitrah is debated; the author prefers interpretation as din to maintain consistency.
3
Analyze the Dalil for Exaggeration in Istinshaq
The hadith 'Exaggerate in istinshaq unless fasting' indicates sunnah due to the exception and consensus on non-obligation.
The hadith is sound but zanni in thubut and dalala.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on various purification matters. The author first discusses a trick (hila) for someone suffering from waswas (whisperings of Satan) regarding wetness: if he sees wetness on his clothes and doubts whether it is water or urine, he should assume it is water if the time is close and the wetness has not dried; if it has dried, he must repeat wudu. This is a practical solution for waswas. The passage then moves to the sunan al-fitrah: istihdad (shaving the pubic hair) is sunnah, and its meaning is clarified. Letting the beard grow (i'fa' al-lihyah) is also sunnah. Cleaning the knuckles (barajim) is recommended, and some scholars include ear dirt. The term fitrah is discussed: some say it means religion (din), others say it means sunnah (the prophetic practice), but the author favors the interpretation as religion to avoid implying that all listed items are sunnah. The passage also notes that maddmadah (rinsing the mouth) and istinshaq (sniffing water) are sunnah in wudu and ghusl, and exaggeration in them is sunnah except for a fasting person. This is supported by a sound hadith. The author also mentions a weak hadith about janabah under every hair, noting its weakness. All these rulings are based on the Hanafi school, with preference for the view of the majority.

Key Terms
الاستحداد (al-istihdad): Shaving the pubic hair using a blade.
الفطرة (al-fitrah): The original nature or the prophetic practice (sunnah) in the context of these characteristics.
المبالغة في الاستنشاق (al-mubalaghah fi al-istinshaq): Exaggeration in sniffing water up the nose during wudu or ghusl.
